Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
5 min
Self-Evident vs Evident Implication
Author distinguishes between self-evidence and evident implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a proposition being self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi) and an implication (luzum) being evident, and explain how a non-self-evident consequent can be known through an evident implication.

Core Idea
A thing may be self-evident while its consequent is not; the implication makes it known.

In demonstration, we often assume that if premises are evident, the conclusion must also be evident. The author warns against conflating two types of evidentness: a thing being self-evident versus the implication from that thing being evident. This distinction is crucial for understanding how certain knowledge is transmitted in a syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of evidentness
The author contrasts 'bayyin bi-nafsihi' (self-evident) and 'bayyin al-luzum' (evident in implication).
2
Understand the relationship
A self-evident thing can have a necessary consequent that is not self-evident, but the implication (the relation of necessity) is evident.
3
Apply the distinction
If the implication is evident, then the consequent is known through it, without the consequent itself being self-evident.

Source Text

وبالجملة فهذا البيان من جملة ما فيه نظر فإن المقدم لا يلزم أن يكون مشكوكا فيه أو غير مشكوك في جميع الصور. ولا يلزم أن يكون الشيء بينا بنفسه إذا كان لازما لما هو بين بنفسه؛ إذ الشيء ربما كان بينا بنفسه وله لازم ليس بينا بنفسه لكن لزومه لذلك الشيء البين بينا فيعلم بتوسطه ذلك اللازم. وفرق بين قولنا إنه بين بنفسه وبين قولنا إنه بين لزومه عن بين بنفسه؛ فاللازم لا بد وأن يكون بين اللازم أو ينتهي آخر الأمر إلى لازم بين اللزوم وإلا لكانت الأشياء كلها بينة بأنفسها. فيلزم أن لا يكون الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول منتجا؛ وذلك لأن قياساته تنحل إلى مقدمتين بينتين بأنفسهما ثم النتيجة بين اللزوم منهما. فنسبة القياس إلى النتيجة نسبة التالي إلى المقدم؛ فيكون المقدم أمرا بينا كما في قولنا إن كل ج ب وكل «ب أ»؛ أو التالي بين اللزوم عنه فيجب قولنا كل «ج أ» بينا. وكذلك جميع النتائج الثواني إلى غير النهاية. وأيضا ليس يجب أن يكون التالي بين اللزوم فإنه ربما كان غير بين اللزوم؛ فيتبين لزومه. فإذا صار بين اللزوم بحجة ثم استثنى المقدم بعينه للتالي بعينه فصار قياسا مفيدا فيمكن إذن يكون المقدم بينا بنفسه؛ واللزوم لا يكون بينا؛ فيتبين . وما يجب ذكره في هذا الموضع هو أن كل ما يتعلق من الأمور تعلقا بينا بأمر واحد بين كان خطور المتعلق به بالبال معينا في وقوف الذهن على صحة التالي اللازم ؛ فيكون إذا كان «أب» بينا وكان لزوم ج د » له بينا فمتى خطر «أب» بالبال لم يحتج إلى أن يستعمل قياس بالفعل في إلزام التالي البتة. فأما إذا لم يكن بينا فحينئذ يحتاج إلى أن يجرد النظر في أجزائه كما ينبغي. وكذلك حال القياس الاقتراني إذا صار مقدمًا فإنه ينعكس بیان مقدماته عن استئناف الاستثناء؛ فالقياس مقدم شرطي والتالي هو لزوم النتيجة فبحسب ذلك ليس تحتاج النفس إذا كان اللزوم بينا إلى أن يرجع. فنقول ولكن كل «ج ب» وكل «ب أ»؛ إذ هذا قد اندرج في الذهن مع خُطور المقدم لكنه ليس بين اللزوم قبل القياس وقبل الوضع وإلا فلا؛ فإنه ليس لزومه عن أمر واحد بين بل عن أمرين وعن اجتماع بينهما. وليس صورة هذا الاجتماع ثابتة في الذهن فإنه ربما خطر في الذهن التصديق بإحدى القضيتين ولم يصحبه التصديق بالأخرى على أنه ليس يخطر بالبال إلا على أنه ليس بين الصدق، وربما خطر التصديق بالأخرى، وربما خطرتهما جميعا. ولم يكن مرتبا بالفعل الترتيب الذي يؤدّي إلى النتيجة: فلم يشعر بالمشترك؛ فإن كان معنى فيه تصديق واحد فكلما خطر خطر معه الثاني. وإن كان لا يعني بل يحتاج إلى تصديقات أكثر من واحد احتاج إلى أن يجتمع معا في الحالين جميعا؛ فإن وضع المقدم يفيد علمًا بلازم غير بين بنفسه، وفي الحالين جميعا؛ إذ الخطور بالبال على تمامه يعني عن استئناف الاستثناء لكن الملزوم في أحدهما تصديق واحد، وفي الآخر تصديق أكثر من واحد مع شروط أخرى؛ وليس هذا في المقدم الذي يكون بينا بنفسه بل إذا بان أيضًا بقياس. وكذلك إذا كان اللزوم بان بحجة، وكان المقدم بينا بنفسه، فإذا كان الاستثناء المبتدأ فصلا كان تكميل القياس على صورته فصلا. ولهذا لا يستعمل أمثال المقدمات من الشرطية في العلوم بصورة القياس بل يقال لما كان كذا كان كذا؛ ولا يقال إن كان كذا كان كذا، لكن كان كذا فإذن كذا. وليس لقائل أن يقول فيلزم أن يكون استعمال المقدمات الكبرى البينة بأنفسها في القياسات فصلا وأن يكون الضمير في ذلك، وكافيا على نحو ما يستعمل : فنقول إن الفصل في القول على وجهين؛ أحدهما فصل يكون الاستثناء عنه على سبيل أنه قد فرغ من إخطاره بالبال في ضمن ما قيل؛ فلو قيل لا يستأنف إخطاره بالبال مرة ثانية على سبيل التكثير. والثاني أن يكون استثناء
In summary, this explanation is among those that require consideration: for the premise does not have to be doubtful or not doubtful in all forms. Nor does it have to be that a thing is self-evident if it is a necessary consequent of what is self-evident; for a thing may be self-evident and have a necessary consequent that is not self-evident, but its implication from that self-evident thing is evident, so that consequent is known through it. There is a difference between our saying it is self-evident and our saying its implication from a self-evident thing is evident. The consequent must be evident in implication or ultimately lead to an evident implication; otherwise all things would be self-evident. Then it would follow that the first mood of the first figure is not productive; because its syllogisms resolve into two self-evident premises, then the conclusion is evident in implication from them. So the relation of the syllogism to the conclusion is the relation of the consequent to the antecedent; the antecedent is an evident matter, as in our saying 'every C is B and every B is A'; or the consequent is evident in implication from it, so our saying 'every C is A' is evident. And similarly all secondary conclusions ad infinitum. Also, it is not necessary that the consequent be evident in implication, for it may be not evident in implication; then its implication is made evident. So when it becomes evident in implication by an argument, then the antecedent is taken for the consequent and it becomes a useful syllogism. So it is possible that the antecedent is self-evident and the implication is not evident; then it is made evident. What must be mentioned here is that every matter that is related evidently to one evident matter – the occurrence of that related matter in the mind determines the mind's stopping at the correctness of the necessary consequent. So if 'AB' is evident and the implication of 'CD' from it is evident, then whenever 'AB' occurs to the mind, there is no need to actually use a syllogism to infer the consequent at all. But if it is not evident, then one needs to abstractly consider its parts as appropriate. Similarly, the case of the categorical syllogism when it becomes a premise: its premises are clarified by repeating the exception (istithna'). So the syllogism is a conditional premise, and the consequent is the implication of the conclusion; accordingly, the soul does not need to return if the implication is evident. But we say: however, 'every C is B' and 'every B is A' – this has been subsumed in the mind with the occurrence of the premise, but it is not evident in implication before the syllogism and before the position; otherwise not. For its implication is not from one evident matter but from two matters and from the conjunction between them. The form of this conjunction is not fixed in the mind; for it may happen that the belief in one of the two propositions occurs to the mind without being accompanied by belief in the other, unless it occurs to the mind only as not evident in truth; and sometimes belief in the other occurs, and sometimes both occur. And it is not actually arranged in the order that leads to the conclusion; so one does not perceive the common term. For if there is a meaning in it of a single belief, then whenever it occurs, the second occurs with it. But if it does not mean that, but rather requires more than one belief, it needs to come together in both cases. For positing the premise yields knowledge of a consequent that is not self-evident, and in both cases; for the occurrence to the mind in its completeness means without repeating the exception, but the antecedent in one case is a single belief, and in the other is more than one belief with other conditions. This is not in the premise that is self-evident; rather, even if it is made evident by a syllogism. Similarly, if the implication is made evident by an argument, and the antecedent is self-evident, then if the exception is initiated as a distinction (fasl), completing the syllogism in its form is a distinction. For this reason, such conditional premises are not used in the sciences in the form of a syllogism; rather, it is said 'since such is such, then such is such', and it is not said 'if such is such, then such is such', but 'such is such, therefore such is such'. And one cannot say: 'Then it would follow that the use of self-evident major premises in syllogisms is a distinction, and that the pronoun in that is sufficient in the manner used.' So we say: the distinction (fasl) in speech is of two kinds: one kind is a distinction where the exception is made in the way that one has already finished bringing it to mind within what was said; so if it is said, one does not bring it to mind again by repetition. The second is that the exception is...

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the conditions under which premises and their implications are considered evident (bayyin) in demonstration. The author begins by critiquing a previous claim that a premise must be doubtful or not doubtful; he argues that it is not necessary for a premise to be doubtful in all forms. He then distinguishes between a thing being self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi) and its implication being evident (bayyin al-luzum). A thing may be self-evident while its necessary consequent is not self-evident, but the implication (luzum) from the self-evident thing to that consequent is itself evident, thus the consequent becomes known through that implication. The author then applies this to the first mood of the first figure of categorical syllogism. He argues that if we require every consequent to be self-evident, then the first mood would not be productive, because its syllogisms resolve into two self-evident premises, and the conclusion is evident only in implication from them. He explains that the relation of the syllogism to the conclusion is like that of the antecedent to the consequent in a conditional statement. For example, in 'every C is B and every B is A', the conjunction of premises is an evident antecedent, and the conclusion 'every C is A' is the consequent. The conclusion is evident because its implication from the premises is evident. However, the author notes that it is not necessary that the implication be evident initially; it may become evident through another argument. So it is possible that the antecedent (the premises) is self-evident while the implication is not, and then the implication is clarified. He then introduces a key point: when an implication relates evidently to one evident matter, the occurrence of that matter in the mind is sufficient to grasp the correctness of the consequent. For instance, if 'AB' is self-evident and the implication of 'CD' from it is evident, then whenever 'AB' occurs to the mind, one does not need to construct a syllogism actively; the consequent is immediately known. But if the implication is not evident, one must examine the parts. The author then discusses the categorical syllogism as a premise in a conditional inference. He says that when a categorical syllogism becomes a premise (i.e., when we take its form as a conditional), we need to 'repeat the exception' (istithna') to clarify the premises. The syllogism is like a conditional premise, and the consequent is the implication of the conclusion. If the implication is evident, the soul does not need to return to construct the syllogism again. However, the author points out that in the case of a two-premise syllogism like 'every C is B and every B is A', the implication is not evident before the syllogism is actually performed, because it does not arise from one evident matter but from two matters and their conjunction. The form of this conjunction is not fixed in the mind; belief in the two premises may not be simultaneously present, and even if both occur, they are not arranged in the order that yields the conclusion. Thus one does not perceive the middle term (mushtarak). The author contrasts this with cases where a single belief is sufficient: then whenever the belief occurs, the concomitant belief (the conclusion) occurs with it. But when multiple beliefs are needed, they must come together. The passage ends with a discussion of 'fasl' (distinction or separation) in speech. There are two types: one where the exception (istithna') is already completed in what was said, so that it does not need to be brought to mind again; the other type is where the exception is the initiation of a new statement. The author seems to be arguing against a potential objection that using self-evident major premises in syllogisms is a 'fasl' (superfluous separation), and he clarifies that 'fasl' can have two meanings. This passage is dense and technical, characteristic of advanced logical theory in the Islamic tradition.

لزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between a self-evident thing and its necessary consequent?
A) The consequent must always be self-evident.
B) The consequent may not be self-evident, but the implication from the self-evident thing is evident.
C) The consequent is always evident by itself.
D) There is no relationship.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between self-evidence and evident implication allows a logical chain where not every link needs to be self-evident; the evident implication suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated (hadith). Every temporally originated thing has a cause. Therefore the world has a cause.' Identify which part is self-evident, which is evident in implication, and which is not self-evident but known via the implication.
?
In usul al-fiqh, the ruling for an act may be derived from a general principle. For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Here, the implication from the premises to the conclusion is evident (qiyas jali). Explain whether the conclusion 'nabidh is forbidden' is self-evident or known through an evident implication.

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
5 min
Application to the First Mood of the First Figure
Author argues that the first mood of the first figure remains productive because the conclusion is evident in implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the epistemological status of the conclusion in a first-figure categorical syllogism using the distinction between self-evident premises and evident implication.

Core Idea
The conclusion of a first-figure syllogism is evident in implication, not self-evident, even though the premises are self-evident.

The author applies the previous distinction to the first mood of the first figure (Barbara). He argues that if we required the conclusion to be self-evident, this syllogism would not be 'productive'. However, the conclusion is evident in implication from the two self-evident premises. This shows that the syllogism is valid without requiring the conclusion to be self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first mood of the first figure
Form: All B is C, All A is B; therefore All A is C. (Arabic variables: every ج is ب, every ب is أ; therefore every ج is أ).
2
Identify the premises
In the passage, the premises are 'every C is B' and 'every B is A'. They are taken as self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi).
3
Identify the conclusion
'Every C is A'. The author says this conclusion is 'bayyin al-luzum' (evident in implication).
4
Understand the relation
The syllogism as a whole is a conditional: if premises (antecedent) then conclusion (consequent). The implication from the premises to the conclusion is evident.

الضمير في ذلك، وكافيا على نحو ما يستعمل : فنقول إن الفصل في القول على وجهين؛ أحدهما فصل يكون الاستثناء عنه على سبيل أنه قد فرغ من إخطاره بالبال في ضمن ما قيل؛ فلو قيل لا يستأنف إخطاره بالبال مرة ثانية على سبيل التكثير. والثاني أن يكون استثناء
In summary, this explanation is among those that require consideration: for the premise does not have to be doubtful or not doubtful in all forms. Nor does it have to be that a thing is self-evident if it is a necessary consequent of what is self-evident; for a thing may be self-evident and have a necessary consequent that is not self-evident, but its implication from that self-evident thing is evident, so that consequent is known through it. There is a difference between our saying it is self-evident and our saying its implication from a self-evident thing is evident. The consequent must be evident in implication or ultimately lead to an evident implication; otherwise all things would be self-evident. Then it would follow that the first mood of the first figure is not productive; because its syllogisms resolve into two self-evident premises, then the conclusion is evident in implication from them. So the relation of the syllogism to the conclusion is the relation of the consequent to the antecedent; the antecedent is an evident matter, as in our saying 'every C is B and every B is A'; or the consequent is evident in implication from it, so our saying 'every C is A' is evident. And similarly all secondary conclusions ad infinitum. Also, it is not necessary that the consequent be evident in implication, for it may be not evident in implication; then its implication is made evident. So when it becomes evident in implication by an argument, then the antecedent is taken for the consequent and it becomes a useful syllogism. So it is possible that the antecedent is self-evident and the implication is not evident; then it is made evident. What must be mentioned here is that every matter that is related evidently to one evident matter – the occurrence of that related matter in the mind determines the mind's stopping at the correctness of the necessary consequent. So if 'AB' is evident and the implication of 'CD' from it is evident, then whenever 'AB' occurs to the mind, there is no need to actually use a syllogism to infer the consequent at all. But if it is not evident, then one needs to abstractly consider its parts as appropriate. Similarly, the case of the categorical syllogism when it becomes a premise: its premises are clarified by repeating the exception (istithna'). So the syllogism is a conditional premise, and the consequent is the implication of the conclusion; accordingly, the soul does not need to return if the implication is evident. But we say: however, 'every C is B' and 'every B is A' – this has been subsumed in the mind with the occurrence of the premise, but it is not evident in implication before the syllogism and before the position; otherwise not. For its implication is not from one evident matter but from two matters and from the conjunction between them. The form of this conjunction is not fixed in the mind; for it may happen that the belief in one of the two propositions occurs to the mind without being accompanied by belief in the other, unless it occurs to the mind only as not evident in truth; and sometimes belief in the other occurs, and sometimes both occur. And it is not actually arranged in the order that leads to the conclusion; so one does not perceive the common term. For if there is a meaning in it of a single belief, then whenever it occurs, the second occurs with it. But if it does not mean that, but rather requires more than one belief, it needs to come together in both cases. For positing the premise yields knowledge of a consequent that is not self-evident, and in both cases; for the occurrence to the mind in its completeness means without repeating the exception, but the antecedent in one case is a single belief, and in the other is more than one belief with other conditions. This is not in the premise that is self-evident; rather, even if it is made evident by a syllogism. Similarly, if the implication is made evident by an argument, and the antecedent is self-evident, then if the exception is initiated as a distinction (fasl), completing the syllogism in its form is a distinction. For this reason, such conditional premises are not used in the sciences in the form of a syllogism; rather, it is said 'since such is such, then such is such', and it is not said 'if such is such, then such is such', but 'such is such, therefore such is such'. And one cannot say: 'Then it would follow that the use of self-evident major premises in syllogisms is a distinction, and that the pronoun in that is sufficient in the manner used.' So we say: the distinction (fasl) in speech is of two kinds: one kind is a distinction where the exception is made in the way that one has already finished bringing it to mind within what was said; so if it is said, one does not bring it to mind again by repetition. The second is that the exception is...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the conditions under which premises and their implications are considered evident (bayyin) in demonstration. The author begins by critiquing a previous claim that a premise must be doubtful or not doubtful; he argues that it is not necessary for a premise to be doubtful in all forms. He then distinguishes between a thing being self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi) and its implication being evident (bayyin al-luzum). A thing may be self-evident while its necessary consequent is not self-evident, but the implication (luzum) from the self-evident thing to that consequent is itself evident, thus the consequent becomes known through that implication. The author then applies this to the first mood of the first figure of categorical syllogism. He argues that if we require every consequent to be self-evident, then the first mood would not be productive, because its syllogisms resolve into two self-evident premises, and the conclusion is evident only in implication from them. He explains that the relation of the syllogism to the conclusion is like that of the antecedent to the consequent in a conditional statement. For example, in 'every C is B and every B is A', the conjunction of premises is an evident antecedent, and the conclusion 'every C is A' is the consequent. The conclusion is evident because its implication from the premises is evident. However, the author notes that it is not necessary that the implication be evident initially; it may become evident through another argument. So it is possible that the antecedent (the premises) is self-evident while the implication is not, and then the implication is clarified. He then introduces a key point: when an implication relates evidently to one evident matter, the occurrence of that matter in the mind is sufficient to grasp the correctness of the consequent. For instance, if 'AB' is self-evident and the implication of 'CD' from it is evident, then whenever 'AB' occurs to the mind, one does not need to construct a syllogism actively; the consequent is immediately known. But if the implication is not evident, one must examine the parts. The author then discusses the categorical syllogism as a premise in a conditional inference. He says that when a categorical syllogism becomes a premise (i.e., when we take its form as a conditional), we need to 'repeat the exception' (istithna') to clarify the premises. The syllogism is like a conditional premise, and the consequent is the implication of the conclusion. If the implication is evident, the soul does not need to return to construct the syllogism again. However, the author points out that in the case of a two-premise syllogism like 'every C is B and every B is A', the implication is not evident before the syllogism is actually performed, because it does not arise from one evident matter but from two matters and their conjunction. The form of this conjunction is not fixed in the mind; belief in the two premises may not be simultaneously present, and even if both occur, they are not arranged in the order that yields the conclusion. Thus one does not perceive the middle term (mushtarak). The author contrasts this with cases where a single belief is sufficient: then whenever the belief occurs, the concomitant belief (the conclusion) occurs with it. But when multiple beliefs are needed, they must come together. The passage ends with a discussion of 'fasl' (distinction or separation) in speech. There are two types: one where the exception (istithna') is already completed in what was said, so that it does not need to be brought to mind again; the other type is where the exception is the initiation of a new statement. The author seems to be arguing against a potential objection that using self-evident major premises in syllogisms is a 'fasl' (superfluous separation), and he clarifies that 'fasl' can have two meanings. This passage is dense and technical, characteristic of advanced logical theory in the Islamic tradition.

لزوم
مقدم
تالي

A) Because the premises are not really self-evident.
B) Because the conclusion is evident in implication from the premises.
C) Because the syllogism is not actually used.
D) Because the conclusion is more evident than the premises.

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism of the first figure produces a conclusion that is evident in implication, even if not self-evident, thus preserving the chain of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Role of Conjunction and Istithna' in Evident Implication
Author discusses why a two-premise syllogism's implication is not automatically evident and clarifies the meaning of fasl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the implication from a multi-premise categorical syllogism is not immediately evident without mental arrangement, and how the concept of fasl (distinction) addresses the use of self-evident premises.

Core Idea
A two-premise syllogism requires both premises to be present and arranged; its implication is not evident until the mind perceives the conjunction.

The author now delves into the psychological and logical conditions for an implication to be evident. He argues that in a simple conditional with one antecedent, the implication is evident immediately when the antecedent occurs. But in a categorical syllogism with two premises, the implication from the conjunction is not automatically present unless both beliefs are held together and arranged. He then discusses the concept of fasl (distinction) in speech, clarifying that using self-evident major premises does not constitute a 'separation' if the exception is already implicit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction between simple and complex antecedents
A single belief (e.g., 'AB is evident') has an evident implication. A conjunction of two beliefs (e.g., 'every C is B' and 'every B is A') does not have an automatically evident implication because the mind may not hold both simultaneously or in the correct order.
2
Understand the need for the middle term
The mind does not perceive the middle term unless the premises are brought together. Without perceiving the middle term, the implication is not evident.
3
Examine the concept of fasl
The author distinguishes two types of fasl: one where the exception (istithna') is already completed in the speech (so no need to repeat), and another where it is a new initiation. This addresses an objection that using self-evident premises in syllogisms is fasl.

In summary, this explanation is among those that require consideration: for the premise does not have to be doubtful or not doubtful in all forms. Nor does it have to be that a thing is self-evident if it is a necessary consequent of what is self-evident; for a thing may be self-evident and have a necessary consequent that is not self-evident, but its implication from that self-evident thing is evident, so that consequent is known through it. There is a difference between our saying it is self-evident and our saying its implication from a self-evident thing is evident. The consequent must be evident in implication or ultimately lead to an evident implication; otherwise all things would be self-evident. Then it would follow that the first mood of the first figure is not productive; because its syllogisms resolve into two self-evident premises, then the conclusion is evident in implication from them. So the relation of the syllogism to the conclusion is the relation of the consequent to the antecedent; the antecedent is an evident matter, as in our saying 'every C is B and every B is A'; or the consequent is evident in implication from it, so our saying 'every C is A' is evident. And similarly all secondary conclusions ad infinitum. Also, it is not necessary that the consequent be evident in implication, for it may be not evident in implication; then its implication is made evident. So when it becomes evident in implication by an argument, then the antecedent is taken for the consequent and it becomes a useful syllogism. So it is possible that the antecedent is self-evident and the implication is not evident; then it is made evident. What must be mentioned here is that every matter that is related evidently to one evident matter – the occurrence of that related matter in the mind determines the mind's stopping at the correctness of the necessary consequent. So if 'AB' is evident and the implication of 'CD' from it is evident, then whenever 'AB' occurs to the mind, there is no need to actually use a syllogism to infer the consequent at all. But if it is not evident, then one needs to abstractly consider its parts as appropriate. Similarly, the case of the categorical syllogism when it becomes a premise: its premises are clarified by repeating the exception (istithna'). So the syllogism is a conditional premise, and the consequent is the implication of the conclusion; accordingly, the soul does not need to return if the implication is evident. But we say: however, 'every C is B' and 'every B is A' – this has been subsumed in the mind with the occurrence of the premise, but it is not evident in implication before the syllogism and before the position; otherwise not. And it is not actually arranged in the order that leads to the conclusion; so one does not perceive the common term. This is not in the premise that is self-evident; rather, even if it is made evident by a syllogism. Similarly, if the implication is made evident by an argument, and the antecedent is self-evident, then if the exception is initiated as a distinction (fasl), completing the syllogism in its form is a distinction. For this reason, such conditional premises are not used in the sciences in the form of a syllogism; rather, it is said 'since such is such, then such is such', and it is not said 'if such is such, then such is such', but 'such is such, therefore such is such'. And one cannot say: 'Then it would follow that the use of self-evident major premises in syllogisms is a distinction, and that the pronoun in that is sufficient in the manner used.' So we say: the distinction (fasl) in speech is of two kinds: one kind is a distinction where the exception is made in the way that one has already finished bringing it to mind within what was said; so if it is said, one does not bring it to mind again by repetition. Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the conditions under which premises and their implications are considered evident (bayyin) in demonstration. The author begins by critiquing a previous claim that a premise must be doubtful or not doubtful; he argues that it is not necessary for a premise to be doubtful in all forms. He then distinguishes between a thing being self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi) and its implication being evident (bayyin al-luzum). A thing may be self-evident while its necessary consequent is not self-evident, but the implication (luzum) from the self-evident thing to that consequent is itself evident, thus the consequent becomes known through that implication. The author then applies this to the first mood of the first figure of categorical syllogism. He argues that if we require every consequent to be self-evident, then the first mood would not be productive, because its syllogisms resolve into two self-evident premises, and the conclusion is evident only in implication from them. He explains that the relation of the syllogism to the conclusion is like that of the antecedent to the consequent in a conditional statement. For example, in 'every C is B and every B is A', the conjunction of premises is an evident antecedent, and the conclusion 'every C is A' is the consequent. The conclusion is evident because its implication from the premises is evident. However, the author notes that it is not necessary that the implication be evident initially; it may become evident through another argument. So it is possible that the antecedent (the premises) is self-evident while the implication is not, and then the implication is clarified. He then introduces a key point: when an implication relates evidently to one evident matter, the occurrence of that matter in the mind is sufficient to grasp the correctness of the consequent. For instance, if 'AB' is self-evident and the implication of 'CD' from it is evident, then whenever 'AB' occurs to the mind, one does not need to construct a syllogism actively; the consequent is immediately known. But if the implication is not evident, one must examine the parts. The author then discusses the categorical syllogism as a premise in a conditional inference. He says that when a categorical syllogism becomes a premise (i.e., when we take its form as a conditional), we need to 'repeat the exception' (istithna') to clarify the premises. The syllogism is like a conditional premise, and the consequent is the implication of the conclusion. If the implication is evident, the soul does not need to return to construct the syllogism again. However, the author points out that in the case of a two-premise syllogism like 'every C is B and every B is A', the implication is not evident before the syllogism is actually performed, because it does not arise from one evident matter but from two matters and their conjunction. The form of this conjunction is not fixed in the mind; belief in the two premises may not be simultaneously present, and even if both occur, they are not arranged in the order that yields the conclusion. Thus one does not perceive the middle term (mushtarak). The author contrasts this with cases where a single belief is sufficient: then whenever the belief occurs, the concomitant belief (the conclusion) occurs with it. But when multiple beliefs are needed, they must come together. The passage ends with a discussion of 'fasl' (distinction or separation) in speech. There are two types: one where the exception (istithna') is already completed in what was said, so that it does not need to be brought to mind again; the other type is where the exception is the initiation of a new statement. The author seems to be arguing against a potential objection that using self-evident major premises in syllogisms is a 'fasl' (superfluous separation), and he clarifies that 'fasl' can have two meanings. This passage is dense and technical, characteristic of advanced logical theory in the Islamic tradition.

The Two Methods of Reductio ad Absurdum and Their Critique
Author explains two methods of reductio ad absurdum and critiques both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two methods of qiyas al-khalf and articulate the author's critique of each, including the flaw in the major premise's claimed necessity.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum is not always a slam dunk; its validity depends on the necessity of its premises.

This passage examines two ways of constructing a reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf) and then critiques both. The author shows that the standard method of the First Teacher (Aristotle) relies on a major premise whose truth is claimed necessary, but that claim may be false. A variant method is even more problematic, being unnecessarily cumbersome. Understanding these flaws is crucial for evaluating arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh that use reductio.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two methods
The author first presents the method of the First Teacher (Aristotle) and then a variant by a group. Note the key difference: the first uses an exceptional syllogism on a conditional; the second argues that the consequent is impossible because it combines with a truth that entails the impossible.
2
Trace the first method's structure
Follow the steps: for a categorical conclusion (e.g., 'Not every J is B'), assume its contradictory ('Every J is B') is true, derive a conditional, then use an exceptional syllogism by denying the consequent to produce the antecedent (the original desired conclusion). The same pattern is used for conditional conclusions.
This method produces a conditional conclusion that is then used in a further syllogism.
3
Examine the second method's reasoning
Here, after positing the conditional minor premise, they claim the consequent is impossible because it combines with a truth that leads to an impossibility. They argue that the impossibility is neither the composition of premises nor the major premise, so it must be the consequent itself, making it impossible.
This method involves an extra step of analyzing the source of impossibility.
4
Understand the critique of the first method
The author denies the claimed necessity of the major premise. It is possible that the composite (the whole argument) necessitates the impossibility, not the parts. Therefore, the first method's conclusion is not guaranteed.
This is a logical refutation by possibility.
5
Understand the critique of the second method
The author says the second method suffers from the same flaw as the first and adds unnecessary complexity. It is a cumbersome extension without need, making it even less satisfactory.
The critique shows that both methods fail to provide a sound reductio.

(ج) ب) كاذبا كان كل (ج) أ» صادقًا. ثم نجعل هذه النتيجة مقدمة شرطية لقياس استثنائي يستثنى نقيض تاليها بأن يقال لكن ليس كل ( أ) على أنه محال فينتج نقيض المقدم الذي هو المطلوب وأما إذا كان المطلوب إثبات مقدمة شرطية ولتكن هي قولنا كلما كان ( د) فـ «هـ ز » فنقول إن لم يكن قولنا كلما كان (ج) ده فـ هـز» صادقًا كان قولنا فليس كلما كان جد» فـ هـز» صادقا؛ فنجعلها صغرى ونضيف إليها قولنا وكلَّما كان «ج د» فـ «هز» لينتظم منهما قياس شطري اقتراني في الشكل الثاني، وينتج قولنا إن لم يكن قولنا كلَّما كان جد» فـ «هز» صادقًا كان قولنا ليس كلما كان ج د فحط صادقًا. ثم نجعل هذه النتيجة مقدمة شرطية لقياس استثنائي يستثنى نقيض تاليها بأن يقال كلما كان (ج) د فـ ح ط ينتج نقيض مقدمها وهو قولنا كلما كان «ج د» ف هز» وهو المطلوب. ثم تقرير الخلف على الوجه الذي مرَّ ذكره هو الذي ذهب إليه المعلم الأول ومن تابعه، وقوم منهم يقررون على وجه آخر وهو أنهم لما وضعوا الصغرى الشرطية فقالوا لكن التالي محال لما أنه يجتمع منه ومن حق ما يلزم منه المحال ، وكل ما يجتمع منه؛ ومن حق ما يلزم منه المحال فهو محال. ثم إنهم بينوا الصغرى في كل مادة بما يليق به. وأما الكبرى فقد ادعوا الضرورة في صدقها. ومنهم من تعرض لبيانه بعد بيان أن التالي يلزم منه ومن حق محال فقال هذا المحال إما أن يكون عين الصغرى أو عين الكبرى أو عين نفس التأليف لكن ليس عين نفس التأليف بدليل الخلف في غير هذا الموضع، ولا عين الكبرى؛ فإن الحق لا يفضي إلى الباطل فهو إذن عين الصغرى التي هي التالي، فيكون التالي محالا . ثم كل واحد من هذين الطريقين لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ أما الأول فلأن الكبرى التي ادعوا الضرورة في صدقها ممنوعة لاحتمال أن يكون المستلزم للمحال المجموع من حيث هو المجموع ليس ما يكون في ضمنه من الأجزاء. وأما الثاني فلأنه مشتمل على ما عليه الأول وعلى غيره كذلك، وهو التكلُّف في التطويل من غير حاجة.
The first [method] and its exceptional premise is the removal of the contradictory of the consequent of this conditional. In sum, it is reasoning from the impossibility of a necessary implication of one of the two contradictories to its impossibility, and from its impossibility to the truth of the other side. So it is said: if the sought conclusion is categorical, for example 'Not every J is B' – if our statement 'Not every J is B' is false, then its contradictory, i.e., 'Every J is B', is true. So we make this conditional a minor premise, and add to it a true categorical premise, i.e., 'Every B is A'. It produces a conditional: 'If "Not every J is B" is false, then "Every J is A" is true.' Then we make this result a conditional premise for an exceptional syllogism that excepts the contradictory of its consequent, by saying: 'But it is not that every A is ...' (indicating impossibility), so it produces the contradictory of the antecedent, which is the sought conclusion. If the sought conclusion is to establish a conditional premise, for example say: 'Whenever D is H, then H is Z?' We say: If our statement 'Whenever J is D, then H is Z' is not true, then our statement 'It is not that whenever J is D, then H is Z' is true; we make it a minor premise and add to it our statement 'Whenever J is D, then H is Z' so that from them a bipartite categorical syllogism in the second figure is formed, and it produces our statement: 'If our statement "Whenever J is D, then H is Z" is not true, then our statement "It is not that whenever J is D, then H is Z" is true.' Then we make this result a conditional premise for an exceptional syllogism that excepts the contradictory of its consequent, by saying: 'Whenever J is D, then H is Z' produces the contradictory of its antecedent, i.e., our statement 'Whenever J is D, then H is Z', which is the sought conclusion. Then the exposition of the reductio (khalf) in the manner mentioned is what the First Teacher and his followers adopted. Some others adopt a different manner: when they posit the conditional minor premise, they say: 'But the consequent is impossible because it combines with a truth that entails something impossible, and whatever combines with a truth that entails something impossible is itself impossible.' Then they explain the minor premise in every subject matter appropriately. As for the major premise, they claimed its truth is necessary. Among them there are those who undertook to explain it after explaining that the consequent entails from itself and from a truth an impossibility. He said: 'This impossibility is either the minor premise itself, or the major premise itself, or the composition itself. But it is not the composition itself, by the evidence of the reductio elsewhere; nor the major premise itself, because truth does not lead to falsehood. So it is then the minor premise itself, which is the consequent, so the consequent is impossible.' Then each of these two methods is not as it should be: As for the first, the major premise whose truth they claimed necessary is denied, because it is possible that what necessitates the impossibility is the composite as composite, not what is within it of parts. As for the second, it includes what the first includes and other things as well, and it is a cumbersome extension without need.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the method of reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf). It first presents two ways of constructing the reductio: the method of the First Teacher (Aristotle) and a variant by a group. The author then criticizes both: the first is invalid because the claimed necessary major premise may not be necessary; the second is redundant and verbose. The author follows the argument order: first describing the mechanics of the first method with categorical and conditional constructions, then the second method's claim that the consequent is impossible because it combines with a truth leading to impossibility. The critique points out that the major premise's necessity is not proven in the first, and the second is unnecessarily long.

Key Terms
الخلف
المقدمة الشرطية
نقيض التالي
القياس الاستثنائي
المعلم الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the flaw in the first method of qiyas al-khalf?
A) The minor premise is not always true.
B) The major premise claimed necessary may not be necessary.
C) The argument never produces a valid conclusion.
D) The method only works for categorical propositions.

Key Takeaway
Reductio ad absurdum is only valid if the major premise's necessity is assured; the author shows that the composite may be the source of impossibility, not the parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the two methods of khalf is being used in the following argument: 'If the Quran is created, then it is not pre-eternal. But if it is not pre-eternal, then it is not the speech of Allah. However, the Quran is the speech of Allah. Therefore, the Quran is not created.' Then explain why the author would find this argument problematic.
?
Construct a reductio ad absurdum using the first method described in the lesson to prove that 'Every action is subject to divine decree' from the premise 'If an action is not subject to divine decree, then it is independent of God's will.' Show the steps as given in the passage.

Logical Flaws in the Causal Proof for Origination
Author reconstructs and critiques the logicians' syllogism for temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logicians' categorical syllogism for the origination of bodies and identify its formal and material flaws.

Core Idea
A formally valid syllogism can still be invalid in demonstration if its premises are unacceptable.

The logicians claim a strong proof for the temporal origination of bodies. The author reconstructs their argument, then systematically exposes its weaknesses – both in its logical form and in the truth of its premises. This lesson will walk you through the syllogism and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the argument
Identify the key claims: the building is originated, its origination is from a cause, that cause is a property, the property is either B, J, or D, not B or J, therefore it is D. Write this as a series of categorical propositions.
The variables B, J, D are placeholders for specific properties (e.g., composition, accidentalness, corporeality).
2
Identify the syllogistic figure
The author notes that one formulation yields two affirmative premises in the second figure. Check: second figure has middle term as predicate of both premises. Is the syllogism valid in that figure?
Second figure with two affirmative premises is invalid.
3
Locate the conversion error
The conclusion 'the cause is D' becomes universal: 'everything that is a cause of origination is a body'. But universal propositions do not convert simply. The intended conclusion 'every body is a cause of origination' does not follow.
This is a classic case of illicit conversion.
4
Assess material truth of premises
Beyond form, the author challenges the premises: why must the cause be a property? Why only B, J, D? Why not the essence itself? These are material (not formal) objections.
Material flaws make the argument unsuitable for demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البناء حادث وحدوثه إما لذاته أو لعلة؛ وليس لذاته فهو لعلة. والعلة صفة، والصفة إما «ب» وإما (ج) وإما «د»، فالعلة إما «ب» وإما «ج» وإما «د» لكن ليس «ب» ولا «ج» فينتج أن العلة «د» لا أنها هي الدال؛ إذ موضوع النتيجة ومحمولها لا يعبر كل واحد منهما عما كانا عليه في المقدمتين. ولا يمكنهم أن يقولوا والعلة صفة والصفة إما هي الباء أو الجيم أو الدال حتى تكون النتيجة أن هذه العلة هي الدال، ولا أن يقولوا إما أن يكون (ب) صفة أو (ج) صفة أو «د» صفة لكذب القضيتين أولا، فإن كل ذلك صفة لا واحد منها فقط. ولعدم إنتاج الوضع الثاني فإنهما موجبتان من الشكل الثاني. وإذا كانت النتيجة إن العلة هي كذا فهذه قضية عامتها أن تصير كلية فيكون كل ما هو علة للحدوث جسما والقضية الكلية لا تنعكس فلا تصير كل ما هو جسم علة للحدوث هذا هو بيان فاسد ما يكون أقوى الطرق عندهم إلا أنه. وإن كان كذلك فلا بأس بأن يستعمل في الجدل؛ إذ ليس المطلوب فيه التيقن بل إقناع النفس وتطييبه بما يعتقد في المشهور أنه منتج، هذا إذا كان المطلوب كليا. فأما إذا كان جزئياً وأريد إثباته بالمثال فالمثال في إثباته قياس منتج من الشكل الثالث كما إذا قلت البناء جسم والبناء محدث ينتج أن بعض الجسم محدث. ولكن لا يلزم أن ذلك الجسم هو الجسم المنازع فيه وهو السماء أو غيره من الأمور المنازع فيها. وقد نقل عن الشيخ في ضعف هذا الطريق وجوه أولها أن يقال لا نسلم أن الحدوث معلل بالعلة بل لا يكون فإنه إذا كان من اللوازم أن يكون كل حكم معللا بالعلة لكان من اللوازم أن تكون علة ذلك الحكم، وعليتها أيضًا معللًا بعلة أخرى إلى غير النهاية؛ وذلك محال. وإن سلمنا أنه معلل لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون معللا بالذات التي هو عارض لها فإن من الأحكام ما تكون عللها ذوات معروضاتها. وإن سلمنا أنه معلل بوصف لكن لم قلتم بأنه معلل بهذا دون غيره. ولا يقال إنها منحصرة فيما ذكرنا؛ وغيره لا يكون علة فيتعين هذا؛ إذ الانحصار في المنع لما أنه وهو البناء مؤلف من العناصر الأربعة. وذلك التركيب كان صادرًا من الآدمي في زمان معين على وجه لا يبعد أن يصدر مثله عن مثله بخلاف السماء. وإن قيل هب أنه كذلك لكن الصالح للعلية ليس إلا أو للجسمية، فنقول هذا في حيز المنع. وإن قيل لو كان لكان معلوما بعد التكلّف في الاستقراء فنقول عدم العلم لا يدل على عدم الوجود. وإن سلمنا أن الجسمية علة للحدوث لكن لم قلتم بأنه يلزم منه أن تكون السماء حادثة. وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو لم تكن السماء مشتملة على الموانع يمكن أن يكون لها من الخواص ما يكون مانعا عن الحدوث. ولأن العلة لا تكون مؤثرة إلا وأن تكون تامة وهي المستجمعة لجميع الشرائط وكونها تامة في كل صورة من هاتين الصورتين لا يكون متحققا بل في حيز المنع على ما عرف. وأما الاعتراضات على الطرفين الآخرين فإنها من جملة المشهورات فلا حاجة إلى التعرض لها في معرض الإثبات.
The building is originated, and its origination is either from itself or from a cause; it is not from itself, so it is from a cause. And the cause is a property, and the property is either 'B' or 'J' or 'D', so the cause is either 'B' or 'J' or 'D', but it is not 'B' and not 'J', so it follows that the cause is 'D', but not that it is the letter D; because the subject and predicate of the conclusion do not each express what they were in the two premises. And they cannot say: the cause is a property, and the property is either the B, the J, or the D, so that the conclusion is that this cause is the D; nor can they say: either B is a property, or J is a property, or D is a property, because the two disjunctions are false first, for all of that is a property, not only one of them. And because the second position does not produce a conclusion, for the two propositions are affirmative from the second figure. And if the conclusion is 'the cause is such-and-such', then this proposition is generally such that it becomes universal, so that everything that is a cause of origination is a body, and the universal proposition does not convert, so it does not become that everything that is a body is a cause of origination. This is the exposition of the weakness of what they consider the strongest method. However, if this is the case, there is no harm in using it in dialectic; for the goal there is not certainty but to persuade the soul and make it comfortable with what is commonly believed to be productive. This is if the desired conclusion is universal. As for if it is particular and one wants to prove it by example, then the example in proving it is a productive syllogism from the third figure, as when you say: the building is a body, and the building is originated, it produces that some body is originated. But it is not necessary that that body is the body under dispute, which is the heaven or other things under dispute. And it has been transmitted from the Shaykh about the weakness of this method: first, it may be said: we do not accept that origination is caused by a cause; rather it is not, for if it were necessary that every judgment be caused by a cause, it would be necessary that the cause of that judgment also be caused by another cause ad infinitum, which is impossible. And if we accept that it is caused, why is it not permissible that it be caused by the essence to which it is an accident? For among judgments are those whose causes are the essences of their subjects. And if we accept that it is caused by a description, why did you say that it is caused by this and not another? And it cannot be said that they are confined to what we mentioned, and that other than it is not a cause, so this is specified; because the confinement is denied, since it (the building) is composed of the four elements. That composition originated from a human being at a specific time, in a manner not unlikely that the like of it could originate from the like of it, unlike the heaven. And if it is said: suppose it is so, but that which is fit to be the cause is only corporeality, we say: this is in the realm of denial. And if it is said: if it were so, it would be known after effort in induction, we say: lack of knowledge does not indicate lack of existence. And if we accept that corporeality is the cause of origination, why do you say that it necessarily follows that the heaven is originated? It would only be so if the heaven did not include impediments; it is possible that it has properties that prevent origination. And because the cause is only effective when it is complete, i.e., comprising all conditions, and its being complete in each of these two forms is not realized, but is in the realm of denial, as known. As for the objections on the other two sides, they are among the commonplaces, so there is no need to bring them up in the context of proof.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of the weakness of what the logicians consider their strongest method for proving the origination of bodies. The author first reconstructs their argument: the building (a body) is originated; its origination must be from a cause; that cause is a property; the property is either B, J, or D; it is not B or J; therefore it is D. The author then points out several flaws: the categorical syllogism used is invalid because the subject and predicate of the conclusion do not correspond correctly, and if formulated differently, the premises are false or unproductive. Additionally, the universal conclusion 'every cause of origination is a body' does not convert to 'every body is a cause of origination'. The author concedes the argument may be used in dialectic (jadal) because dialectic aims for persuasion, not certainty. Then objections transmitted from 'the Shaykh' (likely Ibn Sina) are listed: (1) it is not accepted that every judgment has a cause; (2) the cause might be the subject's essence, not an attribute; (3) the confinement of causes to those mentioned is denied; (4) corporeality might not be the cause; (5) even if it were, the heaven may have impediments preventing origination; (6) the cause must be complete, which is not shown. The author concludes that further objections are from commonplaces and need not be mentioned.

Key Terms
علة
حدوث
الشكل الثاني

Checkpoint
Which logical error does the author identify when the logicians' conclusion is universalized to 'every cause of origination is a body'?
A) The conclusion is particular, not universal.
B) Universal propositions cannot be converted.
C) The middle term is not distributed.
D) The second figure is invalid with two negative premises.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' proof fails because the conclusion does not follow from the premises as claimed, and the universal proposition cannot be converted.

?

Dialectical Use of a Weak Proof
Author discusses the dialectical utility of the weak proof and transmits objections from Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between demonstrative and dialectical reasoning and evaluate the strength of the transmitted objections against the causal proof.

Core Idea
Dialectic aims for persuasion, not certainty; thus a flawed argument may still be useful in dialectic.

After exposing the logical flaws in the causal proof, the author makes a surprising concession: the argument can still be used in dialectic (jadal) because dialectic's goal is persuasion, not certainty. He then transmits a series of objections from 'the Shaykh' (Ibn Sina) that further undermine the proof. This lesson explores the boundary between demonstration and dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dialectic concession
Read the author's statement: 'ليس المطلوب فيه التيقن بل إقناع النفس وتطييبه بما يعتقد في المشهور أنه منتج'. Why does the author allow a flawed argument in dialectic? What is the standard of dialectic?
Dialectic relies on accepted opinions (mashhūrāt), not certain premises.
2
List the objections from the Shaykh
The passage lists six objections. Identify each one: (1) origination may not require a cause; (2) cause could be the essence; (3) confinement to specific properties is denied; (4) corporeality may not be the cause; (5) even if cause is corporeality, the heaven may have impediments; (6) the cause must be complete, which is not shown.
These objections attack both formal and material aspects.
3
Classify each objection
For each objection, decide whether it targets the logical form (syllogistic structure) or the material truth (acceptability of premises). For example, objecting to the confinement of properties is a material objection.
This helps see the multi-layered critique.
4
Assess the dialectical strength
Even though the argument is weak for demonstration, it may be persuasive to an opponent who accepts the premises. Why would the author still consider it useful in dialectic?
The goal is to convince, not to prove with certainty.

المطلوب فيه التيقن بل إقناع النفس وتطييبه بما يعتقد في المشهور أنه منتج، هذا إذا كان المطلوب كليا. فأما إذا كان جزئياً وأريد إثباته بالمثال فالمثال في إثباته قياس منتج من الشكل الثالث كما إذا قلت البناء جسم والبناء محدث ينتج أن بعض الجسم محدث. ولكن لا يلزم أن ذلك الجسم هو الجسم المنازع فيه وهو السماء أو غيره من الأمور المنازع فيها. وقد نقل عن الشيخ في ضعف هذا الطريق وجوه أولها أن يقال لا نسلم أن الحدوث معلل بالعلة بل لا يكون فإنه إذا كان من اللوازم أن يكون كل حكم معللا بالعلة لكان من اللوازم أن تكون علة ذلك الحكم، وعليتها أيضًا معللًا بعلة أخرى إلى غير النهاية؛ وذلك محال. وإن سلمنا أنه معلل لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون معللا بالذات التي هو عارض لها فإن من الأحكام ما تكون عللها ذوات معروضاتها. وإن سلمنا أنه معلل بوصف لكن لم قلتم بأنه معلل بهذا دون غيره. ولا يقال إنها منحصرة فيما ذكرنا؛ وغيره لا يكون علة فيتعين هذا؛ إذ الانحصار في المنع لما أنه وهو البناء مؤلف من العناصر الأربعة. وذلك التركيب كان صادرًا من الآدمي في زمان معين على وجه لا يبعد أن يصدر مثله عن مثله بخلاف السماء. وإن قيل هب أنه كذلك لكن الصالح للعلية ليس إلا أو للجسمية، فنقول هذا في حيز المنع. وإن قيل لو كان لكان معلوما بعد التكلّف في الاستقراء فنقول عدم العلم لا يدل على عدم الوجود. وإن سلمنا أن الجسمية علة للحدوث لكن لم قلتم بأنه يلزم منه أن تكون السماء حادثة. وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو لم تكن السماء مشتملة على الموانع يمكن أن يكون لها من الخواص ما يكون مانعا عن الحدوث. ولأن العلة لا تكون مؤثرة إلا وأن تكون تامة وهي المستجمعة لجميع الشرائط وكونها تامة في كل صورة من هاتين الصورتين لا يكون متحققا بل في حيز المنع على ما عرف. وأما الاعتراضات على الطرفين الآخرين فإنها من جملة المشهورات فلا حاجة إلى التعرض لها في معرض الإثبات.
The building is originated, and its origination is either from itself or from a cause; it is not from itself, so it is from a cause. And the cause is a property, and the property is either 'B' or 'J' or 'D', so the cause is either 'B' or 'J' or 'D', but it is not 'B' and not 'J', so it follows that the cause is 'D', but not that it is the letter D; because the subject and predicate of the conclusion do not each express what they were in the two premises. And they cannot say: the cause is a property, and the property is either the B, the J, or the D, so that the conclusion is that this cause is the D; nor can they say: either B is a property, or J is a property, or D is a property, because the two disjunctions are false first, for all of that is a property, not only one of them. And because the second position does not produce a conclusion, for the two propositions are affirmative from the second figure. And if the conclusion is 'the cause is such-and-such', then this proposition is generally such that it becomes universal, so that everything that is a cause of origination is a body, and the universal proposition does not convert, so it does not become that everything that is a body is a cause of origination. This is the exposition of the weakness of what they consider the strongest method. However, if this is the case, there is no harm in using it in dialectic; for the goal there is not certainty but to persuade the soul and make it comfortable with what is commonly believed to be productive. This is if the desired conclusion is universal. As for if it is particular and one wants to prove it by example, then the example in proving it is a productive syllogism from the third figure, as when you say: the building is a body, and the building is originated, it produces that some body is originated. But it is not necessary that that body is the body under dispute, which is the heaven or other things under dispute. And it has been transmitted from the Shaykh about the weakness of this method: first, it may be said: we do not accept that origination is caused by a cause; rather it is not, for if it were necessary that every judgment be caused by a cause, it would be necessary that the cause of that judgment also be caused by another cause ad infinitum, which is impossible. And if we accept that it is caused, why is it not permissible that it be caused by the essence to which it is an accident? For among judgments are those whose causes are the essences of their subjects. And if we accept that it is caused by a description, why did you say that it is caused by this and not another? And it cannot be said that they are confined to what we mentioned, and that other than it is not a cause, so this is specified; because the confinement is denied, since it (the building) is composed of the four elements. That composition originated from a human being at a specific time, in a manner not unlikely that the like of it could originate from the like of it, unlike the heaven. And if it is said: suppose it is so, but that which is fit to be the cause is only corporeality, we say: this is in the realm of denial. And if it is said: if it were so, it would be known after effort in induction, we say: lack of knowledge does not indicate lack of existence. And if we accept that corporeality is the cause of origination, why do you say that it necessarily follows that the heaven is originated? It would only be so if the heaven did not include impediments; it is possible that it has properties that prevent origination. And because the cause is only effective when it is complete, i.e., comprising all conditions, and its being complete in each of these two forms is not realized, but is in the realm of denial, as known. As for the objections on the other two sides, they are among the commonplaces, so there is no need to bring them up in the context of proof.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of the weakness of what the logicians consider their strongest method for proving the origination of bodies. The author first reconstructs their argument: the building (a body) is originated; its origination must be from a cause; that cause is a property; the property is either B, J, or D; it is not B or J; therefore it is D. The author then points out several flaws: the categorical syllogism used is invalid because the subject and predicate of the conclusion do not correspond correctly, and if formulated differently, the premises are false or unproductive. Additionally, the universal conclusion 'every cause of origination is a body' does not convert to 'every body is a cause of origination'. The author concedes the argument may be used in dialectic (jadal) because dialectic aims for persuasion, not certainty. Then objections transmitted from 'the Shaykh' (likely Ibn Sina) are listed: (1) it is not accepted that every judgment has a cause; (2) the cause might be the subject's essence, not an attribute; (3) the confinement of causes to those mentioned is denied; (4) corporeality might not be the cause; (5) even if it were, the heaven may have impediments preventing origination; (6) the cause must be complete, which is not shown. The author concludes that further objections are from commonplaces and need not be mentioned.

تيقن
إقناع

C) Because dialectic accepts any argument regardless of validity.

Ranking Demonstration Over Dialectic: Priority by Importance vs Generality
Author defends the priority of demonstration over dialectic with a qualified concession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse and evaluate the author's argument for prioritizing the art of demonstration over dialectic, using the principles of importance and natural priority.

Core Idea
Certainty (burhan) outranks persuasion (jadal), but generality may justify a different pedagogical order.

In this passage, the author engages in a classical debate about the proper ordering of the logical arts. He argues that demonstration (burhan) should come first because it serves the highest goal: certainty. Yet he concedes that some place dialectic (jadal) before demonstration because its premises are more general—a principle of natural priority. This tension between importance and generality is a recurring theme in Islamic epistemology. By working through this passage, you will learn to spot and weigh such ordering principles in their own right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim about ordering
Reread the Arabic and locate where the author states that the First Teacher (Aristotle) likely placed the art of demonstration before all others because its purpose—attaining truth and certainty—is the highest. This is the central thesis.
2
Extract the two principles of priority
The author uses two different principles: (1) natural priority of the more general (a'amm), and (2) priority of the more important (ahamm). Note how each principle would lead to a different ordering.
3
Analyze the counter-argument for dialectic
Find where the author mentions that some place dialectic before demonstration, and why: because dialectical premises (common, inductive) are more general than demonstrative premises (sense, empirical, primary), and the more general is naturally prior.
This is not the author's own view; he presents it as an objection.
4
Evaluate the author's response
The author does not fully reject the counter-argument. He quotes the Master saying that such a person does not deserve complete disapproval, because starting with the more general can be beneficial. This shows a nuanced acceptance of pedagogical flexibility.
Note the use of the chain: First Teacher → Master → our author.
5
Apply the analysis to an Islamic science hierarchy
Think of how this reasoning might apply in usul al-fiqh: should one first study general principles (e.g., 'umum) or the most important proofs (e.g., qat'i texts)? Simulate a short debate with the same structure.

Chapter: On the Rank of the Book of Demonstration. The arts previously mentioned are mostly in a natural order of arrangement. So it is proper that the art dealing with simples should be placed before that dealing with composites, and it is proper that the art dealing with the first composite (the categorical statement) should be placed before that dealing with the syllogism. And it is proper that the art dealing with the absolute syllogism should be placed before dealing with specific syllogisms. And we may place some of these arts before others, but the more likely is that the First Teacher placed this art before all other arts, because the purpose in all that has preceded, and in the syllogism itself, is the attainment of truth and certainty; and this purpose is only provided by this art, not by the other arts. And it is more appropriate in everything that the more important be placed first, and this is the more important. For what the other arts provide are like matters that some are used for in common civic affairs without attaining particular perfections, some are used to guard against, some for practice, some to repel the opponent of truth, and some to be able to address the public in urging them towards benefits. All that, in comparison to what this art provides, is like an action compared to an obligation, or like the root compared to the branch. And the consideration of time for the root is like its consideration for the branch. But despite this, some have placed dialectic before this art; and that is disapproved of. And the Master used to say: 'That man does not deserve all disapproval,' for that is better in one respect, because the principles of dialectic are common premises and inductive premises, while the principles of demonstration are sense-percepts, empirical premises, and primary premises. The former are more general than the latter, for these premises are all common without reversal, and the more general is prior by nature, so it must be prior in position. And because beginning with the more general is beneficial with an evident benefit, strengthening...
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the proper ordering of the logical arts, specifically arguing that the Book of Demonstration (Posterior Analytics) should be placed before other works like Topics, Sophistics, etc. The author begins by stating a natural order: simple before composite, the art of the categorical statement before that of the syllogism, and the art of absolute syllogism before specific syllogisms. He then asserts that the First Teacher (Aristotle) likely placed this art first because its goal—attainment of truth and certainty—is the highest. Other arts serve lesser purposes: some for civic affairs, some for avoidance, practice, refutation of sophists, or addressing the public. These are like action to obligation, or root to branch. Despite this, some placed dialectic before demonstration, which is generally disapproved. However, the Master (possibly al-Farabi or Avicenna) says such a person does not deserve complete disapproval, because dialectical premises (common premises, inductive) are more general than demonstrative premises (sense-percepts, empirical, primary). The more general is naturally prior, so starting with dialectic has some pedagogical benefit. In Islamic sciences, this reasoning parallels how one might first study general principles of usul al-fiqh before delving into specific proofs, but the priority of demonstration remains for certainty.

Why Demonstration Precedes Dialectic: The Hierarchy of Logical Arts
Author argues for the precedence of demonstration over dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish demonstrative premises from dialectical premises and explain the logical reasoning behind the traditional ordering of the arts.

Core Idea
Demonstration yields certainty; dialectic provides generality and practice—both have their place in the logical curriculum.

This passage addresses a crucial question in Islamic logic: why is the art of demonstration (Burhan) given priority over dialectic (Jadal) and other arts? The author navigates both the natural order of simplicity and the practical benefits of starting with more general premises. You will learn to identify the different types of premises and understand the pedagogical wisdom behind the sequence of logical studies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arts being compared
Find the names of the arts: Burhan (demonstration) and Jadal (dialectic). Note their Greek origins and Islamic significance.
2
Extract the premises of each art
From the passage, list the types of premises for burhan (sense-perceptions, experientials, primaries) and for jadal (well-known and inductive premises).
These are the 'mabadi' (starting points) of each art.
3
Analyze the argument for precedence
The author gives two perspectives: one based on the ultimate purpose (certainty) and one based on generality. Trace how each supports either placing burhan first or jadal first.
The purpose perspective favors burhan; the generality perspective favors jadal.
4
Assess the compromise view
The Shaykh's view that placing dialectic first is not entirely objectionable because it trains the mind and its premises are more general. Evaluate this reasoning.
The author ultimately prefers demonstration but acknowledges dialectic's benefits.
5
Apply to Islamic sciences
Consider how this ordering applies in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where dialectical reasoning often precedes demonstrative proofs.
For example, in kalam, arguments for God's existence often start with well-known premises before moving to demonstrative ones.

فصل: في مرتبة كتاب البرهان إن الفنون التي سبق ذكرها أكثرها على نهج طبيعي من الترتيب فكان من حق الذي في البسائط أن يُقدم على المركبات، ومن حق الفن الذي في التركيب الأول الجازم أن يقدم على الذي في القياس. وكان من حق الفن الذي في القياس المطلق أن يقدم على القياسات الخاصة. ولنا أن نقدم بعض هذه الفنون على بعض لكن الأشبه أن يكون المعلم الأوّل قدم هذا الفن على سائر الفنون لأن الغرض في جميع ما سلف، وفي القياس نفسه هو التوصل إلى اكتساب الحق واليقين؛ وهذا الغرض إنما يفيد هذا الفن دون سائر الفنون والأولى في كل شيء أن يقدم الأهم؛ والأهم هذا. فإن ما يفيده سائر الفنون فكأنه من الأمور التي ينتفع ببعضها في الأحوال المدنية المشتركة دون استفادة الكمالات الخاصة، وبالبعض ليتحرّز منه، وبالبعض ليرتاض به، وبالبعض ليندفع به معاند الحق، وبالبعض ليقتدر به على مخاطبة الجمهور في حملهم على المصالح. وجميع ذلك بالنسبة إلى ما يفيده هذا الفن كالفعل بالنسبة إلى الفرض، أو كالأصل بالنسبة إلى الفرع. وحرف الزمان إلى الأصل مثل حرفه إلى الفرع لكن مع هذا فمنهم من قدم الجدل على هذا الفن؛ وذلك مستنكر منه. والشيخ كان يقول ليس يستحق ذلك الرجل كل الاستنكار فإن ذلك أحسن من وجه لما أن مبادئ الجدل المقدمات المشهورة والاستقرائية، ومبادئ البرهان المحسوسات والمجربات والأوليات؛ والأولى أعم من الثانية فإن هذه المقدّمات كلها مشهورة من غير عكس، والأعم أقدم طبعا فيجب أن يكون أقدم وضعًا. ولأن الابتداء بالأعم نافع نفعا ظاهرا مقوما كان الأعم أو ليس بمقوم. ثمَّ الذهن في تقديم الجدل يرتاض بالقياسات الجدلية؛ وذلك نافع في إدراك القياسات البرهانية؛ إذ الجدل قد يشارك البرهان في المبدأ ولكن الجدل نأخذه من حيث إنه مشهور لا من حيث إنه صادق. نعم تقديم البرهان من جهة حسن الاختيار والشفقة على الزور صار أحسن لكن ذلك من جهة اختيار حسن التدريج أحسن. وأما الخطابة والشعر فلا يكون بعينهما في الأمور الكلية، والمغالطة بالجدل في جواز تقديمهما على البرهان بالزمان لكن تقديمهما لا ينفع تقدم الجدل؛ إذ ليست مادتها مشاركة لمادة البرهان بوجه.
Section: On the rank of the book of demonstration. The arts previously mentioned are mostly in a natural order of arrangement: it is right that which deals with simples should be placed before compounds, and that the art dealing with the first assertive composition should be placed before that dealing with syllogism. And it is right that the art dealing with absolute syllogism should be placed before specific syllogisms. We have the liberty to place some of these arts before others, but the most plausible is that the first teacher placed this art before all other arts, because the purpose in all that preceded, and in the syllogism itself, is to attain acquisition of truth and certainty; and this purpose is only achieved by this art, not by the other arts. And the most important thing should be given priority; and this is the most important. For what the other arts provide are like matters that are useful in common civil affairs, not for acquiring special perfections; some are for self-protection, some for exercise, some to repel the opponent of truth, some to be able to address the public to lead them to beneficial ends. All that, relative to what this art provides, is like the action relative to the intention, or like the branch relative to the root. And the share of time to the root is like its share to the branch. But despite this, some have placed dialectic before this art; that is disapproved by him. The Shaykh used to say: that man does not deserve all that disapproval, because that is better in one respect: the premises of dialectic are the well-known and inductive premises, while the premises of demonstration are sense-perceptions, experientials, and primaries; the former are more general than the latter, for all these premises are well-known but not conversely. And what is more general is prior by nature, so it must be prior in position. And beginning with what is more general yields apparent benefit, whether it is constitutive or not. Moreover, the mind, by giving precedence to dialectic, becomes exercised in dialectical syllogisms; that is beneficial for grasping demonstrative syllogisms, for dialectic may share the same principle with demonstration, but we take dialectic as it is well-known, not as it is true. Yes, giving precedence to demonstration from the perspective of good choice and pity for falsehood becomes better, but that from the perspective of choosing good gradation is better. As for rhetoric and poetry, they do not deal with universals, and sophistry involves dialectic in terms of allowing them to precede demonstration temporally, but their precedence does not benefit from the precedence of dialectic, since their material does not share in the material of demonstration in any way.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a justification for the arrangement of the arts in the logical corpus, specifically arguing that the book on demonstration (Burhan) should be placed before other arts. The author begins by referencing a natural order: simples before compounds, first assertive composition before syllogism, absolute syllogism before specific ones. He then states that while one could order them differently, the most plausible is that the First Teacher (Aristotle) placed demonstration first because the ultimate aim of all previous arts is the acquisition of truth and certainty, and only demonstration achieves this. The other arts (dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, sophistics) serve various practical purposes—civil affairs, exercise, repelling opponents, addressing the public—but these are subordinate to the goal of certain knowledge, like a branch to the root. The author then addresses an objection: some place dialectic before demonstration. He quotes the Shaykh (likely Avicenna) who says this is not entirely objectionable because dialectical premises (well-known and inductive) are more general than demonstrative premises (sense-perceptions, experientials, primaries). What is more general is naturally prior, and starting with the general is beneficial. Additionally, practicing dialectical syllogisms trains the mind for demonstrative ones since they share principles, though dialectic is taken as well-known rather than true. However, giving precedence to demonstration is better from the perspective of good choice and pity for falsehood, but the gradation argument also holds. Finally, he notes that rhetoric and poetry do not deal with universals, and sophistry's temporal precedence does not benefit from dialectic's precedence because their material does not share in the material of demonstration.

برهان
جدل
مبادئ
أعم

D) Because dialectic deals with universals while demonstration does not.

Key Takeaway
The ordering of logical arts balances the ultimate goal of certainty with the pedagogical benefit of starting with more general, dialectical reasoning.
