Burhan Limmi and Burhan Inni Distinguished
Refining burhan definition and distinguishing two types with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish burhan limmi from burhan inni by determining whether the middle term gives the cause of the predicate's attribution.

Core Idea
Burhan limmi gives the why; burhan inni gives the that.

Here the author refines the definition of burhan (demonstration), settling the debate about whether 'certain' refers to premises or conclusion. Then he draws the crucial distinction between two types: one that reveals the cause (limmi) and one that only establishes the fact (inni). The medical example of white urine and delirium illustrates how burhan inni can be certain even without causal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refined definition of burhan
Locate where the author argues that certainty should apply to the premises, not the conclusion. Why does he say this?
This sets the foundation: burhan is defined as a syllogism of certain premises aiming at a certain conclusion.
2
Extract the criteria for burhan limmi
Find the condition that the middle term must be a cause for the existence of the predicate in the subject. This gives both the fact and the reason.
The term 'illa' (cause) is key.
3
Extract the criteria for burhan inni
Find the condition that the middle term proves the fact without being the cause. Note the author says the middle term could be a correlate, accident, or even an effect (sign).
Distinguish between absolute burhan inni (middle term not a cause or effect) and dalil (middle term is an effect).
4
Map the medical example to the syllogism
Identify the terms: major (delirium), minor (this fever patient), middle (white urine due to hot cause). Determine whether this is burhan limmi or inni, and why.
The middle term is not the cause but a sign; therefore it is burhan inni of the dalil subtype.

ثم في تفسير هذا القول أقوال مختلفة ونسبة أن لا يكون المراد من اليقيني أنه يقيني النتيجة فإنه إذا كان يقيني النتيجة فليس هو في نفسه يقينيا بل المراد منه ظاهرًا أنه قياس مؤلف من يقينيات. فاليقينية إذا كانت في المقدمات كان ذلك حال البرهان من جهة نفسه، وإذا كانت في النتيجة كان ذلك حاله بالقياس إلى غيره. وكونه يقيني المقدمات أمر له في ذاته فهو أولى أن يكون مأخوذا في حده ومعرفاً لطبيعته؛ فيعرف إذن بأنه قول مؤلف من مقدمات يقينية لإنتاج نتيجة يقينية. وهو ثم هذا القول [1] إن كان يعطي التصديق بوجود الأكبر للأصغر أو سلبه عنه، ويعطي العلة في وجود الأكبر للأصغر أيضًا حتى يكون الحد الأوسط فيه كما هو علة للتصديق بوجود هذا لذلك أو سلبه عنه، فهو برهان «لم»؛ وقد مرَّ من قبل أن مطلب «لم» بحسب القول هو الذي مطلب للحد الأوسط ر العلة لاعتقاد القول والتصديق. [ب] وإن كان يعطي التصديق بذلك وهو وجود الأكبر للأصغر أو سلبه عنه في البيان، ولا يعطي العلة في ذلك فهو برهان (إن)»؛ وقد يتفق فيه أن يكون الحد الأوسط في الوجود لا علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر ، ولا معلولة بل أمرًا مضايفًا أو مساويًا في النسبة إلى علة عارضًا معه أو غير ذلك مما هو يعد في الطبع معا. وقد يتفق أن يكون في الوجود معلولا لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر: [۱] فالأول يسمّى برهان الإن على الإطلاق، [٢] والثاني يسمى دليلا مثال ما يكون على الإطلاق إن هذا المحموم قد عرض له بول أبيض جائر في علية الحارة، وكل من يعرض له ذلك فقد جيف عليه الترسام؛ ثم ينتج أن هذا المحموم يجاف عليه الترسام. وأنت تعلم أن البول الأبيض والترسام معا معلولًا علة واحدة؛ وهي حركة الأخلاط الحارة إلى ناقبة الرأس
Then regarding the interpretation of this statement, there are various opinions. It is denied that what is meant by 'certain' is that it has a certain result, because if it has a certain result then it is not in itself certain. Rather what is apparently meant is that it is a syllogism composed of certain premises. So if certainty is in the premises, that is the state of the demonstration from its own aspect; if it is in the result, that is its state in relation to another. Its being certain in premises is a matter inherent in it, so it is more appropriate to be taken in its definition and as a description of its nature. Thus it is defined as a statement composed of certain premises to produce a certain result. Then this statement: if it gives assent to the existence of the greater for the lesser or its negation, and also gives the cause of the existence of the greater for the lesser, such that the middle term is as it is a cause for assent to the existence of this for that or its negation, then it is demonstration of 'why' (burhan limmi). It has passed before that the demand for 'why' according to the statement is that which demands the middle term as the cause for belief and assent. (b) If it gives assent to that, i.e., the existence of the greater for the lesser or its negation in the proof, and does not give the cause for that, then it is demonstration of 'that' (burhan inni). It may happen that the middle term in existence is not a cause for the existence of the greater in the lesser, nor an effect, but rather a correlative or equal in relation to a cause, an accident accompanying it, or something else considered together in nature. And it may happen that it is in existence an effect of the existence of the greater in the lesser: (1) the first is called demonstration of 'that' absolutely, (2) the second is called a sign (dalil). For example, absolutely: this fever patient has white urine due to a hot cause, and everyone who has that has been afflicted with delirium; then it follows that this fever patient is afflicted with delirium. And you know that white urine and delirium are both effects of one cause, which is the movement of hot humors to the meninges of the head.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage provides the definitive characterization of the two types of demonstration. First, the author refines the definition of burhan: there was debate whether 'certain' refers to the conclusion or the premises. He argues it should refer to the premises, since the certainty of the conclusion depends on them, and the essence of burhan lies in its premises being certain. So burhan is defined as a syllogism composed of certain premises aiming at a certain conclusion. Then he distinguishes the two types: If the middle term gives the cause (illa) for the predicate's attribution, it is burhan limmi (demonstration of why). If it only proves the fact without the cause, it is burhan inni (demonstration of that). He further subdivides burhan inni: sometimes the middle term is not a cause at all (e.g., a correlate), and sometimes it is an effect (a sign). The example given is from medicine: the presence of white urine (middle term) indicates yellow bile (the cause), but white urine and delirium are both effects of the same cause. This shows how a burhan inni can work without knowing the cause. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: the white urine serves as a sign (dalil) of delirium, not a cause.

برهان إن

C) Delirium.
D) The cause (hot humors).

Key Takeaway
Burhan limmi provides the cause of the predicate's attribution; burhan inni proves the fact without the cause, and may use a sign (dalil) that is an effect of the same cause.

Defining Burhan: The Role of Certain Premises
Author defines burhan and discusses locus of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristic of burhan and distinguish between intrinsic certainty (premises) and relational certainty (conclusion).

Core Idea
Burhan is defined by the certainty of its premises, not its conclusion.

In classical Islamic logic, the demonstrative syllogism (burhan) is the highest form of proof, yielding certain knowledge. But what exactly makes a proof demonstrative? Is it the certainty of the conclusion or the certainty of the premises? The author of this passage clarifies that the defining feature of burhan lies in its premises—they must be certain (yaqini). This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows why the definition focuses on premises rather than result.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of burhan in the text
Find where the author explicitly states the definition. The definition appears at the end: 'a statement composed of certain premises to produce a certain conclusion'.
The Arabic phrasing is 'قول مؤلف من مقدمات يقينية لإنتاج نتيجة يقينية'.
2
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition
Break down the definition: 'statement composed of premises' is the genus (syllogism), and 'certain' (يقينية) is the differentia that distinguishes burhan from other syllogisms.
In classical terms, the genus is 'قياس' (syllogism).
3
Analyze the author's argument about the locus of certainty
The author considers two possibilities: certainty in premises vs. certainty in conclusion. He argues that if certainty were in the conclusion, that would be relational (relative to the knower), whereas certainty in premises is intrinsic to the burhan itself. Therefore, the definition should refer to certain premises.
This is a philosophical distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic properties.
4
Apply the definition: Check whether a given syllogism qualifies as burhan
Given a syllogism with certain premises, it is a burhan. If the premises are only probable (zanni), it is not a burhan, even if the conclusion is by chance certain.
This is the litmus test for identifying demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الثاني فهو يقيني يمكن أن يكون غير دائم بل وقتا ما فالبرهان قياس مؤلف يقيني. ثم في تفسير هذا القول أقوال مختلفة ونسبة أن لا يكون المراد من اليقيني أنه يقيني النتيجة فإنه إذا كان يقيني النتيجة فليس هو في نفسه يقينيا بل المراد منه ظاهرًا أنه قياس مؤلف من يقينيات. فاليقينية إذا كانت في المقدمات كان ذلك حال البرهان من جهة نفسه، وإذا كانت في النتيجة كان ذلك حاله بالقياس إلى غيره. وكونه يقيني المقدمات أمر له في ذاته فهو أولى أن يكون مأخوذا في حده ومعرفاً لطبيعته؛ فيعرف إذن بأنه قول مؤلف من مقدمات يقينية لإنتاج نتيجة يقينية.
This second [type] is certain, though it may not be permanent but rather at some time. So burhan is a syllogism composed of certain [premises]. Then there are different interpretations of this statement, and it is likely that what is meant by 'certain' is not that the conclusion is certain, for if it is certain in conclusion, then it is not in itself certain; rather, the apparent meaning is that it is a syllogism composed of certainties. So if certainty is in the premises, then that is the state of burhan with respect to itself; and if it is in the conclusion, then that is its state relative to something else. And its being certain in premises is a matter intrinsic to it, so it is more appropriate to be taken in its definition and as a description of its nature. Therefore it is defined as a statement composed of certain premises to produce a certain conclusion.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage defines burhan (demonstration) and clarifies the locus of certainty. The author begins by stating that burhan is a syllogism composed of certain premises, though this certainty may be temporary. He then discusses different interpretations: some might think 'certain' refers to the conclusion, but he argues that if the conclusion is certain, that does not make the burhan itself certain—rather, certainty belongs primarily to the premises. He distinguishes between intrinsic certainty (in the premises) and relational certainty (in the conclusion). Since the premises are the foundation, the definition of burhan should highlight them. Thus burhan is formally defined as 'a statement composed of certain premises to produce a certain conclusion.' This aligns with the standard view in Islamic logic: the demonstrative syllogism is defined by the epistemic quality of its premises.

The Middle Term as Absolute vs. Relative Cause in Burhan Lim
Author examines whether the middle term in burhan lim is also a cause of the predicate in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the middle term being a cause for the predicate in itself versus being a cause for the predicate in a specific subject, using examples from burhan lim.

Core Idea
In burhan lim, the middle term is always a cause for the conclusion, but not always a cause for the predicate absolutely.

This lesson refines your understanding of causality in demonstration. The author argues that the middle term's causal role can be either absolute or relative. You will learn to identify which type is at play in a given demonstrative syllogism, a skill essential for evaluating the strength of proofs in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author asserts that the middle term in burhan lim is necessarily a cause for the existence of the predicate in the subject (the conclusion). Note this as the baseline.
2
Distinguish two cases
He then divides the causality into two possibilities: (1) the middle term is also a cause for the predicate in itself (absolute causality), or (2) it is not (relative causality). Understand the difference: absolute means the predicate depends on the middle term for its very existence; relative means only for this particular subject.
3
Analyze the first example
Consider the fever example: 'recurrent fever is caused by putrefaction of bile absolutely'. Here the middle term (bile putrefaction) is the absolute cause of the predicate (fever) because fever is defined by that cause.
4
Analyze the second example
In the syllogism 'Zayd is an animal because he is human', the middle term 'human' is a cause for Zayd's being animal, but 'human' is not the absolute cause of 'animal' (since animal is defined independently of human). Recognize this as relative causality.
5
Apply to other cases
The author mentions 'growing body' as another example. Trace the causality: 'growing body' is predicated of human, and human of Zayd, so 'animal' is a cause for 'human' being a growing body. This reinforces that the middle term's causal role can be relative to the subject hierarchy.
Verify the conclusion: 'human' alone is not the absolute cause of 'animal' universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن الحد الأوسط في هذا البرهان وهو برهان لم» علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر لا محالة: [١] فإما أن يكون مع ذلك علة لوجود الأكبر في نفسه، [۲] أو لا يكون علة له؛ فذاك أمر آخر. مثال الأول إن حمى الغُت معلولة عفونة الصفراء على الإطلاق ومعلولة لها أيضًا في وجودها للشخص المعين الصفراء، ومثال الثاني إن الحيوان محمول على زید بتوسط حمله على الإنسان؛ فالإنسان علة لوجود زيد حيوانا لأن الحيوان محمول أولا على الإنسان والإنسان محمول على زيد: فالحيوان محمول عليه كذلك. وعلى هذا في الغير فإن الجسم النامي مثلا محمول أولا ثم على الإنسان : فالحيوان وجوده للإنسان علة في وجود الإنسان جسما ناميًا. فأما على الإطلاق فليس الإنسان وحده علة لوجوده للحيوان على الإطلاق، ولا
If you know this, then we say that the middle term in this burhan—and it is burhan 'lim'—is necessarily a cause for the existence of the predicate in the subject: (1) either it is also a cause for the existence of the predicate in itself, (2) or it is not a cause for it; that is another matter. Example of the first: recurrent fever is caused by putrefaction of bile absolutely, and it is also caused by it in its existence for a particular person. Example of the second: 'animal' is predicated of Zayd by means of its being predicated of human; so 'human' is a cause for Zayd's being an animal, because 'animal' is predicated first of human, and 'human' is predicated of Zayd; thus 'animal' is predicated of him accordingly. And likewise in other cases: 'growing body', for example, is predicated first, then 'human'. So the existence of 'animal' for 'human' is a cause in the existence of 'human' as a growing body. But absolutely, 'human' alone is not a cause for its existence for 'animal' absolutely, nor...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage refines the understanding of causality in burhan lim. The author states that the middle term in burhan lim is necessarily a cause for the predicate in the subject (i.e., for the conclusion). However, this causality can be of two kinds: (1) the middle term is also a cause for the predicate in itself (absolute causality), or (2) it is only a cause for the predicate in this particular subject. The author gives two examples: First, 'recurrent fever is caused by putrefaction of bile absolutely'—here the middle term (bile putrefaction) is the absolute cause of the predicate (fever). Second, in the syllogism 'Zayd is an animal because he is human', the middle term 'human' is a cause for Zayd's being an animal, but 'human' is not the absolute cause of 'animal' (since animal is defined independently of human). Similarly, 'growing body' is predicated before 'human', and 'animal' is a cause for 'human' being a growing body. The author concludes that 'human' is not the absolute cause of 'animal' universally. This nuanced discussion shows that causality in demonstration can be relative to the subject matter.

Burhan 'Inn and Burhan 'Lim: Two Types of Demonstration
Author exemplifies and defines burhan 'inn and burhan 'lim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish burhan 'inn from burhan 'lim by identifying whether the middle term is an effect or a cause of the major term.

Core Idea
In burhan 'inn, the middle term is an effect; in burhan 'lim, it is a cause.

In this passage, the author draws a sharp distinction between two forms of syllogistic proof used in Islamic sciences: demonstration of existence (burhan 'inn) and demonstration of causation (burhan 'lim). Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples of burhan 'inn
Find the three examples the author provides for burhan 'inn: fever, moon, wood. For each, locate the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat).
In the fever example, the middle term is 'recurrent fever'; in the moon, 'receiving light in a certain way'; in the wood, 'being burnt'.
2
Identify the examples of burhan 'lim
Find the three examples for burhan 'lim: bile putrefaction, moon's contact with light, wood touched by fire. Note the middle term in each.
Middle terms: 'bile putrefaction', 'being such-and-such that receives light from the opposite side', 'being touched by fire'.
3
Determine the causal direction
For each example, decide whether the middle term is the cause ('illa) or the effect (ma'lul) of the major term's existence in the minor.
In burhan 'inn, the middle is the effect; in burhan 'lim, the middle is the cause.
4
Summarize the defining feature
Formulate a concise rule: burhan 'inn proves that something is (existence), while burhan 'lim proves why it is (cause).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واندفاعها نحوه وليس ولا واحد منهما بعلة ولا معلول للآخر. ومثال ما يسمى بالدليل هذا المحموم ينوب حماه غنا وكل من ناب حُمَّاه غتًا فحماه من عفونة الصفراء. أو تقول إن القمر يتشكل بأشكال مختلفة عند الاستنارة ثم تتراجع على تلك النسبة، وكل ما يقبل الضوء هكذا فهو كُروى. أو تقول هذه الخشبة محترقة، وكل محترق فقد مسته النار ; فهذه الخشبة قد مستها النار، فالأوسط في كل واحد منهما هو المعلول لوجود الأكبر للأصغر. وأما البرهان المطلق وهو برهان «لم»، فمثاله إن هذا الإنسان عفن[؟] فيه الصفراء لاحتقانها وانسداد المسام، وكل من عرض له هذا فهو محموم غتّا [النقرس] ماسة أو لازمة يستند في الثالث. أو تقول إن القمر كذا وكل كذا فإن استعاد به النور من المقابل يكون على شكل كذا وكذا يعني يكون هلاليا ثمَّ نِصْفَ قرص ثمَّ بدرًا : ثم تتراجع على تلك النسبة أو تقول إن هذه الخشبة قد مستها النار وكل خشبة قد مسَّتها النار فذاك أمر آخر. مثال الأول إن حمى الغُت معلولة عفونة الصفراء على الإطلاق ومعلولة لها أيضًا في وجودها للشخص المعين الصفراء، ومثال الثاني إن الحيوان محمول على زید بتوسط حمله على الإنسان؛ فالإنسان علة لوجود زيد حيوانا لأن الحيوان محمول أولا على الإنسان والإنسان محمول على زيد: فالحيوان محمول عليه كذلك. وعلى هذا في الغير فإن الجسم النامي مثلا محمول أولا ثم على الإنسان : فالحيوان وجوده للإنسان علة في وجود الإنسان جسما ناميًا. فأما على الإطلاق فليس الإنسان وحده علة لوجوده للحيوان على الإطلاق، ولا الحيوان وحده علة لوجود معنى الجسم النامي على الإطلاق. وأما حال فصل الجنس أنه يكون أو لا للنوع أو الجنس فنقول إن الجنس علة للنوع في حمل فصل الجنس عليه كما هو علة له في حمل جنس الجنس عليه؛ وإنه يعرف على التحقيق من بعد. والمعتبر في برهان (لم) علة كون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر لا لكونه علة للأكبر مطلقا بل ربَّما كان الأوسط معلولا للأكبر لكن يكون علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر، ولا يمتنع أن يكون معلول الشيء علة لحصول صفة ما لعلته. ألا ترى؟ أن حركة النار معلولة لطبيعتها ثم إنها أعني الحركة قد تصير علة لحصول طبيعتها عند الشيء الذي حصلت عنده ففعلت فيه. ولذلك فعلت المماسة التي هي معلولة حركتها للحد الأوسط دون نفس طبيعة النار. فإذن كل ما هو علة لوجود الأكبر مطلقا فهو علة لحصوله في كل موضوع. وفي الأصغر أيضًا؛ وليس كل ما هو علة لحصول الأكبر في الأصغر فهو علة له مطلقا، لا يقال / لو صدق قولنا كل ما هو علة لوجود الشيء فهو علة لوجوده في كل موضوع لاستحال أن يكون الأكبر علة للأوسط في برهان لم: فإنه إذا كان علة له كان علة لحصوله في الأصغر. وإذا كان الأكبر علة الحصول الأوسط في الأصغر استحال أن يكون حصول الأوسط للأصغر علة لحصول الأكبر فيه لامتناع أن يكون المعلول علة لعلته؛ لأنــا نقول نحن لا ندعي أن كل ما هو علة لوجود الشيء فهو لوجوده في كل موضوع بل ندعي ذلك النسبة إلى الموضوع الذي لا تتحقق ذات المعلول إلا وأن يكون في ذلك الموضوع. ولعل وجود الأوسط في الأصغر ليس بالنسبة إلى ذات الأوسط كذلك لجواز وجود ذات الأوسط في غير الأصغر. وإذا كان كذلك فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر علة للأوسط أن يكون علة لحصوله في الأصغر. أو نقول بل
And its repulsion toward it, and neither of them is a cause nor an effect of the other. An example of what is called a proof (dalil): this feverish person, his fever recurs every other day, and everyone whose fever recurs every other day has fever from bile putrefaction. Or you say: The moon takes different shapes when illuminated, then reverts to that proportion, and everything that receives light in this way is spherical. Or you say: This piece of wood is burnt, and everything burnt has been touched by fire; therefore this piece of wood has been touched by fire. So the middle term in each of these is the effect of the existence of the major in the minor. As for the absolute demonstration, which is the demonstration of 'why' (burhan 'lim), its example is: This human has bile putrefaction in him due to its congestion and blocked pores, and everyone who has this condition has a quartan fever... or you say: The moon is such-and-such, and everything such-and-such that receives light from the opposite side becomes shaped such-and-such, meaning it becomes crescent, then half-disc, then full moon, then reverts to that proportion. Or you say: This piece of wood has been touched by fire, and every piece of wood touched by fire is burnt; therefore this piece of wood is burnt. If you understand this, we say: The middle term in this demonstration, which is the demonstration of 'why', is necessarily the cause of the existence of the major in the minor. It is either also the cause of the existence of the major in itself, or it is not the cause of it; that is another matter. An example of the first: Quartan fever is caused by bile putrefaction absolutely, and it is also caused by it in its existence for the specific person of bile. An example of the second: Animal is predicated of Zayd by mediation of its predication of human; so human is the cause of Zayd's being animal, because animal is predicated first of human and human is predicated of Zayd; thus animal is predicated of him likewise. And similarly for others: The growing body, for example, is predicated first then of human; so animal's existence for human is a cause of human's being a growing body. As for absolutely, human alone is not a cause of its existence for animal absolutely, nor is animal alone a cause of the existence of the meaning of growing body absolutely. As for the case of the differentia of the genus, whether it is for the species or the genus, we say: The genus is a cause for the species in predicating its differentia of it, just as it is a cause for it in predicating the genus of its genus; and that will be known upon investigation later. What matters in the demonstration of 'why' is that the middle term is a cause of the existence of the major in the minor, not that it is a cause of the major absolutely; rather, sometimes the middle term is an effect of the major but is a cause of the existence of the major in the minor. It is not impossible for an effect of a thing to be a cause of the occurrence of some attribute of its cause. Do you not see? The motion of fire is an effect of its nature, then it – I mean the motion – may become a cause of the occurrence of its nature in the thing where it occurs and thus acts in it. Therefore, the contact, which is an effect of its motion, serves as the middle term, not the nature of fire itself. So every thing that is a cause of the existence of the major absolutely is a cause of its occurrence in every subject, and also in the minor; but not every thing that is a cause of the occurrence of the major in the minor is a cause of it absolutely. It cannot be said: If our statement 'every thing that is a cause of the existence of a thing is a cause of its existence in every subject' were true, it would be impossible for the major to be a cause of the middle in the demonstration of 'why'; for if it were a cause of it, it would be a cause of its occurrence in the minor. And if the major is the cause of the occurrence of the middle in the minor, it would be impossible for the occurrence of the middle in the minor to be a cause of the occurrence of the major in it, because it is impossible for an effect to be a cause of its cause. We say: We do not claim that every thing that is a cause of the existence of a thing is its cause of existence in every subject; rather, we claim that relation to the subject without which the essence of the effect is not realized except while it is in that subject. But perhaps the existence of the middle in the minor is not relative to the essence of the middle in that way, because it is possible for the essence of the middle to exist in something other than the minor. And if that is so, it does not follow from the major being a cause of the middle that it is a cause of its occurrence in the minor. Or we say: Rather...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of the nature of demonstration (burhan) in Aristotelian logic as adopted by Islamic logicians. The author distinguishes between two types of proof: the proof of existence (burhan 'inn or dalil) and the proof of causation (burhan 'lim or burhan mutlaq). The author first provides examples of burhan 'inn: in the fever example, the middle term (recurrent fever) is an effect (ma'lul) of the existence of the major (fever from bile) in the minor (this person). Similarly, the moon's spherical shape is inferred from its light reception. In burhan 'lim, the middle term is a cause ('illa) of the major's existence in the minor. The examples given are: the human has bile putrefaction (middle) causing fever (major); the moon's contact with light (middle) causes its phases (major); fire's contact (middle) causes wood to burn (major). The author then clarifies that the middle term in burhan 'lim is necessarily a cause of the major's existence in the minor, but it may or may not be the absolute cause of the major itself. For instance, human is a cause of Zayd being animal, but not an absolute cause of animality. The author further discusses the relationship of genus and differentia, stating that the genus is a cause for the species in predicating its differentia. The crucial point is that in burhan 'lim, what matters is that the middle term causes the major's presence in the subject, not that it causes the major absolutely. The author anticipates an objection: if the middle term were an effect of the major, how could it be a cause? The response is that an effect can become a cause for its cause in a specific context, like fire's motion causing contact, which then causes fire's nature to affect another body. The passage concludes with a rebuttal: not every absolute cause causes in every subject, and the middle term's existence in the subject is not essential to it, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
برهان إن
برهان لم
الحد الأوسط
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a burhan 'lim, what is the role of the middle term with respect to the major term?
A) It is an effect of the major term's existence.
B) It is the cause of the major term's existence in the minor.
C) It has no causal relation to the major term.
D) It is the subject of the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Burhan 'inn uses the middle term as an effect to prove existence, while burhan 'lim uses the middle term as a cause to explain why.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following syllogisms is burhan 'inn or burhan 'lim: (1) This nabidh is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore this nabidh is forbidden. (2) Every intoxicant is forbidden because it causes intoxication; this nabidh causes intoxication; therefore this nabidh is forbidden. Explain your reasoning.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
10 min
Middle Term: Cause but Not Absolute Cause
Author clarifies the limited causal role of the middle term in burhan 'lim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the middle term in burhan 'lim may not be the absolute cause of the major term, using the example of human causing animal in Zayd.

Core Idea
The middle term causes the major's existence in the minor, not necessarily its absolute existence.

The author further refines the concept of causation in burhan 'lim. Although the middle term is a cause of the major's predication in the subject, it is not always the cause of the major term itself. The example of 'human' causing 'animal' in Zayd illustrates this subtle but crucial point.

Guided Path
1
Extract the example of human and Zayd
The author says: 'Animal is predicated of Zayd by mediation of its predication of human; so human is the cause of Zayd's being animal.' Write out the syllogism.
Premises: All humans are animals. Zayd is human. Conclusion: Zayd is animal.
2
Identify the middle term and the major
Here, the middle term is 'human', the major is 'animal', and the minor is 'Zayd'. The middle term 'human' causes 'animal' to be predicated of Zayd.
3
Realize the limitation
Is 'human' the absolute cause of 'animal'? No, because animal exists in other species like horse, bird, etc. 'Human' is only a cause of 'animal' being in Zayd, not of animal itself.
The author states: 'As for absolutely, human alone is not a cause of its existence for animal absolutely.'
4
Apply the distinction to genus and differentia
The author mentions that the genus is a cause for the species in predicating its differentia. Understand how this extends the same principle: the genus (e.g., animal) is a cause for the species (human) having its differentia (rational).
This is a more advanced application showing the causal role in definition.

فنقول إن الجنس علة للنوع في حمل فصل الجنس عليه كما هو علة له في حمل جنس الجنس عليه؛ وإنه يعرف على التحقيق من بعد. والمعتبر في برهان (لم) علة كون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر لا لكونه علة للأكبر According to the passage, which of the following is true about the middle term in burhan 'lim?
A) It is always the absolute cause of the major term.

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
12 min
When an Effect Becomes a Cause: The Fire Example
Author resolves the objection that an effect cannot be a cause of its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the apparent paradox that an effect can serve as a cause of its cause in a specific context, using the fire example from the passage.

Core Idea
An effect can be a cause of its cause's action in a particular subject.

The author addresses a potential objection: if the middle term in burhan 'lim is a cause of the major in the minor, but the major itself might be the cause of the middle term, how is this possible? The answer lies in the dynamic interplay of cause and effect, illustrated with the motion of fire and its nature.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector says: If the major is the cause of the middle term, then the middle cannot be a cause of the major in any respect, because an effect cannot cause its cause.
This objection challenges the coherence of burhan 'lim.
2
Analyze the fire example
The author gives the example: Fire's nature causes motion; motion causes contact; contact causes fire's nature to act on another body. Here, motion is an effect of nature, but motion (via contact) becomes a cause of nature's action in the wood.
Thus, the middle term 'contact' is both an effect (of motion) and a cause (of burning in the wood).
3
Identify the logical structure
In the burhan 'lim syllogism: 'This wood is touched by fire; everything touched by fire is burnt; therefore this wood is burnt.' The middle term 'touched by fire' is an effect of fire's nature, but it is the cause of 'being burnt' in the wood.
The middle term's causality is limited to this subject.
4
Generalize the principle
Understand that an effect can be a cause of its cause's attribute in a specific substrate, without violating causal hierarchy.
The author's rebuttal: the effect's existence in the subject is not essential to its own essence, so the objection fails.

للأصغر. وأما البرهان المطلق وهو برهان «لم»، فمثاله إن هذا الإنسان عفن[؟] فيه الصفراء لاحتقانها وانسداد المسام، وكل من عرض له هذا فهو محموم غتّا [النقرس] ماسة أو لازمة يستند في الثالث. أو تقول إن القمر كذا وكل كذا فإن استعاد به النور من المقابل يكون على شكل كذا وكذا يعني يكون هلاليا ثمَّ نِصْفَ قرص ثمَّ بدرًا : ثم تتراجع على تلك النسبة أو تقول إن هذه الخشبة قد مستها النار وكل خشبة قد مسَّتها النار فهي محترقة؛ فهذه الخشبة إذن محترقة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن الحد الأوسط في هذا البرهان وهو برهان لم» علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر لا محالة: فإما أن يكون مع ذلك علة لوجود الأكبر في نفسه، أو لا يكون علة له؛ فذاك أمر آخر. مثال الأول إن حمى الغُت معلولة عفونة الصفراء على الإطلاق ومعلولة لها أيضًا في وجودها للشخص المعين الصفراء، ومثال الثاني إن الحيوان محمول على زید بتوسط حمله على الإنسان؛ فالإنسان علة لوجود زيد حيوانا لأن الحيوان محمول أولا على الإنسان والإنسان محمول على زيد: فالحيوان محمول عليه كذلك. وعلى هذا في الغير فإن الجسم النامي مثلا محمول أولا ثم على الإنسان : فالحيوان وجوده للإنسان علة في وجود الإنسان جسما ناميًا. فأما على الإطلاق فليس الإنسان وحده علة لوجوده للحيوان على الإطلاق، ولا الحيوان وحده علة لوجود معنى الجسم النامي على الإطلاق. وأما حال فصل الجنس أنه يكون أو لا للنوع أو الجنس فنقول إن الجنس علة للنوع في حمل فصل الجنس عليه كما هو علة له في حمل جنس الجنس عليه؛ وإنه يعرف على التحقيق من بعد. والمعتبر في برهان (لم) علة كون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر لا لكونه علة للأكبر مطلقا بل ربَّما كان الأوسط معلولا للأكبر لكن يكون علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر، ولا يمتنع أن يكون معلول الشيء علة لحصول صفة ما لعلته. ألا ترى؟ أن حركة النار معلولة لطبيعتها ثم إنها أعني الحركة قد تصير علة لحصول طبيعتها عند الشيء الذي حصلت عنده ففعلت فيه. ولذلك فعلت المماسة التي هي معلولة حركتها للحد الأوسط دون نفس طبيعة النار. فإذن كل ما هو علة لوجود الأكبر مطلقا فهو علة لحصوله في كل موضوع. وفي الأصغر أيضًا؛ وليس كل ما هو علة لحصول الأكبر في الأصغر فهو علة له مطلقا، لا يقال / لو صدق قولنا كل ما هو علة لوجود الشيء فهو علة لوجوده في كل موضوع لاستحال أن يكون الأكبر علة للأوسط في برهان لم: فإنه إذا كان علة له كان علة لحصوله في الأصغر. وإذا كان الأكبر علة الحصول الأوسط في الأصغر استحال أن يكون حصول الأوسط للأصغر علة لحصول الأكبر فيه لامتناع أن يكون المعلول علة لعلته؛ لأنــا نقول نحن لا ندعي أن كل ما هو علة لوجود الشيء فهو لوجوده في كل موضوع بل ندعي ذلك النسبة إلى الموضوع الذي لا تتحقق ذات المعلول إلا وأن يكون في ذلك الموضوع. ولعل وجود الأوسط في الأصغر ليس بالنسبة إلى ذات الأوسط كذلك لجواز وجود ذات الأوسط في غير الأصغر. وإذا كان كذلك فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر علة للأوسط أن يكون علة لحصوله في الأصغر. أو نقول بل
And its repulsion toward it, and neither of them is a cause nor an effect of the other. An example of what is called a proof (dalil): this feverish person, his fever recurs every other day, and everyone whose fever recurs every other day has fever from bile putrefaction. Or you say: The moon takes different shapes when illuminated, then reverts to that proportion, and everything that receives light in this way is spherical. Or you say: This piece of wood is burnt, and everything burnt has been touched by fire; therefore this piece of wood has been touched by fire. So the middle term in each of these is the effect of the existence of the major in the minor. As for the absolute demonstration, which is the demonstration of 'why' (burhan 'lim), its example is: This human has bile putrefaction in him due to its congestion and blocked pores, and everyone who has this condition has a quartan fever... or you say: The moon is such-and-such, and everything such-and-such that receives light from the opposite side becomes shaped such-and-such, meaning it becomes crescent, then half-disc, then full moon, then reverts to that proportion. Or you say: This piece of wood has been touched by fire, and every piece of wood touched by fire is burnt; therefore this piece of wood is burnt. If you understand this, we say: The middle term in this demonstration, which is the demonstration of 'why', is necessarily the cause of the existence of the major in the minor. It is either also the cause of the existence of the major in itself, or it is not the cause of it; that is another matter. An example of the first: Quartan fever is caused by bile putrefaction absolutely, and it is also caused by it in its existence for the specific person of bile. An example of the second: Animal is predicated of Zayd by mediation of its predication of human; so human is the cause of Zayd's being animal, because animal is predicated first of human and human is predicated of Zayd; thus animal is predicated of him likewise. And similarly for others: The growing body, for example, is predicated first then of human; so animal's existence for human is a cause of human's being a growing body. As for absolutely, human alone is not a cause of its existence for animal absolutely, nor is animal alone a cause of the existence of the meaning of growing body absolutely. As for the case of the differentia of the genus, whether it is for the species or the genus, we say: The genus is a cause for the species in predicating its differentia of it, just as it is a cause for it in predicating the genus of its genus; and that will be known upon investigation later. What matters in the demonstration of 'why' is that the middle term is a cause of the existence of the major in the minor, not that it is a cause of the major absolutely; rather, sometimes the middle term is an effect of the major but is a cause of the existence of the major in the minor. It is not impossible for an effect of a thing to be a cause of the occurrence of some attribute of its cause. Do you not see? The motion of fire is an effect of its nature, then it – I mean the motion – may become a cause of the occurrence of its nature in the thing where it occurs and thus acts in it. Therefore, the contact, which is an effect of its motion, serves as the middle term, not the nature of fire itself. So every thing that is a cause of the existence of the major absolutely is a cause of its occurrence in every subject, and also in the minor; but not every thing that is a cause of the occurrence of the major in the minor is a cause of it absolutely. It cannot be said: If our statement 'every thing that is a cause of the existence of a thing is a cause of its existence in every subject' were true, it would be impossible for the major to be a cause of the middle in the demonstration of 'why'; for if it were a cause of it, it would be a cause of its occurrence in the minor. And if the major is the cause of the occurrence of the middle in the minor, it would be impossible for the occurrence of the middle in the minor to be a cause of the occurrence of the major in it, because it is impossible for an effect to be a cause of its cause. We say: We do not claim that every thing that is a cause of the existence of a thing is its cause of existence in every subject; rather, we claim that relation to the subject without which the essence of the effect is not realized except while it is in that subject. But perhaps the existence of the middle in the minor is not relative to the essence of the middle in that way, because it is possible for the essence of the middle to exist in something other than the minor. And if that is so, it does not follow from the major being a cause of the middle that it is a cause of its occurrence in the minor. Or we say: Rather...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of the nature of demonstration (burhan) in Aristotelian logic as adopted by Islamic logicians. The author distinguishes between two types of proof: the proof of existence (burhan 'inn or dalil) and the proof of causation (burhan 'lim or burhan mutlaq). The author first provides examples of burhan 'inn: in the fever example, the middle term (recurrent fever) is an effect (ma'lul) of the existence of the major (fever from bile) in the minor (this person). Similarly, the moon's spherical shape is inferred from its light reception. In burhan 'lim, the middle term is a cause ('illa) of the major's existence in the minor. The examples given are: the human has bile putrefaction (middle) causing fever (major); the moon's contact with light (middle) causes its phases (major); fire's contact (middle) causes wood to burn (major). The author then clarifies that the middle term in burhan 'lim is necessarily a cause of the major's existence in the minor, but it may or may not be the absolute cause of the major itself. For instance, human is a cause of Zayd being animal, but not an absolute cause of animality. The author further discusses the relationship of genus and differentia, stating that the genus is a cause for the species in predicating its differentia. The crucial point is that in burhan 'lim, what matters is that the middle term causes the major's presence in the subject, not that it causes the major absolutely. The author anticipates an objection: if the middle term were an effect of the major, how could it be a cause? The response is that an effect can become a cause for its cause in a specific context, like fire's motion causing contact, which then causes fire's nature to affect another body. The passage concludes with a rebuttal: not every absolute cause causes in every subject, and the middle term's existence in the subject is not essential to it, so the objection fails.

معلول
برهان لم
الحد الأوسط

A) Because the middle term is the absolute cause of the major.
B) Because the existence of the middle in the minor is not essential to the middle's own essence.
C) Because the major is not actually a cause of the middle.
D) Because the fire example is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
An effect of a thing can be a cause of that thing's attribute in a specific subject, resolving the apparent contradiction in burhan 'lim.

Causality in Burhan Limmi: The Middle Term as Cause
Author explains causality conditions for the middle term in demonstration of why.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute causality from subject-relative causality in burhan limmi and identify the middle term's causal role in a demonstration of why.

Core Idea
In burhan limmi, the middle term causes the major's existence in the minor, not the major absolutely.

This lesson unpacks a critical distinction in the science of demonstration: absolute causality versus causality relative to a subject. The author uses the example of fire to show how an effect can become a cause for its own cause's attribute without circularity. You will learn to dissect this compound argument and apply it to Islamic theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim
Locate the sentence: 'والمعتبر في برهان (لم) علة كون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر لا لكونه علة للأكبر مطلقا'. This tells you that the middle term is a cause for the existence of the major in the minor, not an absolute cause of the major.
The Arabic explicitly contrasts 'علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر' with 'علة للأكبر مطلقا'.
2
Analyze the Fire Example
Read the example: motion of fire is an effect of its nature, yet that motion becomes a cause for obtaining the nature in another object. Here the middle term is motion, which is effect of nature but cause for nature's presence elsewhere. Notice the reversal: the effect serves as a cause for an attribute of its cause.
The contact (المماسة) is the middle term, not the nature of fire itself.
3
Understand the Objection
The objection argues: if the major (the nature) is a cause for the middle (motion) in the minor, then the middle cannot be a cause for the major's existence in the minor, because that would make the effect cause of its cause. This is the circularity concern.
The objection assumes absolute causality: if A causes B, then B cannot cause A in any respect.
4
Follow the Author's Response
The author distinguishes: the major causes the middle's essence in the minor (ذات الأوسط), while the middle causes not the major's essence but its existence in the minor (كونه موجودا في الأصغر). Thus the middle is cause of an attribute of its cause, not the cause itself. This avoids circularity.
The key is the distinction between essence and accidental attribute (صفة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحيوان وحده علة لوجود معنى الجسم النامي على الإطلاق. وأما حال فصل الجنس أنه يكون أو لا للنوع أو الجنس فنقول إن الجنس علة للنوع في حمل فصل الجنس عليه كما هو علة له في حمل جنس الجنس عليه؛ وإنه يعرف على التحقيق من بعد. والمعتبر في برهان (لم) علة كون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر لا لكونه علة للأكبر مطلقا بل ربَّما كان الأوسط معلولا للأكبر لكن يكون علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر، ولا يمتنع أن يكون معلول الشيء علة لحصول صفة ما لعلته. ألا ترى؟ أن حركة النار معلولة لطبيعتها ثم إنها أعني الحركة قد تصير علة لحصول طبيعتها عند الشيء الذي حصلت عنده ففعلت فيه. ولذلك فعلت المماسة التي هي معلولة حركتها للحد الأوسط دون نفس طبيعة النار. فإذن كل ما هو علة لوجود الأكبر مطلقا فهو علة لحصوله في كل موضوع. وفي الأصغر أيضًا؛ وليس كل ما هو علة لحصول الأكبر في الأصغر فهو علة له مطلقا، لا يقال / لو صدق قولنا كل ما هو علة لوجود الشيء فهو علة [٢٧٩] لوجوده في كل موضوع لاستحال أن يكون الأكبر علة للأوسط في برهان لم: فإنه إذا كان علة له كان علة لحصوله في الأصغر. وإذا كان الأكبر علة الحصول الأوسط في الأصغر استحال أن يكون حصول الأوسط للأصغر علة لحصول الأكبر فيه لامتناع أن يكون المعلول علة لعلته؛ لأنــا نقول نحن لا ندعي أن كل ما هو علة لوجود الشيء فهو لوجوده في كل موضوع بل ندعي ذلك النسبة إلى الموضوع الذي لا تتحقق ذات المعلول إلا وأن يكون في ذلك الموضوع. ولعل وجود الأوسط في الأصغر ليس بالنسبة إلى ذات الأوسط كذلك لجواز وجود ذات الأوسط في غير الأصغر. وإذا كان كذلك فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر علة للأوسط أن يكون علة لحصوله في الأصغر. أو نقول بل ندعي ذلك بالنسبة إلى الموضوع الذي لا تتحقق ذات العلة إلا وأن يكون في ذلك الموضوع. ولعل وجود الأكبر في الأصغر ليس بالنسبة إلى ذات الأكبر كذلك لجواز وجوده في غير الأصغر. ولو كان كذلك فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر علة للأوسط كونه علة لحصوله في الأصغر. وإن سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم أن الأكبر لو كان علة لحصول الأوسط في الأصغر يلزم أن لا يكون حصول الأوسط في الأصغر علة لحصول الأكبر فيه، فإن الأكبر له ذات وله كونه موجودا في الأصغر، وأحدهما غير الآخر. وعلة حصول الأوسط في الأصغر هي ذات الأكبر ومعلول حصول الأوسط في الأصغر كونه موجودا في الأصغر. ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون المعلول علة لعلته بل يلزم أن يكون علة لصفة من صفات علته، وذلك ممكن لما مر .
Animal alone is a cause for the existence of the meaning of a growing body absolutely. As for the state of the differentia of the genus, whether it belongs to the species or the genus, we say: the genus is a cause for the species in the attribution of the differentia of the genus upon it, just as it is a cause for it in the attribution of the genus of the genus upon it; and this is known in detail later. And what is considered in the demonstration of why (burhan limmi) is that the middle term is a cause for the existence of the major in the minor, not absolutely that it is a cause for the major absolutely, but sometimes the middle term is an effect of the major, yet it is a cause for the existence of the major in the minor. And it is not impossible that the effect of a thing be a cause for obtaining some attribute of its cause. Do you not see that the motion of fire is an effect of its nature, then that motion becomes a cause for obtaining its nature in the thing at which it arrives and thus acts upon it. Therefore, the contact which is an effect of its motion acts as the middle term, not the nature of fire itself. So then, everything that is a cause for the existence of the major absolutely is a cause for its existence in every subject, and also in the minor. But not everything that is a cause for the existence of the major in the minor is a cause for it absolutely. It cannot be said: if our statement 'everything that is a cause for the existence of a thing is a cause for its existence in every subject' were true, then it would be impossible that the major be a cause for the middle in burhan limmi, for if it were a cause for it, it would be a cause for its existence in the minor. And if the major were a cause for the existence of the middle in the minor, then it would be impossible for the existence of the middle for the minor to be a cause for the existence of the major in it, because it is impossible that the effect be a cause for its cause. But we say: we do not claim that everything that is a cause for the existence of a thing is [a cause] for its existence in every subject; rather, we claim that with respect to the subject without which the essence of the effect cannot be realized except by being in that subject. Perhaps the existence of the middle in the minor is not with respect to the essence of the middle thus, because it is possible for the essence of the middle to exist in something other than the minor. If that is so, then it does not follow from the major being a cause for the middle that it be a cause for its existence in the minor. Or we say: rather, we claim that with respect to the subject without which the essence of the cause cannot be realized except by being in that subject. Perhaps the existence of the major in the minor is not with respect to the essence of the major thus, because it is possible for it to exist in something other than the minor. If that were so, then it does not follow from the major being a cause for the middle that it be a cause for its existence in the minor. Even if we concede that, we do not concede that if the major were a cause for the existence of the middle in the minor, then it would follow that the existence of the middle in the minor cannot be a cause for the existence of the major in it, for the major has an essence and it has its being existent in the minor, and one of them is other than the other. The cause of the existence of the middle in the minor is the essence of the major, and the effect of the existence of the middle in the minor is its being existent in the minor. From that it does not follow that the effect is a cause for its cause; rather, it follows that it is a cause for an attribute of its cause, and that is possible as has passed.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical explanation of the role of causality in the demonstration of why (burhan limmi). The author first states a preliminary: animal is the cause for the existence of the meaning of growing body absolutely. Then he introduces the main topic: in burhan limmi, the middle term is cause for the existence of the major in the minor, not for the major absolutely. He gives the example of fire: the motion of fire is caused by its nature, but then the motion becomes a cause for the nature's existence in another object; thus the middle term is the motion, not the nature itself. Next, he considers an objection: if the middle term is cause for the major's existence in the minor, it might be thought that the major cannot be cause for the middle, because then the middle would be both cause and effect. The author responds by distinguishing: the middle is cause for the major's existence in the minor, not for the major absolutely; the major can be cause for the middle's existence in the minor without circularity. The key distinction is between absolute causality and causality relative to a subject. The major, as an essence, can be cause for the middle's existence in the minor, while the middle is cause for the major's existence in the minor as an attribute. This avoids the impossibility of the effect being cause of its cause, since the effect is only cause of an attribute of its cause, not of the cause itself.

In burhan limmi, the middle term's causality is relative to the minor subject: it causes the major's existence in that subject, not the major absolutely.

Certain Knowledge as Knowledge from the Cause
Author defines certain knowledge as knowledge from the side of the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish certain knowledge from opinion by identifying whether a judgment is known from its cause or from another aspect.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge requires knowing the cause, not just the fact.

In this passage, the author defines certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini) as knowledge that comes from the side of the cause (min jihati sababihi). He explains that without knowing the cause, the relation between subject and predicate is merely possible, not necessary. This distinction is crucial for understanding what makes knowledge certain versus merely opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. It is 'علم يقيني' (certain knowledge). The author states that everything that has a cause is known from its cause.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'knowledge' (علم). The differentia is 'from the side of the cause' (من جهة سببه). This gives a real definition: certain knowledge is knowledge that is derived from its proper cause.
3
Understand necessity vs. possibility
The author contrasts two modes: if a relation is known from its cause, it is necessary (وجوب); if known from another aspect, it is merely possible (إمكان). Without the cause, the relation is not necessary.
Note: The Arabic term 'جهة' (aspect) refers to the manner of knowing.
4
Apply the definition to an example
Consider 'Every human is a laugher' and 'Every laugher is a speaker'. If you know these only because you have observed humans, you do not have certain knowledge. But if you know them because of the essential nature (e.g., 'human' as a rational animal), then the judgment is necessary.
5
Test the definition's completeness
Check whether the definition includes all instances of certain knowledge and excludes opinion. According to the author, any judgment not known from its cause is merely possible, thus not certain.

فصل: في أن العلم اليقيني فكل ما له سبب من جهة سببه نقول: إذا حمل محمول على موضوع دائمًا أو سلب عنه دائما فلحمله أو السلبه في وقت بعينه علة لتلك العلة صارت النسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول تلك النسبة. وذات المحمول والموضوع ليس لهما لولا تلك العلة تلك النسبة بالوجوب بل بالإمكان. وإذا علم هذا وذلك من غير الجهة التي بها صار الحكم بينهما ضروريًا على تلك النسبة فقد علم من جهة غير الجهة التي بها لا يمكن أن يكونا لتلك الحال؛ وذلك هو أن يعلم الحكم بجهة غير جهة السبب الذي يوجبه لأن كل نسبة للموضوع إلى المحمول وللمحمول إلى الموضوع تفرض واقعة لا من الجهة التي توجبها العلة فهي واقعة من جهة إمكان لا وجوب، فيكون قد علم أن كذا كذا ولا يعلم أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا. فلو قال قائل إن كل إنسان ضحاك وكل ضحاك ناطق
Section: On that certain knowledge: everything that has a cause is known from the side of its cause. We say: When a predicate is predicated of a subject always or negated of it always, then its affirmation or its negation at a specific time has a cause for that cause, the relation between subject and predicate becomes that relation. And the essence of the predicate and the subject, without that cause, do not have that relation by necessity but by possibility. And if one knows this and that from a different aspect than that by which the judgment became necessary regarding that relation, then he has known from an aspect other than the aspect by which it is impossible for them to be in that state; that is to know the judgment by an aspect other than the aspect of the cause that necessitates it, because every relation of subject to predicate and of predicate to subject that is supposed to occur not from the aspect that necessitates it by the cause, then it occurs from an aspect of possibility, not necessity. Thus he knows that such and such, but he does not know that it cannot not be such. So if someone says: 'Every human is a laugher, and every laugher is a speaker' ...
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini). The author states that certain knowledge is knowledge of something from the side of its cause (min jihati sababihi). He then explains: when a predicate is always affirmed or negated of a subject, the specific instance of that affirmation or negation has a cause. Without that cause, the relation between subject and predicate would be possible, not necessary. If one knows this relation from a different aspect than the one that makes the judgment necessary, then he has not known it with certainty; he only knows that it is the case, but not that it must be the case. The example given is: 'Every human is a laugher, every laugher is a speaker' — but the argument is cut off. The point is that knowing these propositions from a cause (e.g., because of the essence) yields certainty; knowing them otherwise yields only opinion.

جهة
وجوب
إمكان

D) From possibility

Key Takeaway
Certain knowledge is not just truth; it is truth known from its cause, making the opposite impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the proposition 'The universe is created.' According to the author's definition, what makes this certain knowledge? Identify the cause from which one knows this, and contrast it with knowing it from mere tradition or assumption.
?
2. Take the statement 'Zakat is obligatory.' In Islamic jurisprudence, this is known from the Qur'an and Sunnah. Explain how knowing it from its revealed cause (the textual evidence) yields certainty, whereas knowing it from cultural practice would yield only possibility.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
10 min
Certain Knowledge Requires Knowledge Through the Cause
Author explains that certain knowledge must come from the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a syllogism yields certain knowledge by checking if the middle term is the cause of the conclusion.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge of a proposition comes only through its cause.

This lesson examines the author's key epistemological claim: genuine certainty (yaqīn) is only achieved when the cause of the fact is known. We will analyze two syllogisms—one where the middle term is the cause and one where it is an effect—to see why only the former yields certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two syllogisms
Find the two examples in the passage: one about 'man, laughing, rational' and another about 'man, rational, laughing'. The author argues that the latter yields certainty, while the former does not.
2
Determine the causal relation
In the second syllogism (man → rational → laughing), rationality is the cause of laughter. In the first, laughter is an effect of rationality.
3
Evaluate certainty
The syllogism with the cause as middle term gives certainty because the connection is necessary. The one with the effect gives only probability or knowledge by accident.
4
Apply the principle
Generalize: For any fact that has a cause, certain knowledge of it must come through that cause. If the middle term is not the cause, the knowledge is not certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في أن العلم اليقيني فكل ما له سبب من جهة سببه نقول: إذا حمل محمول على موضوع دائمًا أو سلب عنه دائما فلحمله أو السلبه في وقت بعينه علة لتلك العلة صارت النسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول تلك النسبة. وذات المحمول والموضوع ليس لهما لولا تلك العلة تلك النسبة بالوجوب بل بالإمكان. وإذا علم هذا وذلك من غير الجهة التي بها صار الحكم بينهما ضروريًا على تلك النسبة فقد علم من جهة غير الجهة التي بها لا يمكن أن يكونا لتلك الحال؛ وذلك هو أن يعلم الحكم بجهة غير جهة السبب الذي يوجبه لأن كل نسبة للموضوع إلى المحمول وللمحمول إلى الموضوع تفرض واقعة لا من الجهة التي توجبها العلة فهي واقعة من جهة إمكان لا وجوب، فيكون قد علم أن كذا كذا ولا يعلم أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا. فلو قال قائل إن كل إنسان ضحاك وكل ضحاك ناطق فلا يجب من هذا أن يتيقن أن كل إنسان ناطق بحيث لا يمكن أن يكون على خلافه؛ وذلك لأن قوة الضحك معلولة لقوة النطق فيما لم يعلم وجوب قوة النطق أولا ووجوب امتناع قوة الضحك لقوة النطق لم يجب أن يتيقن أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد إنسان ليست له قوة الضحك إلا أن يوجد ذلك بالحس أو لا يمكن أن لا تكون له قوة الضحك دائمًا وإن وجد له قوة الضحك. فأما إذا قيل كل إنسان ناطق وكل ناطق ضاحك يجب أن يتيقن أن كل إنسان ضاحك لما أن قوة النطق علة لقوة الضحك فظهر أن الشيء إذا كان له سبب لن يتيقن إلا من سببه فإن كان الأكبر للأصغر لا بسبب بل لذاته لكنه ليس بين الوجود والأوسط للأصغر كذلك إلا أنه بين الوجود للأصغر ثمَّ الأكبر للأوسط كذلك. فينعقد برهان يقيني ضرورة كون كل واحدة من مقدمتيه يقينية؛ ويكون برهان إنَّ لأن العلم لم يحصل من السبب الذي به تجب هذه النسبة بل أخذ من جهة هو بها لا يجب. وأما هنا فكان يدل السبب الذات، وكان الأكبر للأصغر لذاته؛ ولكن لم يكن بينا وكان الأوسط للأصغر لذاته لا بسبب لكنه بين، فعلمت الصغرى بوجوبها والكبرى أيضًا كذلك كما مرَّ . ثمَّ لقائل أن يقول كيف تكون الذات الواحدة تقتضي لذاتها بشيئين مثلا «ج» الأصغر كيف يقتضي «ب» الأوسط والأكبر. اللهم إلا أن يقتضي أحدهما لذاته أولا ونقيض الآخر لا لذاته بل بتوسط ذلك الأول فحينئذ يكون «ب» علة لما لا بحسب البيان فقط بل بحسب الوجود. لكنا نقول إن المنطقي من حيث هو منطقي يجب أن يأخذ أن هذا يمكن في كل مادة هذه صفتها ولا يمكن فيما يخالفها. وأما هل لهذه
Section: On Certain Knowledge: Everything that has a cause is known through its cause. We say: When a predicate is always attributed to a subject or always negated from it, then the attribution or negation at a particular time is a cause for that cause making the relation between subject and predicate that relation. The essence of the predicate and subject, apart from that cause, do not have that relation necessarily but possibly. If this is known from a perspective other than that by which the judgment became necessary for that relation, then it is known from a perspective other than that by which they cannot be in that state; that is, to know the judgment from a perspective other than the cause that necessitates it, because every relation of subject to predicate and predicate to subject that occurs not from the cause that necessitates it, occurs from possibility, not necessity. So one knows that something is so but does not know that it cannot be otherwise. So if someone says: Every man is laughing, and every laughing is rational, it is not necessary from this to be certain that every man is rational such that it cannot be contrary; because the power of laughter is an effect of the power of reason, so unless the necessity of the power of reason is first known and the impossibility of the power of laughter without the power of reason, it is not necessary to be certain that there cannot exist a man without the power of laughter unless that is known by sense, or that he cannot always lack the power of laughter even if he has the power of laughter. But if it is said: Every man is rational and every rational is laughing, then one must be certain that every man is laughing, because the power of reason is the cause of the power of laughter. Thus it appears that if a thing has a cause, it is only certain through its cause. If the major belongs to the minor not through a cause but by itself, yet it is not evident, and the middle belongs to the minor by itself but not through a cause and is evident, then a demonstrative syllogism necessarily occurs, each of its premises being certain; and it is a demonstration of 'inna (that) because knowledge is not obtained from the cause by which this relation is necessitated but is taken from a perspective by which it is not necessary. But here the cause indicates the essence, and the major belongs to the minor by itself; but it was not evident, and the middle belongs to the minor by itself not through a cause, but it is evident, so the minor premise is known with necessity, and the major also likewise as earlier. Then one may say: How can a single essence necessitate by itself two things? For example, how does 'j' (the minor) necessitate 'b' (the middle) and the greater? Unless it necessitates one by itself first, and the other not by itself but through the mediation of that first; then 'b' is a cause not only in terms of explanation but also in terms of existence. But we say: The logician qua logician must take that this is possible in every matter with this description and not possible in what contradicts it. As for whether this...
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the nature of certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī) and its relation to causation. The author begins by stating that every certain knowledge must be through the cause (sabab) of the known fact. He explains: if a predicate is always affirmed or denied of a subject, the time-specific affirmation or denial is a cause that makes the relation necessary; without that cause, the relation is merely possible. Knowing the relation from a non-causal perspective gives mere possibility, not necessity. To illustrate, he presents two syllogisms. The first: 'Every man is laughing; every laughing is rational.' This does not yield certainty that every man is rational, because laughter is an effect of rationality; unless one first knows the necessity of rationality and that laughter cannot exist without it, certainty is not attained—it might be known by sense or otherwise. The second: 'Every man is rational; every rational is laughing.' Here certainty is achieved because rationality is the cause of laughter. Thus, anything with a cause is only known certainly through its cause. The author then considers a case where the major term belongs to the minor by itself (not through an external cause) but is not evident, while the middle belongs to the minor by itself and is evident. This yields a demonstrative syllogism (burhān) with certain premises, but it is a burhān ʿinna (demonstration 'that') because the knowledge does not come from the cause that necessitates the relation—the cause is the essence itself, but the middle is not the cause. The author distinguishes the logical perspective from the material one: the logician qua logician accepts the possibility of such a case in appropriate matter, though the actual existence of such a cause is a separate question. Finally, an objection is raised: how can a single essence (e.g., the minor 'j') necessitate both the middle and the major? The reply: it may necessitate one by itself and the other by mediation of the first, so the middle becomes a cause both in explanation and existence. The passage emphasizes the importance of causal knowledge for certainty, a key topic in Islamic logic and epistemology.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following syllogisms would yield certain knowledge (yaqīn)?
A) Every human is rational; every rational is laughing; therefore every human is laughing.
B) Every human is laughing; every laughing is rational; therefore every human is rational.
C) Every cat is an animal; every animal is mortal; therefore every cat is mortal.
D) Every wine is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore every wine is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Certain knowledge of a proposition is only possible when we know it through its cause, not through its effects or accidental relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Construct a syllogism with a causal middle term using a fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (by divine law); nabidh is intoxicating; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Explain why this yields certain knowledge of the ruling for nabidh.
?
2. Compare: take the syllogism 'Every intoxicating drink is forbidden; every forbidden thing is sinful; therefore every intoxicating drink is sinful.' Here the middle term (forbidden) is an effect of the divine law, not the cause. Why does this not produce the same level of certainty?

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
10 min
Burhān ʿInna: Demonstration of the Fact vs. Demonstration of the Reason Why
Author distinguishes between causal and non-causal demonstrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between burhān ʿinna (demonstration 'that') and burhān limā (demonstration 'why') based on whether the middle term is the cause of the conclusion.

Core Idea
A demonstration 'that' gives knowledge of the fact without the cause; a demonstration 'why' gives both, using the cause as middle term.

The author introduces the concept of burhān ʿinna, a demonstrative syllogism where the middle term is not the cause of the conclusion but is rather an effect or concomitant. This is contrasted with a full causal demonstration (burhān limā). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the epistemological strength of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the description of burhān ʿinna
In the passage, after the example, the author says: 'فينعقد برهان يقيني ... ويكون برهان إنَّ لأن العلم لم يحصل من السبب الذي به تجب هذه النسبة بل أخذ من جهة هو بها لا يجب.' This is the definition: a demonstration 'that' where knowledge is not obtained from the cause.
2
Identify the non-causal middle term
In the example provided, the middle term is 'laughing' which is an effect of rationality. The syllogism 'Every man is laughing; every laughing is rational; therefore every man is rational' gives knowledge that men are rational, but not the reason why.
3
Contrast with causal demonstration
If we reverse the premises so that rationality is the middle term (cause), we get a demonstration 'why'. The causal syllogism gives certainty because the connection is necessary and explains why the conclusion holds.
4
Apply to theological reasoning
Consider the argument for the world's contingent nature: the world is temporally originated; whatever is temporally originated is contingent; therefore the world is contingent. The middle term 'temporally originated' is not the cause of contingency but a sign. This is burhān ʿinna. To have burhān limā, we would need the middle term to be the cause of contingency, e.g., 'whatever is composed of parts is contingent' (if composition is the cause).

According to the passage, what distinguishes a burhān ʿinna from a burhān limā?
A) The burhān ʿinna has only true premises, while burhān limā may have false premises.
B) In burhān ʿinna, the middle term is not the cause of the conclusion; in burhān limā, it is.
C) Burhān ʿinna is used only in logic, while burhān limā is used only in fiqh.
D) Burhān ʿinna yields certainty, while burhān limā yields only probability.

Key Takeaway
A demonstration 'that' (burhān ʿinna) gives knowledge of the fact but not the reason; a demonstration 'why' (burhān limā) gives both, using the cause as the middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify whether the following syllogism is a burhān ʿinna or burhān limā: 'Every contingent thing is temporally originated; the world is contingent; therefore the world is temporally originated.' Explain your reasoning based on the middle term's causal role.
?
2. Construct a burhān limā for the permissibility of selling (bay') using the 'illah of mutual consent (tarāḍin), ensuring the middle term is the cause of the ruling.
