Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
5 min
Certainty in Burhan Inni vs Burhan Limmi
The author compares burhan inni and burhan limmi using the example of rationality and laughter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish burhan inni from burhan limmi based on whether the middle term represents the cause, and determine when each yields certainty.

Core Idea
Knowledge through the cause is certain; knowledge from non-causal aspects may be conditional.

In this passage, the author refines our understanding of demonstration. While burhan limmi gives knowledge through the cause, burhan inni gives knowledge that a thing is so without knowing why. But the author shows that even burhan inni can sometimes yield perpetual certainty when the subject matter has no cause from that aspect. Let's unpack this using the example of rationality and laughter in humans.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two demonstrations in the example.
The author contrasts 'every human is rational, and every rational is capable of laughter' (causal) with the case where the causal relation is not known. Identify which is burhan limmi and which is burhan inni.
Burhan limmi: the middle term (rationality) is the cause of laughter. Burhan inni: only the correlation is observed, not the cause.
2
Determine the role of the middle term.
In the causal syllogism, the middle term 'rational' is the cause of the major term 'capable of laughter'. In the non-causal syllogism, the middle term is not known as cause.
3
Assess the certainty in each case.
The author argues that only when the cause is known does the conclusion become necessary and certain. Without the cause, the certainty is conditional on the premises being necessary, but the knowledge is not from the cause.
4
Generalize the principle.
The passage states that when a thing has a cause, it can only be certain from its cause. This is the foundation of burhan limmi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يجب من هذا أن يتيقن أن كل إنسان ناطق بحيث لا يمكن أن يكون على خلافه؛ وذلك لأن قوة الضحك معلولة لقوة النطق فيما لم يعلم وجوب قوة النطق أولا ووجوب امتناع قوة الضحك لقوة النطق لم يجب أن يتيقن أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد إنسان ليست له قوة الضحك إلا أن يوجد ذلك بالحس أو لا يمكن أن لا تكون له قوة الضحك دائمًا وإن وجد له قوة الضحك. فأما إذا قيل كل إنسان ناطق وكل ناطق ضاحك يجب أن يتيقن أن كل إنسان ضاحك لما أن قوة النطق علة لقوة الضحك فظهر أن الشيء إذا كان له سبب لن يتيقن إلا من سببه فإن كان الأكبر للأصغر لا بسبب بل لذاته لكنه ليس بين الوجود والأوسط للأصغر كذلك إلا أنه بين الوجود للأصغر ثم الأكبر للأوسط كذلك. فينعقد برهان يقيني ضرورة كون كل واحدة من مقدمتيه يقينية؛ ويكون برهان إنّ لأن العلم لم يحصل من السبب الذي به تجب هذه النسبة بل أخذ من جهة هو بها لا يجب. وأما هنا فكان يدل السبب الذات، وكان الأكبر للأصغر لذاته؛ ولكن لم يكن بينا وكان الأوسط للأصغر لذاته لا بسبب لكنه بين، فعلمت الصغرى بوجوبها والكبرى أيضًا كذلك كما مر. ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف تكون الذات الواحدة تقتضي لذاتها بشيئين مثلا «ج» الأصغر كيف يقتضي «ب» الأوسط والأكبر. اللهم إلا أن يقتضي أحدهما لذاته أولا ونقيض الآخر لا لذاته بل بتوسط ذلك الأول فحينئذ يكون «ب» علة لما لا بحسب البيان فقط بل بحسب الوجود. لكنا نقول إن المنطقي من حيث هو منطقي يجب أن يأخذ أن هذا يمكن في كل مادة هذه صفتها ولا يمكن فيما يخالفها. وأما هل لهذه المواد إمكان أم لا؛ فليس ذلك على المنطقي؛ إذ هو وأمثاله من المباحث المعلقة بالفلسفة الأولى. وبالجملة فقد يحصل من هذا أن برهان الإن قد يعطي في مواضع يقينا دائما. وأما فيما له سبب ولا يعطي اليقين الدائم بل فيما ليس له سبب من هذه الجهة نقول إن الرياضي لا يقين له في كثير من الأمور المنسوبة إلى الهيئة؛ لأنه يأخذها من جهة ما وجدت بالرصد مثلا أوج الشمس من جهة أن حركة الشمس غير مستوية في أجزاء فلك البروج سرعة وبطئا؛ فبطؤها للأوج وسرعتها الحضيض ولا يعطي العلة في شيء من هذا، وإنما يعطيها الطبيعي. لا يقال إنا إذا رأينا صنعة علمنا بالضرورة أن لها صانعا، ولا يمكن أن يزول عنا هذا العلم البتة. وهو الاستدلال بالمعلول على العلة أعني برهان الإن لأنا نقول الصغرى في هذا القياس إن كانت جزئية كما في قولنا هذا البناء مصوّر وكل مصوّر فله مصوّر؛ فلا يسلم أن نتيجته وهو قولنا فهذا البناء له مصوّر مما يقع به اليقين دائما. وكيف هذا البناء إذا فسد فقد زال ذلك الاعتقاد؛ وإن كانت كلية كما في قولنا كل جسم مؤلّف من هيولى وصورة، وكل مؤلّف فله مؤلف: فنقول كون الجسم مؤلفًا من هيولى وصورة إما أمر ذاتي للجسم، وإما عرضي لازم لذاته؛ فإن كان ذاتيا فالمحمول عليه أن له مؤلفًا لا المؤلف فليس المحمول العلة؛ لأن العلة هو المؤلف؛ وليس المؤلف هو الحد الأكبر بل له مؤلفًا. ولا نقول إن المؤلف مؤلّف ثم ذو المؤلف هو أو لا للمؤلف ثم المؤلف من هيولي وصورة سواء كان مقوما للمؤلف أو لازما. وإذا كان المؤلف في نفس الوجود هو أولا للمؤلف فهو لما تحت المؤلف بسبب المؤلف على ما عرف فيكون اليقين حاصلا بعلة ويكون للمؤلف علة لوجود ذي المؤلف. وإن كان جزءا من ذي المؤلف وهو المؤلف علة المؤلف فقد بان الحد الأكبر.
So it is not necessary from this that one be certain that every human is rational such that it cannot be otherwise; that is because the potentiality for laughter is an effect of the potentiality for rationality, insofar as the necessity of the potentiality for rationality is not known first, and the necessity of the impossibility of the potentiality for laughter due to the potentiality for rationality is not known, it is not necessary to be certain that it is impossible for a human to exist who does not have the potentiality for laughter, unless that is found by sense, or that it is impossible for him not to have the potentiality for laughter perpetually even if he has the potentiality for laughter. But if it is said 'Every human is rational and every rational is capable of laughter', it must be certain that every human is capable of laughter, because the potentiality for rationality is the cause of the potentiality for laughter. So it appears that when a thing has a cause, it is only certain from its cause. For if the major is predicated of the minor not through a cause but through itself, but it is not evident, and the middle is predicated of the minor not through a cause but through itself, yet it is evident, then a certain demonstration is formed, both its premises necessarily being certain; and it is a demonstration of 'that' (burhan inni) because knowledge was not acquired from the cause by which this relation is necessary, but rather it was taken from an aspect by which it is not necessary. But here, the cause indicated the essence, and the major was predicated of the minor through itself; but it was not evident, and the middle was predicated of the minor through itself not through a cause, but it was evident, so the minor premise was known with its necessity and the major also similarly as has passed. Then one might say: How can one essence necessitate by itself two things? For example, 'c' the minor, how does it necessitate 'b' the middle and the major? Except if it necessitates one of them by itself first, and the other not by itself but by mediation of that first; then 'b' is a cause of what not only according to explanation but according to existence. But we say that the logician qua logician must take that this is possible in every matter having this description and not possible in what differs from it. As for whether these matters have possibility or not, that is not incumbent on the logician, since it and its like are among the investigations attached to first philosophy. In sum, it may be obtained from this that the demonstration of 'that' sometimes gives perpetual certainty. As for what has a cause and does not give perpetual certainty, but regarding what does not have a cause from this aspect, we say that the mathematician has no certainty in many of the matters attributed to astronomy; because he takes them from the aspect that they are found by observation, for example the apogee of the sun from the aspect that the motion of the sun is uneven in the parts of the zodiacal sphere, speed and slowness; so its slowness is for the apogee and its speed for the perigee, and he does not give the cause in any of this, but the natural philosopher gives it. It cannot be said that when we see an artifact we know necessarily that it has a maker, and this knowledge cannot leave us at all—and this is reasoning from effect to cause, i.e., the demonstration of 'that'—for we say: The minor premise in this syllogism, if it is particular, as in our statement 'This building is formed, and every formed thing has a form-maker', then it is not granted that its conclusion, which is our statement 'This building has a form-maker', is something by which perpetual certainty occurs. How? If this building is destroyed, that belief ceases. And if it is universal, as in our statement 'Every body is composed of matter and form, and every composed thing has a composer', then we say: The body's being composed of matter and form is either essential to the body, or accidental necessarily attached to its essence. If it is essential, then the predicate is that it has a composer, not the composer, so the predicate is not the cause; because the cause is the composer; and the composer is not the major term, but rather 'has a composer'. And we do not say that the composer is composed, then the possessor of the composer is or is not for the composer, then the composer from matter and form, whether it is constitutive of the composer or necessary. And when the composer in itself is first for the composer, then it is for what is under the composer by reason of the composer as known, so certainty is obtained with a cause, and the composer is a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer. And if it is a part of the possessor of the composer, and the composer is the cause of the composer, then the major term has become clear.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the conditions under which demonstrations yield certainty, distinguishing between burhan inni (demonstration that something is the case) and burhan limmi (demonstration of why it is the case). The author begins by considering the example of the relationship between rationality and laughter in humans. If one knows that every human is rational and every rational being is capable of laughter, then one must be certain that every human is capable of laughter, because rationality is the cause of laughter. However, if the causal relation is not known, certainty is not attained. The passage then analyzes different configurations of the syllogism: if the major term is predicated of the minor through itself but is not evident, and the middle term is also predicated through itself but is evident, then the demonstration is one of 'that' (burhan inni) because the cause is not the source of knowledge. The author anticipates an objection: how can a single essence necessitate two different predicates (the middle and the major)? The response is that the logician accepts this as possible in appropriate matters, leaving the metaphysical verification to first philosophy. The author then states that burhan inni can sometimes give perpetual certainty (when the matter has no cause from that aspect), but for things with causes, it does not. An example from astronomy illustrates that the mathematician (e.g., Ptolemy) describes phenomena like the sun's apogee from observation but does not give the physical cause; the natural philosopher does. The argument from design (seeing an artifact implies a maker) is examined. If the minor premise is particular (e.g., 'This building is formed'), the conclusion is not perpetually certain because if the building is destroyed, the belief ceases. If the minor is universal (e.g., 'Every body is composed of matter and form has a composer'), then further analysis is needed: if composition is essential to body, then the predicate is 'has a composer', not 'the composer', so the cause is not captured in the major term. The discussion concludes that certainty with a cause is achieved when the composer is a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer, clarifying the status of the major term. Throughout, the author maintains a rigorous distinction between logical form and material content, emphasizing that the logician's concern is with the formal validity, not with the ontological possibility of the materials.

Key Terms
برهان إنّ (burhan inni): A demonstration that a predicate holds for a subject, but without knowledge of the cause; also called demonstration of 'that'. [Note: In earlier Aristotelian logic, burhan inni is often contrasted with burhan limmi; this text refines the conditions under which it yields certainty.]
برهان لمّ (burhan limmi): A demonstration that gives knowledge through the cause, yielding certainty that the conclusion is necessary. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable.]
علة ('illa): The cause that necessitates the existence of the effect; in demonstration, the middle term often represents the cause. [Note: In logic, 'illa is the middle term in burhan limmi; in usul al-fiqh, it is the effective cause for a legal ruling.]
يقين (yaqin): Certainty that is necessary and unchanging; the passage explores when certainty is perpetual vs. conditional. [Note: In kalam, yaqin often refers to necessary knowledge from revelation; here it is epistemological and tied to demonstration.]
أوسط (awsat): The middle term in a categorical syllogism, which links the major and minor terms. [Note: Stable term in syllogistic logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes the syllogism 'Every human is rational and every rational is capable of laughter' yield certainty?
A) The major term is self-evident.
B) The middle term is the cause.
C) The minor is particular.
D) It is in the second figure.

Key Takeaway
Burhan limmi yields certainty because the middle term is the cause; burhan inni yields only conditional certainty unless the matter has no cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a burhan limmi for the statement 'The Quran is the word of Allah' using the middle term as the cause.
?
Take the statement 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Is the demonstration burhan inni or burhan limmi? Explain why.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
5 min
When Burhan Inni Yields Perpetual Certainty
The author discusses when burhan inni yields perpetual certainty, using the sun's apogee example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a burhan inni yields perpetual certainty, using the example of the sun's apogee.

Core Idea
Burhan inni gives perpetual certainty only when the subject matter has no cause from the aspect considered.

The author makes a crucial refinement: burhan inni does not always yield conditional certainty. When the matter has no cause from the relevant aspect, as in astronomical observations, the certainty is perpetual. But the mathematician does not give the cause; the natural philosopher does. Let's explore this using the sun's motion example.

Guided Path
1
Locate the sun's apogee example.
The author mentions that the mathematician takes the sun's apogee from observation (slowness for apogee, speed for perigee) but does not give the cause. Identify what the mathematician knows and what he lacks.
2
Determine why the mathematician has no certainty.
Because he only has empirical correlation, not causal knowledge. The knowledge is based on sense, not demonstration. Therefore, it is not perpetual certainty.
3
Contrast with the natural philosopher.
The natural philosopher gives the physical cause, so his knowledge is certain and perpetual.
4
Generalize: when does burhan inni give perpetual certainty?
The author says that for things that have no cause from this aspect, burhan inni can give perpetual certainty. For things with a cause, it does not, because the cause is not known.

مصوّر فله مصوّر؛ فلا يسلم أن نتيجته وهو قولنا فهذا البناء له مصوّر مما يقع به اليقين دائما. وكيف هذا البناء إذا فسد فقد زال ذلك الاعتقاد؛ وإن كانت كلية كما في قولنا كل جسم مؤلّف من هيولى وصورة، وكل مؤلّف فله مؤلف: فنقول كون الجسم مؤلفًا من هيولى وصورة إما أمر ذاتي للجسم، وإما عرضي لازم لذاته؛ فإن كان ذاتيا فالمحمول عليه أن له مؤلفًا لا المؤلف فليس المحمول العلة؛ لأن العلة هو المؤلف؛ وليس المؤلف هو الحد الأكبر بل له مؤلفًا. ولا نقول إن المؤلف مؤلّف ثم ذو المؤلف هو أو لا للمؤلف ثم المؤلف من هيولي وصورة سواء كان مقوما للمؤلف أو لازما. وإذا كان المؤلف في نفس الوجود هو أولا للمؤلف فهو لما تحت المؤلف بسبب المؤلف على ما عرف فيكون اليقين حاصلا بعلة ويكون للمؤلف علة لوجود ذي المؤلف. وإن كان جزءا من ذي المؤلف وهو المؤلف علة المؤلف فقد بان الحد الأكبر.
So it is not necessary from this that one be certain that every human is rational such that it cannot be otherwise; that is because the potentiality for laughter is an effect of the potentiality for rationality, insofar as the necessity of the potentiality for rationality is not known first, and the necessity of the impossibility of the potentiality for laughter due to the potentiality for rationality is not known, it is not necessary to be certain that it is impossible for a human to exist who does not have the potentiality for laughter, unless that is found by sense, or that it is impossible for him not to have the potentiality for laughter perpetually even if he has the potentiality for laughter. But if it is said 'Every human is rational and every rational is capable of laughter', it must be certain that every human is capable of laughter, because the potentiality for rationality is the cause of the potentiality for laughter. So it appears that when a thing has a cause, it is only certain from its cause. For if the major is predicated of the minor not through a cause but through itself, but it is not evident, and the middle is predicated of the minor not through a cause but through itself, yet it is evident, then a certain demonstration is formed, both its premises necessarily being certain; and it is a demonstration of 'that' (burhan inni) because knowledge was not acquired from the cause by which this relation is necessary, but rather it was taken from an aspect by which it is not necessary. But here, the cause indicated the essence, and the major was predicated of the minor through itself; but it was not evident, and the middle was predicated of the minor through itself not through a cause, but it was evident, so the minor premise was known with its necessity and the major also similarly as has passed. Then one might say: How can one essence necessitate by itself two things? For example, 'c' the minor, how does it necessitate 'b' the middle and the major? Except if it necessitates one of them by itself first, and the other not by itself but by mediation of that first; then 'b' is a cause of what not only according to explanation but according to existence. But we say that the logician qua logician must take that this is possible in every matter having this description and not possible in what differs from it. As for whether these matters have possibility or not, that is not incumbent on the logician, since it and its like are among the investigations attached to first philosophy. In sum, it may be obtained from this that the demonstration of 'that' sometimes gives perpetual certainty. As for what has a cause and does not give perpetual certainty, but regarding what does not have a cause from this aspect, we say that the mathematician has no certainty in many of the matters attributed to astronomy; because he takes them from the aspect that they are found by observation, for example the apogee of the sun from the aspect that the motion of the sun is uneven in the parts of the zodiacal sphere, speed and slowness; so its slowness is for the apogee and its speed for the perigee, and he does not give the cause in any of this, but the natural philosopher gives it. It cannot be said that when we see an artifact we know necessarily that it has a maker, and this knowledge cannot leave us at all—and this is reasoning from effect to cause, i.e., the demonstration of 'that'—for we say: The minor premise in this syllogism, if it is particular, as in our statement 'This building is formed, and every formed thing has a form-maker', then it is not granted that its conclusion, which is our statement 'This building has a form-maker', is something by which perpetual certainty occurs. How? If this building is destroyed, that belief ceases. And if it is universal, as in our statement 'Every body is composed of matter and form, and every composed thing has a composer', then we say: The body's being composed of matter and form is either essential to the body, or accidental necessarily attached to its essence. If it is essential, then the predicate is that it has a composer, not the composer, so the predicate is not the cause; because the cause is the composer; and the composer is not the major term, but rather 'has a composer'. And we do not say that the composer is composed, then the possessor of the composer is or is not for the composer, then the composer from matter and form, whether it is constitutive of the composer or necessary. And when the composer in itself is first for the composer, then it is for what is under the composer by reason of the composer as known, so certainty is obtained with a cause, and the composer is a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer. And if it is a part of the possessor of the composer, and the composer is the cause of the composer, then the major term has become clear.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the conditions under which demonstrations yield certainty, distinguishing between burhan inni (demonstration that something is the case) and burhan limmi (demonstration of why it is the case). The author begins by considering the example of the relationship between rationality and laughter in humans. If one knows that every human is rational and every rational being is capable of laughter, then one must be certain that every human is capable of laughter, because rationality is the cause of laughter. However, if the causal relation is not known, certainty is not attained. The passage then analyzes different configurations of the syllogism: if the major term is predicated of the minor through itself but is not evident, and the middle term is also predicated through itself but is evident, then the demonstration is one of 'that' (burhan inni) because the cause is not the source of knowledge. The author anticipates an objection: how can a single essence necessitate two different predicates (the middle and the major)? The response is that the logician accepts this as possible in appropriate matters, leaving the metaphysical verification to first philosophy. The author then states that burhan inni can sometimes give perpetual certainty (when the matter has no cause from that aspect), but for things with causes, it does not. An example from astronomy illustrates that the mathematician (e.g., Ptolemy) describes phenomena like the sun's apogee from observation but does not give the physical cause; the natural philosopher does. The argument from design (seeing an artifact implies a maker) is examined. If the minor premise is particular (e.g., 'This building is formed'), the conclusion is not perpetually certain because if the building is destroyed, the belief ceases. If the minor is universal (e.g., 'Every body is composed of matter and form has a composer'), then further analysis is needed: if composition is essential to body, then the predicate is 'has a composer', not 'the composer', so the cause is not captured in the major term. The discussion concludes that certainty with a cause is achieved when the composer is a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer, clarifying the status of the major term. Throughout, the author maintains a rigorous distinction between logical form and material content, emphasizing that the logician's concern is with the formal validity, not with the ontological possibility of the materials.

Key Terms
برهان إنّ (burhan inni): A demonstration that a predicate holds for a subject, but without knowledge of the cause; also called demonstration of 'that'. [Note: In earlier Aristotelian logic, burhan inni is often contrasted with burhan limmi; this text refines the conditions under which it yields certainty.]
برهان لمّ (burhan limmi): A demonstration that gives knowledge through the cause, yielding certainty that the conclusion is necessary. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable.]
علة ('illa): The cause that necessitates the existence of the effect; in demonstration, the middle term often represents the cause. [Note: In logic, 'illa is the middle term in burhan limmi; in usul al-fiqh, it is the effective cause for a legal ruling.]
يقين (yaqin): Certainty that is necessary and unchanging; the passage explores when certainty is perpetual vs. conditional. [Note: In kalam, yaqin often refers to necessary knowledge from revelation; here it is epistemological and tied to demonstration.]
أوسط (awsat): The middle term in a categorical syllogism, which links the major and minor terms. [Note: Stable term in syllogistic logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He relies on observation without cause.

Key Takeaway
Burhan inni may give perpetual certainty only when the matter has no cause from the relevant aspect; otherwise, certainty requires causal knowledge.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate

Particular and Universal Premises in Demonstration from Effect to Cause
The author analyzes the argument from design, showing how particular premises affect perpetual certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a burhan inni from effect to cause yields perpetual certainty by analyzing the universality of the minor premise.

Core Idea
A particular premise in a burhan inni prevents perpetual certainty because the belief ceases with the object.

The author examines the argument from design (seeing an artifact implies a maker). He argues that if the minor premise is particular ('this building is formed'), the conclusion is not perpetually certain because the building may be destroyed. If the minor is universal ('every body is composed of matter and form'), further analysis is needed. Let's dissect this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases of the argument from design.
The author presents two versions: one with a particular minor premise about a single building, and one with a universal minor premise about all bodies. Note the difference.
2
Analyze the particular case.
The particular premise 'this building is formed' leads to a conclusion that ceases when the building is destroyed. So the certainty is not perpetual.
3
Analyze the universal case.
The universal premise 'every body is composed of matter and form' leads to a more complex analysis. If composition is essential, the predicate is 'has a composer', not 'the composer'. The cause (the composer) is not captured in the major term directly.
4
Determine when certainty with a cause is achieved.
The author concludes that if the composer is a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer, then certainty is obtained with a cause.

قوة النطق علة لقوة الضحك فظهر أن الشيء إذا كان له سبب لن يتيقن إلا من سببه فإن كان الأكبر للأصغر لا بسبب بل لذاته لكنه ليس بين الوجود والأوسط للأصغر كذلك إلا أنه بين الوجود للأصغر ثم الأكبر للأوسط كذلك. فينعقد برهان يقيني ضرورة كون كل واحدة من مقدمتيه يقينية؛ ويكون برهان إنّ لأن العلم لم يحصل من السبب الذي به تجب هذه النسبة بل أخذ من جهة هو بها لا يجب. وأما هنا فكان يدل السبب الذات، وكان الأكبر للأصغر لذاته؛ ولكن لم يكن بينا وكان الأوسط للأصغر لذاته لا بسبب لكنه بين، فعلمت الصغرى بوجوبها والكبرى أيضًا كذلك كما مر. ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف تكون الذات الواحدة تقتضي لذاتها بشيئين مثلا «ج» الأصغر كيف يقتضي «ب» الأوسط والأكبر. اللهم إلا أن يقتضي أحدهما لذاته أولا ونقيض الآخر لا لذاته بل بتوسط ذلك الأول فحينئذ يكون «ب» علة لما لا بحسب البيان فقط بل بحسب الوجود. لكنا نقول إن المنطقي من حيث هو منطقي يجب أن يأخذ أن هذا يمكن في كل مادة هذه صفتها ولا يمكن فيما يخالفها. وأما هل لهذه المواد إمكان أم لا؛ فليس ذلك على المنطقي؛ إذ هو وأمثاله من المباحث المعلقة بالفلسفة الأولى. وبالجملة فقد يحصل من هذا أن برهان الإن قد يعطي في مواضع يقينا دائما. وأما فيما له سبب ولا يعطي اليقين الدائم بل فيما ليس له سبب من هذه الجهة نقول إن الرياضي لا يقين له في كثير من الأمور المنسوبة إلى الهيئة؛ لأنه يأخذها من جهة ما وجدت بالرصد مثلا أوج الشمس من جهة أن حركة الشمس غير مستوية في أجزاء فلك البروج سرعة وبطئا؛ فبطؤها للأوج وسرعتها الحضيض ولا يعطي العلة في شيء من هذا، وإنما يعطيها الطبيعي. لا يقال إنا إذا رأينا صنعة علمنا بالضرورة أن لها صانعا، ولا يمكن أن يزول عنا هذا العلم البتة. وهو الاستدلال بالمعلول على العلة أعني برهان الإن لأنا نقول الصغرى في هذا القياس إن كانت جزئية كما في قولنا هذا البناء مصوّر وكل مصوّر فله مصوّر؛ فلا يسلم أن نتيجته وهو قولنا فهذا البناء له مصوّر مما يقع به اليقين دائما. وكيف هذا البناء إذا فسد فقد زال ذلك الاعتقاد؛ وإن كانت كلية كما في قولنا كل جسم مؤلّف من هيولى وصورة، وكل مؤلّف فله مؤلف: فنقول كون الجسم مؤلفًا من هيولى وصورة إما أمر ذاتي للجسم، وإما عرضي لازم لذاته؛ فإن كان ذاتيا فالمحمول عليه أن له مؤلفًا لا المؤلف فليس المحمول العلة؛ لأن العلة هو المؤلف؛ وليس المؤلف هو الحد الأكبر بل له مؤلفًا. ولا نقول إن المؤلف مؤلّف ثم ذو المؤلف هو أو لا للمؤلف ثم المؤلف من هيولي وصورة سواء كان مقوما للمؤلف أو لازما. وإذا كان المؤلف في نفس الوجود هو أولا للمؤلف فهو لما تحت المؤلف بسبب المؤلف على ما عرف فيكون اليقين حاصلا بعلة ويكون للمؤلف علة لوجود ذي المؤلف. وإن كان جزءا من ذي المؤلف وهو المؤلف علة المؤلف فقد بان الحد الأكبر.
So it is not necessary from this that one be certain that every human is rational such that it cannot be otherwise; that is because the potentiality for laughter is an effect of the potentiality for rationality, insofar as the necessity of the potentiality for rationality is not known first, and the necessity of the impossibility of the potentiality for laughter due to the potentiality for rationality is not known, it is not necessary to be certain that it is impossible for a human to exist who does not have the potentiality for laughter, unless that is found by sense, or that it is impossible for him not to have the potentiality for laughter perpetually even if he has the potentiality for laughter. But if it is said 'Every human is rational and every rational is capable of laughter', it must be certain that every human is capable of laughter, because the potentiality for rationality is the cause of the potentiality for laughter. So it appears that when a thing has a cause, it is only certain from its cause. For if the major is predicated of the minor not through a cause but through itself, but it is not evident, and the middle is predicated of the minor not through a cause but through itself, yet it is evident, then a certain demonstration is formed, both its premises necessarily being certain; and it is a demonstration of 'that' (burhan inni) because knowledge was not acquired from the cause by which this relation is necessary, but rather it was taken from an aspect by which it is not necessary. But here, the cause indicated the essence, and the major was predicated of the minor through itself; but it was not evident, and the middle was predicated of the minor through itself not through a cause, but it was evident, so the minor premise was known with its necessity and the major also similarly as has passed. Then one might say: How can one essence necessitate by itself two things? For example, 'c' the minor, how does it necessitate 'b' the middle and the major? Except if it necessitates one of them by itself first, and the other not by itself but by mediation of that first; then 'b' is a cause of what not only according to explanation but according to existence. But we say that the logician qua logician must take that this is possible in every matter having this description and not possible in what differs from it. As for whether these matters have possibility or not, that is not incumbent on the logician, since it and its like are among the investigations attached to first philosophy. In sum, it may be obtained from this that the demonstration of 'that' sometimes gives perpetual certainty. As for what has a cause and does not give perpetual certainty, but regarding what does not have a cause from this aspect, we say that the mathematician has no certainty in many of the matters attributed to astronomy; because he takes them from the aspect that they are found by observation, for example the apogee of the sun from the aspect that the motion of the sun is uneven in the parts of the zodiacal sphere, speed and slowness; so its slowness is for the apogee and its speed for the perigee, and he does not give the cause in any of this, but the natural philosopher gives it. It cannot be said that when we see an artifact we know necessarily that it has a maker, and this knowledge cannot leave us at all—and this is reasoning from effect to cause, i.e., the demonstration of 'that'—for we say: The minor premise in this syllogism, if it is particular, as in our statement 'This building is formed, and every formed thing has a form-maker', then it is not granted that its conclusion, which is our statement 'This building has a form-maker', is something by which perpetual certainty occurs. How? If this building is destroyed, that belief ceases. And if it is universal, as in our statement 'Every body is composed of matter and form, and every composed thing has a composer', then we say: The body's being composed of matter and form is either essential to the body, or accidental necessarily attached to its essence. If it is essential, then the predicate is that it has a composer, not the composer, so the predicate is not the cause; because the cause is the composer; and the composer is not the major term, but rather 'has a composer'. And we do not say that the composer is composed, then the possessor of the composer is or is not for the composer, then the composer from matter and form, whether it is constitutive of the composer or necessary. And when the composer in itself is first for the composer, then it is for what is under the composer by reason of the composer as known, so certainty is obtained with a cause, and the composer is a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer. And if it is a part of the possessor of the composer, and the composer is the cause of the composer, then the major term has become clear.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the conditions under which demonstrations yield certainty, distinguishing between burhan inni (demonstration that something is the case) and burhan limmi (demonstration of why it is the case). The author begins by considering the example of the relationship between rationality and laughter in humans. If one knows that every human is rational and every rational being is capable of laughter, then one must be certain that every human is capable of laughter, because rationality is the cause of laughter. However, if the causal relation is not known, certainty is not attained. The passage then analyzes different configurations of the syllogism: if the major term is predicated of the minor through itself but is not evident, and the middle term is also predicated through itself but is evident, then the demonstration is one of 'that' (burhan inni) because the cause is not the source of knowledge. The author anticipates an objection: how can a single essence necessitate two different predicates (the middle and the major)? The response is that the logician accepts this as possible in appropriate matters, leaving the metaphysical verification to first philosophy. The author then states that burhan inni can sometimes give perpetual certainty (when the matter has no cause from that aspect), but for things with causes, it does not. An example from astronomy illustrates that the mathematician (e.g., Ptolemy) describes phenomena like the sun's apogee from observation but does not give the physical cause; the natural philosopher does. The argument from design (seeing an artifact implies a maker) is examined. If the minor premise is particular (e.g., 'This building is formed'), the conclusion is not perpetually certain because if the building is destroyed, the belief ceases. If the minor is universal (e.g., 'Every body is composed of matter and form has a composer'), then further analysis is needed: if composition is essential to body, then the predicate is 'has a composer', not 'the composer', so the cause is not captured in the major term. The discussion concludes that certainty with a cause is achieved when the composer is a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer, clarifying the status of the major term. Throughout, the author maintains a rigorous distinction between logical form and material content, emphasizing that the logician's concern is with the formal validity, not with the ontological possibility of the materials.

Key Terms
برهان إنّ (burhan inni): A demonstration that a predicate holds for a subject, but without knowledge of the cause; also called demonstration of 'that'. [Note: In earlier Aristotelian logic, burhan inni is often contrasted with burhan limmi; this text refines the conditions under which it yields certainty.]
برهان لمّ (burhan limmi): A demonstration that gives knowledge through the cause, yielding certainty that the conclusion is necessary. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable.]
علة ('illa): The cause that necessitates the existence of the effect; in demonstration, the middle term often represents the cause. [Note: In logic, 'illa is the middle term in burhan limmi; in usul al-fiqh, it is the effective cause for a legal ruling.]
يقين (yaqin): Certainty that is necessary and unchanging; the passage explores when certainty is perpetual vs. conditional. [Note: In kalam, yaqin often refers to necessary knowledge from revelation; here it is epistemological and tied to demonstration.]

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
10 min
Burhan al-Inn: Certainty and the Role of Cause
Author analyzes Burhan al-Inn, its certainty conditions, and responds to objections about cause and inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conditions under which Burhan al-Inn yields perpetual certainty and evaluate objections concerning the role of cause in demonstrative inference.

Core Idea
Burhan al-Inn gives perpetual certainty only when the middle term is a true cause and premises are essential.

In this passage, the author delves into Burhan al-Inn (demonstration of existence) and its capacity to generate certainty. He examines examples from astronomy, building, and number parity, showing that certainty depends on whether the minor premise is particular or universal and whether the middle term is genuinely causal. He also responds to objections that seem to show certainty without cause, and critiques Ibn Sina's definition of qiyas. By following his analysis, you will learn to assess the epistemic strength of inferences from effect to cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of Burhan al-Inn
The author analyzes two cases: a particular premise ('this building is well-formed') and a universal premise ('every body is composed of matter and form'). Note how the author tests their certainty.
The particular case loses certainty when the object decays; the universal case depends on whether the composition is essential.
2
Examine the role of causality in the universal case
The author distinguishes whether the composition is essential or accidental. If essential, the predicate 'has a composer' is not itself the cause; the cause is the composer. Thus the major term is not the cause, and certainty hinges on a proper causal connection.
The middle term must be the cause of the major term's attribution to the minor.
3
Understand the objection about certainty without cause
The objector gives examples: slave/master and odd/even. These seem to provide certainty without the middle term being a cause. The author responds by showing relational knowledge (slave/master) or missing inference (odd/even).
The slave/master case is relational, not a separate inference. The odd/even case requires knowledge of absence of evenness, which does not directly give oddness.
4
Trace the critique of Ibn Sina's definition of qiyas
The author argues that Ibn Sina's definition of qiyas does not require that a syllogism be useful (yield unknown knowledge). He cites Ibn Sina's own example of genus as cause (every human is an animal, every animal is sentient) as a valid Burhan Limma even if the conclusion is known.
This refines the definition: a syllogism can be valid without providing new knowledge, as long as the causal connection holds.
5
Synthesize when Burhan al-Inn gives perpetual certainty
The author concludes that for Burhan al-Inn to yield perpetual certainty, the middle term must be a true cause and the premises must be universal and essential. Otherwise, certainty is conditional or temporary.
This ties the entire analysis together and distinguishes genuine demonstration from probabilistic inference.

وبالجملة فقد يحصل من هذا أن برهان الإن قد يعطي في مواضع يقينا دائما. وأما فيما له سبب ولا يعطي اليقين الدائم بل فيما ليس له سبب من هذه الجهة نقول إن الرياضي لا يقين له في كثير من الأمور المنسوبة إلى الطبيعي. لا يقال إنا إذا رأينا صنعة علمنا بالضرورة أن لها صانعا، ولا يمكن أن يزول عنا هذا العلم البتة. وهو الاستدلال بالمعلول على العلة أعني برهان الإن لأنا نقول الصغرى في هذا القياس إن كانت جزئية كما العلة هو المؤلف؛ وليس المؤلف هو الحد الأكبر بل له مؤلفًا. ولا نقول إن المؤلف مؤلّف ثم ذو المؤلف هو أو لا للمؤلف ثم المؤلف من هيولي وصورة سواء كان مقوما للمؤلف أو لازما. وإذا كان المؤلف في نفس الوجود هو أولا للمؤلف فهو لما تحت المؤلف بسبب المؤلف على ما عرف فيكون اليقين حاصلًا بعلة ويكون للمؤلف علة لوجود ذي المؤلف. وإن كان جزءًا من ذي المؤلف وهو المؤلف علة المؤلف فقد بان الحد الأكبر. في الشيء المتيقن بالحقيقة لا يجوز أن يكون علة للأوسط؛ عسى أن يكون فيه جزء هو علة للأوسط. واعتبار الجزء غير اعتبار الكل. لكن لقائل أن يقول لو كان المتيقن مفتقرًا إلى حصول العلة لما حصل التيقن فيما لا علة فيه كما إذا جعل الأخ مثلا حدا أوسط، وقد حصل التيقن؛ فإنا إذا قلنا هذا عبد، وكل عبد فله مالك يعلم يقينًا أن هذا له مالك. وكذلك إذا علمنا أن هذا العدد ليس بزوج علمنا بالضرورة أنه فرد؛ وليس ذلك غير علة فإنه ليس إنه ليس بزوج علة لكونه فردا بل الأمر على العكس. لكنا نقول في الأول إن حصول التيقن في حيز المنع فإن المضاف لا يعلم بالمضاف بل مع المضاف. ولو كان كذلك فلا تيقن أحدهما بالآخر فإن علمنا بأن هذا أخ هو نفس علمنا بأن له أخا أو يشتمل عليه. وفي الثاني إن الاستثناء المذكور إن لم يكن يقينيا لم تكن النتيجة اللازمة عنه يقينية وإن كان يقينيا؛ وليس ذلك إلا لفقدان ضد الزوجية عن العدد المذكور. ومتى علم فقدان ضد الزوجية عنه لا يعلم أنه فرد أو غير فرد فلا يكون العلم بكونه فردًا من ذلك القياس والنتيجة يجب أن تكون معلومة من القياس المذكور وإلا لم يكن القياس مفيدًا إياها فلا يكون القياس قياسا هكذا ذكره الشيخ في الشفاء، وفيه نظر فإن التعريف الذي عرفه به أي القياس لا يقتضي أن يكون القياس إنما يكون قياسًا إن لو كان مفيدا لما يلزم منه كيف وإنه قال في هذا الفصل إن الجنس علة للنوع في حمل فصل الجنس عليه كما هو علة له في حمل جنس الجنس عليه. فعلى هذا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس كان ذلك قياسا يلزم منه أن كل إنسان حساس بل برهان لِمَ؛ إذ الأوسط فيه علة لثبوت الأكبر في الأصغر؛ وإنه لا يكون مفيدا لما لا حصول له قبل هذا التركيب، وقد كنا علمنا من قبل
And in summary, it may be obtained from this that Burhan al-Inn can give certain knowledge in some places always. As for what has a cause, it does not give perpetual certainty, but rather in what has no cause from this aspect, we say that the mathematician has no certainty in many of the matters attributed to astronomy; because he takes them from the aspect of what is found by observation, for example the apogee of the sun from the aspect that the sun's motion is uneven in speed and slowness across parts of the zodiac; so its slowness is for the apogee and its speed for the perigee, and he does not give the cause in any of this, rather the natural philosopher gives it. It cannot be said that when we see a crafted object we know necessarily that it has a maker, and this knowledge cannot ever depart from us. And it is inference from effect to cause, namely Burhan al-Inn, because we say: the minor premise in this syllogism, if it is particular as in our saying 'this building is well-formed and every well-formed thing has a well-former,' then it is not conceded that its conclusion, which is our saying 'so this building has a well-former,' is among that by which certainty occurs always. And how? If this building decays, then that belief has departed. And if it is universal as in our saying 'every body is composed of matter and form, and every composed thing has a composer,' then we say: the body's being composed of matter and form is either essential to the body or an accidental necessary concomitant of its essence. If it is essential, then the predicate is that it has a composer, not the composer, so the predicate is not the cause; because the cause is the composer, and the composer is not the major term, but rather 'having a composer.' And we do not say that the composer is composed, then the possessor of the composer is either for the composer or not, then the composer from matter and form, whether it constitutes the composer or is accidental. And if the composer in existence itself is first for the composer, then it is for what is under the composer due to the composer as known, so certainty occurs with a cause, and the composer has a cause for the existence of the possessor of the composer. And if it is a part of the possessor of the composer, and the composer is the cause of the composer, then the major term has become clear. Regarding the thing certain in reality, it is not permissible that it be a cause for the middle term; perhaps there is in it a part that is a cause for the middle term. And consideration of the part is other than consideration of the whole. But a speaker may say: if the certain were in need of attaining the cause, then certainty would not occur in what has no cause, as when one makes 'brother' for example as the middle term, yet certainty has occurred; for if we say 'this is a slave, and every slave has a master,' one knows certainly that this one has a master. Likewise, if we know that this number is not even, we know necessarily that it is odd; and that is not other than a cause, for it is not that 'it is not even' is a cause for its being odd, rather the opposite. But we say regarding the first: the attainment of certainty is in the realm of denial, for the relative is not known by the relative but rather with the relative. And if it were so, then there is no certainty of one by the other, for our knowledge that this is a brother is the very knowledge that he has a brother or includes it. And regarding the second: the mentioned exclusion, if it is not certain, then the necessary result from it is not certain; and if it is certain, that is only due to the absence of the opposite of evenness from the mentioned number. And when the absence of the opposite of evenness from it is known, it is not known whether it is odd or not odd, so the knowledge of its being odd is not from that syllogism, and the result must be known from the mentioned syllogism, otherwise the syllogism would not be useful for it, so the syllogism would not be a syllogism. Thus the Master mentioned in al-Shifa', and there is a consideration in it: for the definition by which he defined it, that is the syllogism, does not require that the syllogism be a syllogism only if it were useful for what follows from it. How? And he said in this chapter that the genus is a cause for the species in the predication of the differentia of the genus upon it, just as it is a cause for it in the predication of the genus of the genus upon it. So accordingly, if we say 'every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient,' that would be a syllogism from which it follows that every human is sentient, indeed a Burhan Limma; for the middle term in it is a cause for the affirmation of the major term in the minor term; and it would not be useful for what has no attainment before this composition, and we have already known before.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of Burhan al-Inn (demonstration of existence) and its relationship to certainty and causality. The author begins by summarizing that Burhan al-Inn can sometimes provide perpetual certainty, but not when the subject has a cause in the relevant aspect. He gives the example of astronomy: the mathematician determines the sun's apogee by observation, but does not provide the cause, which is the purview of the natural philosopher. The author then addresses a potential objection: when we see a well-formed building, we know necessarily it has a builder, and this knowledge seems indubitable. He analyzes this as an inference from effect to cause, i.e., Burhan al-Inn. He shows that the certainty depends on whether the minor premise is particular or universal. In the particular case ('this building is well-formed'), if the building decays, the belief disappears, so certainty is not perpetual. In the universal case ('every body is composed of matter and form'), the issue is whether the composition is essential or accidental. If essential, the predicate 'has a composer' is not the composer itself, so the cause is not the major term. He elaborates on the distinction between the composer and having a composer, noting that certainty arises only when the middle term is truly the cause. Next, the author states that the thing that is certain cannot itself be the cause of the middle term; rather, a part of it might be. He then presents an objection: if certainty required cause, we couldn't have certainty in cases without a cause, like the syllogism 'this is a slave, every slave has a master' – we know certainly that this one has a master, but 'slave' is not the cause of 'having a master' (in fact it is relative). Similarly, knowing a number is not even implies it is odd, but the negation of even is not the cause of oddness. The author responds: for the first example, the knowledge that 'this is a slave' already includes knowledge of having a master because of the relational nature; it is not a separate inference. For the second, the certainty of the exclusion (that the number is not even) is either certain or not. If certain, it is only due to the absence of evenness, but from that absence we cannot directly know oddness without additional inference. Hence the syllogism does not provide the knowledge of oddness; if it did, the conclusion would already be known. Thus the syllogism fails to be useful. The author then references Ibn Sina's al-Shifa' and critiques its definition of qiyas, arguing that the definition does not require that a syllogism only be called a syllogism if it provides unknown knowledge. He points out that Ibn Sina himself said that the genus is a cause for the species in predication, giving the example 'every human is an animal, every animal is sentient, therefore every human is sentient' – this is a Burhan Limma (demonstration of why) and it is useful even though the conclusion may be known already? The text ends mid-sentence. Throughout, the author uses Islamic examples: building and builder (indicating a creator), body composed of matter and form (from falsafa), slave and master, number parity.

برهان لِمَ
علة
قياس
يقين

Objection to the Causal Condition of Demonstration and its Refinement
Author refutes an objection to the causal condition in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct the objection that certainty can be obtained without a causal middle term, and explain the author's response using relational and privation examples.

Core Idea
Certainty in demonstration requires the middle term to be a real cause, not a mere correlation.

This passage tackles a potential counterexample to the foundational principle that in a demonstrative syllogism, the middle term is the cause of the major's predication. The objector gives two cases: the slave-owner syllogism and the even-odd inference. The author refines the principle by showing that in these cases, the certainty is either simultaneous or dependent on additional knowledge, rather than derived through a causal middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's challenge
The objector claims certainty can exist without the middle term being a cause. Locate the two examples in the Arabic: the slave-owner syllogism and the even-odd inference.
The objector is testing the necessity of causality for certainty.
2
Analyze the slave-owner example
Note that 'slave' is a relational term (mudaaf). Knowledge of being a slave and having an owner are simultaneous, not sequential cause-effect. The author argues that the certainty here is not from syllogistic causation but from immediate relation.
Relational terms are known together; one does not cause the other.
3
Analyze the even-odd example
The inference 'not even, therefore odd' appears to use privation (istithna'). The author says that certainty about the privation ('not even') requires prior knowledge, and from 'not even' alone we cannot deduce oddness without further assumption. Thus the syllogism does not produce the conclusion from the premises alone.
Privative knowledge alone is insufficient for the conclusion.
4
Understand the author's response
The author rejects both examples by showing that the certainty in each case either is not purely syllogistic (first example) or relies on hidden premises (second example). Therefore, the condition that the middle term must be a cause remains intact.
The response is a refinement, not a refutation of the principle.
5
Reaffirm the principle
The passage ends by stating that for a syllogism to be genuinely demonstrative and yield certain knowledge, the middle term must truly be the cause. This discussion is attributed to Ibn Sina's al-Shifa.
The causal condition is essential for burhan (demonstration).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الشيء المتيقن بالحقيقة لا يجوز أن يكون علة للأوسط؛ عسى أن يكون فيه جزء هو علة للأوسط. واعتبار الجزء غير اعتبار الكل. لكن لقائل أن يقول لو كان المتيقن مفتقرًا إلى حصول العلة لما حصل التيقن فيما لا علة فيه كما إذا جعل الأخ مثلا حدا أوسط، وقد حصل التيقن؛ فإنا إذا قلنا هذا عبد، وكل عبد فله مالك يعلم يقينًا أن هذا له مالك. وكذلك إذا علمنا أن هذا العدد ليس بزوج علمنا بالضرورة أنه فرد؛ وليس ذلك غير علة فإنه ليس إنه ليس بزوج علة لكونه فردا بل الأمر على العكس. لكنا نقول في الأول إن حصول التيقن في حيز المنع فإن المضاف لا يعلم بالمضاف بل مع المضاف. ولو كان كذلك فلا تيقن أحدهما بالآخر فإن علمنا بأن هذا أخ هو نفس علمنا بأن له أخا أو يشتمل عليه. وفي الثاني إن الاستثناء المذكور إن لم يكن يقينيا لم تكن النتيجة اللازمة عنه يقينية وإن كان يقينيا؛ وليس ذلك إلا لفقدان ضد الزوجية عن العدد المذكور. ومتى علم فقدان ضد الزوجية عنه لا يعلم أنه فرد أو غير فرد فلا يكون العلم بكونه فردًا من ذلك القياس والنتيجة يجب أن تكون معلومة من القياس المذكور وإلا لم يكن القياس مفيدًا إياها فلا يكون القياس قياسا هكذا ذكره الشيخ في الشفاء
Regarding the thing that is certain in reality, it is not permissible for it to be a cause for the middle term; perhaps it contains a part that is a cause for the middle term. And considering the part is not considering the whole. But one might say: if the certain thing were dependent on obtaining a cause, then certainty would not be obtained in what has no cause, as when one makes 'brother' for example a middle term, and certainty is obtained. For when we say 'this is a slave, and every slave has an owner', one knows with certainty that this has an owner. Likewise, if we know that this number is not even, we know necessarily that it is odd; and that is not other than a cause, for its not being even is not a cause for its being odd, rather the opposite. But we say regarding the first: that obtaining certainty is in the realm of prevention, for the relative is not known by the relative but with the relative. And if it were so, then there is no certainty of one by the other, for our knowledge that this is a brother is the same as our knowledge that he has a brother or includes it. And regarding the second: the mentioned exception, if it is not certain, then the necessary conclusion from it is not certain; and if it is certain, that is only due to the absence of the opposite of evenness from the mentioned number. And when the absence of the opposite of evenness from it is known, it is not known whether it is odd or not odd, so knowledge of its being odd does not come from that syllogism, and the result must be known from the mentioned syllogism, otherwise the syllogism would not be useful for it, so it would not be a syllogism. Thus mentioned the Shaykh in al-Shifa.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the claim that the middle term in a demonstrative syllogism must be a cause of the major's predication of the minor. The objector argues that in some cases certainty is achieved without an explicit cause. Two examples are given: first, the syllogism 'This is a slave; every slave has an owner' yields certain knowledge that this has an owner, yet being a slave is not the cause of having an owner—rather, the relation is symmetrical. Second, knowing that a number is not even necessarily entails it is odd, but the absence of evenness is not the cause of oddness; it is merely the lack of its opposite. The author responds by distinguishing: in the first case, relational terms (like brother) are known simultaneously, not one from the other, so certainty about one implies the other but not by causation. In the second case, the certainty that the number is not even itself requires prior certainty about its evenness, and from the absence of evenness alone one cannot deduce oddness without additional knowledge; thus the conclusion does not follow from the syllogism as presented. Therefore, the condition stands: for a syllogism to produce genuine certainty, the middle term must be a cause. The passage ends by attributing this discussion to Ibn Sina's al-Shifa.

Critique of Ibn Sina's Definition of Syllogism
Author critiques definition of qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's objection to Ibn Sina's definition of syllogism and evaluate the counter-argument about general usefulness.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism may produce already-known truths.

Ibn Sina defined the syllogism in a way that, according to our author, seems to require that it produces new knowledge. The author shows that even a syllogism whose conclusion is known beforehand can be considered useful in a general sense. This lesson examines that critique and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author’s statement of Ibn Sina’s definition
Find the phrase 'التعريف الذي عرفه به أي القياس' and note what the definition is said to imply.
The definition is not quoted verbatim; the author critiques its implication.
2
Identify the example given
The author gives the syllogism: 'Every human is an animal; every animal is sentient; therefore every human is sentient.' Recognize that the conclusion was known before.
3
Understand the problem
The syllogism does not produce new knowledge; it merely makes explicit what was implicit. The author questions whether the definition requires newness.
The author says 'لا يكون مفيدا لما لا حصول له قبل هذا التركيب'.
4
Consider the proposed response
The author suggests that 'useful' can be taken in a general sense, and this syllogism is useful in general, even if not individually new.
The phrase 'أن يكون مفيدا في الجملة' is key.
5
Evaluate the critique’s force
Assess whether the response fully satisfies the definitional requirement. Does 'useful in general' preserve the definition's intention?
The author seems to accept this response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر فإن التعريف الذي عرفه به أي القياس لا يقتضي أن يكون القياس إنما يكون قياسا إن لو كان مفيدا لما يلزم منه كيف وإنه قال في هذا الفصل إن الجنس علة للنوع في حمل فصل الجنس عليه كما هو علة له في حمل جنس الجنس عليه. فعلى هذا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس كان ذلك قياسا يلزم منه أن كل إنسان حساس بل برهان لِمَ؛ إذ الأوسط فيه علة لثبوت الأكبر في الأصغر؛ وإنه لا يكون مفيدا لما لا حصول له قبل هذا التركيب، وقد كنا علمنا من قبل أن كل إنسان حساس. ولا يبعد أن يقال في الجواب أن المراد بكونه مفيدًا أن يكون مفيدا في الجملة؛ وهذا من جملة ما يكون مفيدا في الجملة دون ذلك. وأما قياس الخلف فإنه إنما يفيد برهان الإن لأنه يبين صدق شيء بكذب نقيضه لإيجابه المحال ؛ وهذه كلها بأمور خارجة لكنه في قوته أن يعود إلى المستقيم الذي يكون الأوسط فيه علة لثبوت الأكبر للأصغر أو سلبه عنه فيكون منه ما في قوته أن يكون برهان لِمَ؛ ويعد هذا كله فيجب أن تعلم أنه لا يكفي في اليقين التام الدائم أن يكون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر وقتا ما بل أن يكون علة له دائمًا أي يكون حاصلا للأصغر دائما إما لأن الأصغر نقيضه لذاته اقتضاء النوع لخواصه، أو لأن الأصغر من خواص الأوسط وإلا لا يكون ما هو علة له يقيناً دائما. ويجب أن تعلم أيضًا أن برهان اللّم ليس هو الذي يعطي العلة قريبة بالفعل فقط بل هو برهان لِمَ؛ وإن لم يفعل ذلك بعد أن يكون إنما يبين ما يبين بالعلة وكان شمل البيان إلى العلل. وأما أنه إذا كان يعطي العلة البعيدة من الحد الأكبر فذلك لا يكون برهان لم فهو أن يكون مثلا الحد الأصغر (ج) والأوسط (ب) والأكبر «أ» لكن «ب» ليس علة قريبة لكون (ج) أ» إنما هو علة لذلك لأجل أنه (د) . وإذا أعطينا إن «ب أ»، فذلك إما أن يكون يقينا لنا أو مقبولاً عندنا أو لا يكون مقبولا، فإن لم يكن مقبولا لم يكن هذا القياس برهانًا فضلا عن أن يكون برهان إنَّ. وإن كان مقبولا لا من جهة د لم يكن يقيناً بأن كل «ب» يقيناً تاما، وكان إنتاجا إن كل «ج أ» لأنه «ب» غير متيقن يقينا دائما. فأما إذا تقدم العلم بأن كل ب أ» لأجل إن «د أ» أو تقدم فعلم ذلك؛ فإن البرهان حينئذ لا يكون برهان إن مجردًا.
And there is consideration. For the definition by which he defined it, i.e., the syllogism, does not require that the syllogism be a syllogism only if it is useful for what follows from it. Moreover, he said in this chapter that the genus is a cause for the species in predicating the differentia of the genus, just as it is a cause for it in predicating the genus of the genus. Therefore, if we say 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient', that is a syllogism that entails that every human is sentient, indeed a demonstration per se; for the middle term is a cause for the affirmation of the major in the minor. And it is not useful for what had no existence before this composition, and we already knew before that every human is sentient. And it is not far to say in response that what is meant by its being useful is that it is useful in general; and this is among the things that are useful in general without that. As for the syllogism of contradiction (qiyas al-khalf), it only benefits demonstration that (burhan inn) because it shows the truth of something by the falsehood of its contrary, necessitating absurdity. All these are by external matters, but it has the power to return to the straight syllogism in which the middle term is a cause for the affirmation of the major for the minor or its negation, so from it comes what has the power to be a demonstration per se. All this being counted, you must know that it is not sufficient for complete perpetual certainty that the middle term be a cause for the existence of the major in the minor at some time, but rather it must be a cause for it perpetually, i.e., it must belong to the minor perpetually: either because the minor is its opposite by essence, by the nature's requiring its properties, or because the minor is among the properties of the middle term. Otherwise, what is a cause for it would not be certain perpetually. And you must also know that demonstration per se is not that which gives the proximate cause actually only, but it is a demonstration per se even if it does not do that, as long as it explains what it explains by the cause, and the explanation encompassed the causes. As for when it gives the remote cause of the major term, then that is not a demonstration per se. For example, let the minor term be (j), the middle (b), and the major 'a', but 'b' is not a proximate cause for (j) being 'a'; rather it is a cause for that because of (d). And if we are given that 'b is a', then that is either certain to us, or accepted by us, or not accepted. If it is not accepted, then this syllogism is not a demonstration at all, let alone a demonstration that. And if it is accepted but not from the perspective of d, then it is not certain that every b is entirely certain, and it produces that every j is a because b is not perpetually certain. But if knowledge preceded that every b is a because of the fact that d is a, or it preceded and thus was known, then the demonstration in that case is not a pure demonstration that.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the definition of syllogism and the characteristics of demonstrative certainty. The author begins with a critique: the definition of syllogism given by Ibn Sina in al-Shifa does not require that the syllogism produce new knowledge. For example, the syllogism 'Every human is an animal; every animal is sentient' yields the conclusion 'Every human is sentient' already known. The author suggests that usefulness can be understood generally. Then the author discusses qiyas al-khalf (syllogism by contradiction), which proves something by showing its opposite leads to absurdity; this is a برهان إن (demonstration that) but has the potential to be converted to a برهان لم (demonstration per se). The main condition for perpetual certainty (اليقين التام الدائم) is that the middle term must be a cause not just at some time, but perpetually. This happens either because the minor term entails the property by its essence (e.g., the nature of the species requires its properties) or because the minor is a property of the middle term. The author then clarifies that برهان لم does not require giving the proximate cause; it can give a remote cause and still be برهان لم as long as the explanation is based on causality. However, if the middle term is not a proximate cause but rather a remote cause mediated by another term (د), and that mediation is not fully known, the demonstration may not achieve full certainty and may reduce to برهان إن. Finally, the author notes that if the major premise (كل ب أ) is known through a prior cause (د), then the demonstration is not a pure برهان إن.

Key Terms
قياس
برهان لم
برهان إن
مفيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the flaw in Ibn Sina's definition of syllogism?
A) It allows circular arguments.
B) It may exclude syllogisms that produce already-known conclusions.
C) It requires the middle term to be a cause.
D) It is too broad and includes invalid forms.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism can be valid and useful even if its conclusion is already known; usefulness can be understood in a general sense.

Qiyas al-Khalf and Perpetual Certainty
Explains qiyas al-khalf and perpetual certainty condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify qiyas al-khalf as a demonstration that (burhan inna) and state the condition for perpetual certainty (al-yaqīn al-tāmm al-dā'im).

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves truth via absurdity; certainty requires the middle term to be a perpetual cause.

The author turns to qiyas al-khalf, the syllogism by contradiction, and classifies it as a demonstration that (burhan inna). He notes that it has the potential to be converted into a demonstration per se (burhan lima). He then lays down a crucial condition for complete perpetual certainty: the middle term must be a cause not merely at some time, but perpetually. This lesson explores these concepts.

Guided Path
1
Understand qiyas al-khalf
Qiyas al-khalf proves a proposition by showing that its negation leads to an absurdity. Identify its structure.
It is a type of burhan inna because it shows that something is true without giving the causal reason.
2
Note its potential conversion
The author says qiyas al-khalf can be 'returned to the straight syllogism' where the middle term is a cause, potentially becoming burhan lima.
The conversion requires identifying the middle term as a cause.
3
Extract the condition for perpetual certainty
The author states that for complete perpetual certainty (اليقين التام الدائم), the middle term must be a cause for the major's predication at all times, not just sometimes.
The phrase 'دائما' is emphasized.
4
Learn the two ways the condition is met
Either the minor term (subject) by its essence entails the property, or the minor is a property of the middle term.
These are the two ways the middle term can be a perpetual cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر فإن التعريف الذي عرفه به أي القياس لا يقتضي أن يكون القياس إنما يكون قياسا إن لو كان مفيدا لما يلزم منه كيف وإنه قال في هذا الفصل إن الجنس علة للنوع في حمل فصل الجنس عليه كما هو علة له في حمل جنس الجنس عليه. فعلى هذا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس كان ذلك قياسا يلزم منه أن كل إنسان حساس بل برهان لِمَ؛ إذ الأوسط فيه علة لثبوت الأكبر في الأصغر؛ وإنه لا يكون مفيدا لما لا حصول له قبل هذا التركيب، وقد كنا علمنا من قبل أن كل إنسان حساس. ولا يبعد أن يقال في الجواب أن المراد بكونه مفيدًا أن يكون مفيدا في الجملة؛ وهذا من جملة ما يكون مفيدا في الجملة دون ذلك. وأما قياس الخلف فإنه إنما يفيد برهان الإن لأنه يبين صدق شيء بكذب نقيضه لإيجابه المحال ؛ وهذه كلها بأمور خارجة لكنه في قوته أن يعود إلى المستقيم الذي يكون الأوسط فيه علة لثبوت الأكبر للأصغر أو سلبه عنه فيكون منه ما في قوته أن يكون برهان لِمَ؛ ويعد هذا كله فيجب أن تعلم أنه لا يكفي في اليقين التام الدائم أن يكون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر وقتا ما بل أن يكون علة له دائمًا أي يكون حاصلا للأصغر دائما إما لأن الأصغر نقيضه لذاته اقتضاء النوع لخواصه، أو لأن الأصغر من خواص الأوسط وإلا لا يكون ما هو علة له يقيناً دائما. ويجب أن تعلم أيضًا أن برهان اللّم ليس هو الذي يعطي العلة قريبة بالفعل فقط بل هو برهان لِمَ؛ وإن لم يفعل ذلك بعد أن يكون إنما يبين ما يبين بالعلة وكان شمل البيان إلى العلل. وأما أنه إذا كان يعطي العلة البعيدة من الحد الأكبر فذلك لا يكون برهان لم فهو أن يكون مثلا الحد الأصغر (ج) والأوسط (ب) والأكبر «أ» لكن «ب» ليس علة قريبة لكون (ج) أ» إنما هو علة لذلك لأجل أنه (د) . وإذا أعطينا إن «ب أ»، فذلك إما أن يكون يقينا لنا أو مقبولاً عندنا أو لا يكون مقبولا، فإن لم يكن مقبولا لم يكن هذا القياس برهانًا فضلا عن أن يكون برهان إنَّ. وإن كان مقبولا لا من جهة د لم يكن يقيناً بأن كل «ب» يقيناً تاما، وكان إنتاجا إن كل «ج أ» لأنه «ب» غير متيقن يقينا دائما. فأما إذا تقدم العلم بأن كل ب أ» لأجل إن «د أ» أو تقدم فعلم ذلك؛ فإن البرهان حينئذ لا يكون برهان إن مجردًا.
And there is consideration. For the definition by which he defined it, i.e., the syllogism, does not require that the syllogism be a syllogism only if it is useful for what follows from it. Moreover, he said in this chapter that the genus is a cause for the species in predicating the differentia of the genus, just as it is a cause for it in predicating the genus of the genus. Therefore, if we say 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient', that is a syllogism that entails that every human is sentient, indeed a demonstration per se; for the middle term is a cause for the affirmation of the major in the minor. And it is not useful for what had no existence before this composition, and we already knew before that every human is sentient. And it is not far to say in response that what is meant by its being useful is that it is useful in general; and this is among the things that are useful in general without that. As for the syllogism of contradiction (qiyas al-khalf), it only benefits demonstration that (burhan inn) because it shows the truth of something by the falsehood of its contrary, necessitating absurdity. All these are by external matters, but it has the power to return to the straight syllogism in which the middle term is a cause for the affirmation of the major for the minor or its negation, so from it comes what has the power to be a demonstration per se. All this being counted, you must know that it is not sufficient for complete perpetual certainty that the middle term be a cause for the existence of the major in the minor at some time, but rather it must be a cause for it perpetually, i.e., it must belong to the minor perpetually: either because the minor is its opposite by essence, by the nature's requiring its properties, or because the minor is among the properties of the middle term. Otherwise, what is a cause for it would not be certain perpetually. And you must also know that demonstration per se is not that which gives the proximate cause actually only, but it is a demonstration per se even if it does not do that, as long as it explains what it explains by the cause, and the explanation encompassed the causes. As for when it gives the remote cause of the major term, then that is not a demonstration per se. For example, let the minor term be (j), the middle (b), and the major 'a', but 'b' is not a proximate cause for (j) being 'a'; rather it is a cause for that because of (d). And if we are given that 'b is a', then that is either certain to us, or accepted by us, or not accepted. If it is not accepted, then this syllogism is not a demonstration at all, let alone a demonstration that. And if it is accepted but not from the perspective of d, then it is not certain that every b is entirely certain, and it produces that every j is a because b is not perpetually certain. But if knowledge preceded that every b is a because of the fact that d is a, or it preceded and thus was known, then the demonstration in that case is not a pure demonstration that.

Proximate and Remote Causes in Burhan Lima
Discusses proximate vs remote cause in burhan limā

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why burhan limā can involve remote causes and distinguish when a demonstration becomes pure burhan inna.

Core Idea
A demonstration per se can use remote causes; only when the causal chain is unknown does it reduce to demonstration that.

The author refines the common understanding that demonstration per se (burhan limā) must give the proximate cause. He argues that if the explanation is based on causality, even a remote cause suffices, as long as the causal relation is known. However, if the middle term's causal role depends on an intermediate term (د) and that relation is not fully known, the demonstration may become a pure burhan inna. This lesson unpacks the example with variables.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's claim about burhan limā
The author says burhan limā is not restricted to proximate causes; it can give remote causes as long as the explanation is causal.
This is a departure from some later logicians.
2
Analyze the example with variables
The author sets up: minor (ج), middle (ب), major (أ). He posits that ب is not the proximate cause for ج being أ; instead, it is a cause because of another term (د).
The variables are placeholders for terms in a syllogism.
3
Examine the epistemic status of the premise 'كل ب أ'
The author considers three possibilities: the premise is certain, accepted, or not accepted. If it is accepted but not known through د, then it is not certain and the demonstration is not burhan limā.
Certainty requires knowledge of the causal chain.
4
Determine when the demonstration becomes pure burhan inna
If the premise 'كل ب أ' is known only through acceptance (not through د), then the syllogism is a burhan inna. If it is known through د, it can be burhan limā.
The author says 'فإن البرهان حينئذ لا يكون برهان إن مجردًا'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is consideration. For the definition by which he defined it, i.e., the syllogism, does not require that the syllogism be a syllogism only if it is useful for what follows from it. Moreover, he said in this chapter that the genus is a cause for the species in predicating the differentia of the genus, just as it is a cause for it in predicating the genus of the genus. All these are by external matters, but it has the power to return to the straight syllogism in which the middle term is a cause for the affirmation of the major for the minor or its negation, so from it comes what has the power to be a demonstration per se. Otherwise, what is a cause for it would not be certain perpetually. And you must also know that demonstration per se is not that which gives the proximate cause actually only, but it is a demonstration per se even if it does not do that, as long as it explains what it explains by the cause, and the explanation encompassed the causes. As for when it gives the remote cause of the major term, then that is not a demonstration per se. ?

Determine whether this is a burhan lima or burhan inna according to the author's conditions, assuming the premise 'the word of God is uncreated' is known through revelation (accepted) or through rational demonstration (certain due to cause).

Burhan al-Khalf and Permanent Causation for Certainty
Author discusses burhan al-khalf and permanent causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a reductio syllogism can be converted to a direct causal syllogism and why the middle term must be a permanent cause for certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Burhan al-khalf proves truth via absurdity but can be recast as a causal demonstration if the middle term is a permanent cause.

In this passage, the author explains the nature of burhan al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) and its potential to become a direct demonstration. He then emphasizes a crucial condition for demonstrative certainty: the middle term must be a permanent cause of the major for the minor, not merely a cause at some time. This lesson will walk you through these concepts and their logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify burhan al-khalf
Understand that it proves a proposition by showing its negation leads to absurdity. Notice that the author says it is essentially a demonstration of fact (burhan anna) but can be transformed into a demonstration of reasoned fact (burhan limm) if the middle term is a cause.
2
Understand the conversion to a direct syllogism
The author says burhan al-khalf can revert to a straight syllogism where the middle term is a cause. This means the reductio can be re-expressed as a categorical syllogism.
3
Recognize the condition for permanent certainty
The author states that the middle term must be a cause permanently, i.e., it must always obtain for the minor. This is not satisfied by occasional causation.
4
Identify the two ways permanence is ensured
Either the minor essence necessitates the major (like species-property), or the minor is a property of the middle term. Without this, certainty is not permanent.
