⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن كل إنسان حساس. ولا يبعد أن يقال في الجواب أن المراد بكونه مفيدًا أن يكون مفيدا في الجملة؛ وهذا من جملة ما يكون مفيدا في الجملة دون ذلك. وأما قياس الخلف فإنه إنما يفيد برهان الإن لأنه يبين صدق شيء بكذب نقيضه لإيجابه المحال ؛ وهذه كلها بأمور خارجة لكنه في قوته أن يعود إلى المستقيم الذي يكون الأوسط فيه علة لثبوت الأكبر للأصغر أو سلبه عنه فيكون منه ما في قوته أن يكون برهان لِمَ؛ ويعد هذا كله فيجب أن تعلم أنه لا يكفي في اليقين التام الدائم أن يكون الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر وقتا ما بل أن يكون علة له دائمًا أي يكون حاصلا للأصغر دائما إما لأن الأصغر نقيضه لذاته اقتضاء النوع لخواصه، أو لأن الأصغر من خواص الأوسط وإلا لا يكون ما هو علة له يقيناً دائما. ويجب أن تعلم أيضًا أن برهان اللّم ليس هو الذي يعطي العلة قريبة بالفعل فقط بل هو برهان لِمَ؛ وإن لم يفعل ذلك بعد أن يكون إنما يبين ما يبين بالعلة وكان شمل البيان (۱) إلى العلل. وأما أنه إذا كان يعطي العلة البعيدة من الحد الأكبر فذلك لا يكون برهان لم فهو أن يكون مثلا الحد الأصغر (ج) والأوسط (ب) والأكبر «أ» لكن «ب» ليس علة قريبة لكون (ج) أ» إنما هو علة لذلك لأجل أنه (د) . وإذا أعطينا إن «ب أ»، فذلك إما أن يكون يقينا لنا أو مقبولاً عندنا أو لا يكون مقبولا، فإن لم يكن مقبولا لم يكن هذا القياس برهانًا فضلا عن أن يكون برهان إنَّ. وإن كان مقبولا لا من جهة د لم يكن يقيناً بأن كل «ب» يقيناً تاما، وكان إنتاجا إن كل «ج أ» لأنه «ب» غير متيقن يقينا دائما. فأما إذا تقدم العلم بأن كل ب أ» لأجل إن «د أ» أو تقدم فعلم ذلك؛ فإن البرهان حينئذ لا يكون برهان إن مجردًا. (۱) مفترض. وصورته وكان شمال السان».
That every human is sentient. And it is not far-fetched to say in response that what is meant by it being useful is that it is useful in a general sense; and this is among the things that are useful in a general sense without that. As for the syllogism of reductio, it only yields the demonstration of fact because it shows the truth of something by the falsity of its contradictory due to its necessitating an absurdity; and all these are by external matters, but it has the potential to revert to the straight syllogism in which the middle term is a cause for the affirmation of the major for the minor or its negation, and from it there can be what has the potential to be a demonstration of reasoned fact. And all this is considered, so you must know that for complete permanent certainty it is not sufficient that the middle term be a cause for the existence of the major in the minor at a time, rather it must be a cause for it permanently, i.e. it must obtain for the minor always, either because the minor necessitates it by its essence, as the species necessitates its properties, or because the minor is among the properties of the middle, otherwise what is a cause for it is not permanently certain. And you must also know that the demonstration of reasoned fact is not only that which gives the proximate cause actually, rather it is a demonstration of 'why?' even if it does not do that, as long as it explains what it explains by the cause and its explanation encompasses the causes. As for when it gives the remote cause of the major, that is not a demonstration of 'why?', for example: the minor (J), the middle (B), and the major (A), but B is not a proximate cause for J being A, rather it is a cause for that because of (D). And if we are given that 'B is A', then that is either certain for us or accepted by us or not accepted. If it is not accepted, then this syllogism is not a demonstration, let alone being a demonstration of fact. And if it is accepted not from the aspect of D, then it is not certain that every B is completely certain, and it produces that 'every J is A' because B is not permanently certain. But if knowledge preceded that 'every B is A' because of 'every D is A' or preceded, then that knowledge; then the demonstration at that point is not a pure demonstration of fact. (1) Presumed. And its form was 'and the north of the speech'.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of demonstrative certainty and the distinction between burhan limm (demonstration of the reasoned fact) and burhan anna (demonstration of fact). The author begins by addressing an objection about what it means for a syllogism to be 'useful', then moves to burhan al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum), noting that it proves the truth of a proposition by showing its contradictory leads to an absurdity, and that such a syllogism can potentially be recast as a direct categorical syllogism where the middle term is a cause. The author then emphasizes a critical point: for permanent certainty, the middle term must not merely be a cause at some time but must be a permanent cause of the major for the minor. This permanence arises either because the minor essence necessitates the major (like species and its properties) or because the minor is a property of the middle term. Without this, certainty is not permanent. Next, the author clarifies that burhan limm is not restricted to giving the proximate cause; it suffices that the explanation proceeds via a cause, even if remote. However, if the middle term is a remote cause, the syllogism may not qualify as burhan limm but rather as something else. The author illustrates with variables: J (minor), B (middle), A (major), where B is not the proximate cause of J being A but is a cause via D. The discussion then turns to the epistemic status of the premise 'B is A'. If it is not accepted, the syllogism is no demonstration at all. If accepted but not based on D, then the certainty is incomplete, because the universal premise is not certain. Only if we already know that 'B is A' because of D (i.e., via a higher cause) does the demonstration become something other than a mere demonstration of fact. Throughout, the passage emphasizes the necessity of causal connection for demonstrative certainty.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف
برهان لم
برهان إن
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for a demonstration to produce permanent certainty?
A) The middle term must be a cause at some time.
B) The middle term must be a permanent cause of the major for the minor.
C) The syllogism must be burhan al-khalf.
D) The major must be a property of the minor.

Key Takeaway
Burhan al-khalf can be converted to a direct causal syllogism, but permanent certainty requires the middle term to be a permanent cause, achieved either by essence or by being a property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a burhan al-khalf for the statement 'Every human is mortal' and then convert it into a direct categorical syllogism where the middle term is a cause. Verify whether the middle term is a permanent cause.
?
Explain why the middle term in the example is or is not a permanent cause.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
10 min
Distinguishing Burhan Limm from Burhan Anna
Author discusses burhan limm vs anna and remote causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between demonstration of reasoned fact (limm) and demonstration of fact (anna) based on the causal role of the middle term, and identify whether a given demonstration gives a proximate or remote cause.

Core Idea
Burhan limm gives the cause (even if remote), while burhan anna only gives a sign; the epistemic status of premises determines whether it is a demonstration at all.

The passage clarifies that burhan limm is not limited to proximate causation; it suffices that the explanation proceeds via a cause. However, if the middle term is a remote cause, the demonstration may not qualify as burhan limm. Additionally, the author discusses the epistemic condition of the premise 'B is A' – it must be certain or accepted based on a higher cause to yield a pure demonstration. This lesson unpacks these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Understand what makes a demonstration burhan limm
It must explain via a cause. The author says it is not only that which gives the proximate cause; even a remote cause qualifies, as long as the explanation is causal.
2
Recognize the boundary
If the middle term is a remote cause (e.g., B is a cause of A because of D), then the syllogism is not burhan limm because the demonstration does not show the proximate cause. The example uses variables J, B, A, D.
3
Examine the epistemic condition of the premise
The premise 'B is A' must be accepted or certain. If not accepted, the syllogism is not a demonstration. If accepted but not based on D, then the universal premise is not fully certain.
4
Determine when a demonstration is pure burhan anna
Only when the premise 'B is A' is known from a higher cause (D) does it become more than a mere burhan anna.

How the Specific is a Cause for the Generic in Demonstration
Author explains how the specific (al-akhass) serves as a cause for inferring the generic (al-a'am) and distinguishes genera/materials from forms/differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the specific term (al-akhass) contains the constitutive causes of the generic term (al-a'am) and distinguish between genera/materials and forms/differentiae.

Core Idea
The specific is not just a subset—it is the cause of the generic.

In this passage, the author explores the relationship between the specific and the generic in demonstration. The specific (e.g., 'human') contains the material and formal causes that constitute the generic (e.g., 'animal'). Understanding this causal dependence is crucial for constructing sound definitions and demonstrations in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
In the author's example, identify the specific (al-akhass) and the generic (al-a'am). Answer: human (specific) and animal (generic).
2
Extract the constitutive causes
The author says bodiliness and sensitivity are two causes for animal. Identify what these correspond to: bodiliness (material cause) and sensitivity (formal cause/soul).
3
Understand the causal relationship
The specific (human) must have both causes. If human were not a body, it could not be animal. If not sensitive, it could not be animal. Therefore, the specific's properties cause the generic to be predicated of it.
4
Distinguish genera from materials
The author intends to clarify the difference between genera (like animal) and materials (like body), and between forms (like soul) and differentiae (like sensitivity). Note: the passage breaks off, but the distinction is that the generic is a composite of matter and form, while the specific adds a differentia.
The passage is incomplete; the full argument continues beyond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في بيان كيفية كون الأخص علة لإنتاج الأعم على ما دون الأخص وإبانة الفرق بين الأجناس والمواد وبين الصور والفصول فنقول: إن الحيوان كيف يكون سببًا لكون الإنسان جسمًا على ما ادعينا؛ وإنه لم يكن جسما لم يكن حيوانا. وكيف يكون سببًا لكون الإنسان حساسا؛ وإنه ما لم يكن حساسًا لم يكن حيوانا؛ لأن الجسمية والحساسية سببان لوجود الحيوان. وإذا كان معنى الجسم ينضم إلى معنى النفس فيكون مجموعهما لا واحد منهما حيوانا؛ فكيف يحمل الجسم على الحيوان فيكون كما يحمل
Section: On explaining how the specific is a cause for the production of the generic relative to what is below the specific, and clarifying the difference between genera and materials and between forms and differentiae. So we say: How is animal (hayawan) a cause for human (insan) being a body, as we have claimed? And if it were not a body, it would not be an animal. And how is it a cause for human being sensitive? For if it were not sensitive, it would not be an animal; because bodiliness and sensitivity are two causes for the existence of animal. And if the meaning of body joins with the meaning of soul, then their combination—not one of them alone—is animal; so how can body be predicated of animal so that it is like predicating...
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage begins a new section (fasl) that aims to explain how the more specific term (al-akhass) serves as a cause for producing the more general term (al-a'am) in a demonstration, specifically regarding what is below the specific. The author also intends to clarify the distinction between genera (ajnas) and materials (mawadd) on the one hand, and forms (suwar) and differentiae (fusul) on the other. He starts with the example: How is 'animal' (hayawan) a cause for 'human' (insan) being a body? The reasoning is that if human were not a body, it could not be an animal. Similarly, how is animal a cause for human being sensitive? Without sensitivity, human would not be an animal. The explanation is that bodiliness (jismiyya) and sensitivity (hassasiyya) are the two causes for the existence of animal (i.e., the genus animal is composed of body and soul). When the meaning of body combines with the meaning of soul, their sum constitutes animal—neither one alone is animal. The text breaks off as the author begins to discuss how body can be predicated of animal. The passage cuts off mid-argument, but the direction is clear: the specific (human) contains the generic (animal) and its constitutive causes, so that in a demonstration the specific can serve as a cause for attributing the generic properties to the subject.

Conditions of Demonstrative Premises
Author enumerates conditions for premises in demonstration: priority, truth, appropriateness, and self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five conditions for demonstrative premises and apply them to evaluate whether a given syllogism constitutes a demonstration.

Core Idea
Demonstrative premises must be true, prior, appropriate, and ultimately self-evident.

In Islamic kalam and fiqh, establishing certainty requires demonstration (burhan). This passage lays out the exact conditions that make a premise suitable for demonstration. Without meeting these conditions, even a true argument may not be demonstrative. The author contrasts demonstration with dialectical persuasion and with inappropriate premises that lead to true conclusions but are not proper demonstrations.

Guided Path
1
Extract the conditions listed
Read the Arabic passage and list the conditions that demonstrative premises must satisfy according to the author. Identify words like 'تقدم بالذات' (essential priority), 'صادقة' (truth), 'مناسبة' (appropriateness), 'أولية بنفسها' (self-evident).
There are four explicit conditions: priority, truth, appropriateness, and self-evident first principles.
2
Understand each condition in context
For each condition, find how the author justifies it. Priority: premises are causes of the conclusion. Truth: to produce a true conclusion. Appropriateness: premises must belong to the same science as the conclusion. Self-evidence: ultimate premises must be known without proof.
Self-evidence is required for the first principles of the demonstration.
3
Distinguish from non-demonstrative arguments
The author gives two counterexamples: dialectical persuasion where false premises yield true conclusions, and the doctor's argument using a geometric premise. Explain why these are not demonstrations: one lacks truth, the other lacks appropriateness.
Both cases produce true conclusions but violate conditions.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Check each condition: Are the premises prior? (Yes, they are causes of the conclusion.) True? (The first premise is debated, but if accepted as self-evident, it works.) Appropriate? (Both are from the same science of kalam.)
This illustrates how the conditions are used to validate a demonstration.
5
Evaluate a potential misuse
Now consider a jurist using a premise from physics to argue a fiqh ruling. For example, 'Water boils at 100°C; therefore, if you cook in boiling water, the food becomes permissible.' Is this a demonstration? Which condition does it violate?
The premise is from physics, not fiqh, violating appropriateness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في اعتبار مقدمات البرهان من جهة تقدمها وعليتها وسائر شرائطها ولما كانت مقدمات البرهان عللا للنتيجة، وللعلل تقدم بالذات كان لمقدمات البرهان تقدم على النتيجة بالذات. وكذلك في المعرفة والزمان لأن النتيجة لا تعرف إلا بها؛ ويجب أن تكون صادقة حتى تنتج الصادق، ومناسبة للنتيجة داخلة في العلم الذي فيه النتيجة. وأن تكون أوائل براهينها من مقدمات أولية بنفسها فإنها إذا لم تكن كذلك لم تكن المقدمات برهانية. وكثيرًا ما تؤخذ في الإقناع الجدلي كواذب مشهورة ينتج منها صادق، وكثيرًا ما تؤخذ صوادق غير مناسبة في قياسات نتائجها صوادق مثل احتجاج الطبيب أن الجراحات المستديرة أعسر بروا لأن المستدير أكبر إحاطة؛ وهذه مقدمة هندسية غير مناسبة لم يكن برهان، والأقدم والأعرف من المقدمات هي الاشياء
Chapter: On considering the premises of demonstration with respect to their priority, causality, and other conditions. Since the premises of demonstration are causes of the conclusion, and causes have priority in essence, the premises of demonstration have priority over the conclusion in essence. Similarly in knowledge and time, because the conclusion is not known except through them. And they must be true in order to produce truth, and appropriate to the conclusion, falling within the science to which the conclusion belongs. And the first principles of its demonstrations must be self-evident premises, because if they are not so, the premises are not demonstrative. Often in dialectical persuasion, commonly accepted falsehoods are taken which produce true conclusions, and often true but inappropriate premises are taken in syllogisms whose conclusions are true, such as the doctor's argument that circular wounds are harder to heal because the circular has greater circumference; this is a geometric premise that is not appropriate, and it is not a demonstration. And the most prior and best known of premises are things...
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and an initial listing of the conditions required for premises in a demonstration (burhan). The author states that demonstrative premises are causes of the conclusion, so they must be essentially prior to the conclusion, both in essence and in knowledge and time, since the conclusion is known through them. They must be true to produce a true conclusion, and they must be appropriate, meaning they belong to the same science as the conclusion. Furthermore, the ultimate premises of a demonstration must be self-evident first principles (awwaliyya); otherwise, the premises are not truly demonstrative. The author contrasts this with dialectical persuasion, where commonly accepted falsehoods can yield true conclusions, and with cases where true but inappropriate premises are used, such as a doctor using a geometric principle (that a circle has the greatest circumference) to argue that circular wounds heal more slowly—this is not a demonstration because the premise is from geometry, not medicine. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing with examples of truly prior and known premises.

التقدم بالذات
أولية بنفسها
الإقناع الجدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the premise is false.
B) Because the premise is not appropriate to the science of medicine.
C) Because the conclusion is false.
D) Because the argument is dialectical.

Key Takeaway
The strength of a demonstration depends not only on the truth of its premises but also on their priority, appropriateness, and ultimate self-evidence.

Two Senses of Priority in Demonstrative Premises
Conditions of demonstration and order of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish what is prior and better known by nature (inda al-tabi'a) from what is prior and better known by us (indana), and determine the correct starting point for a demonstrative proof.

Core Idea
Demonstration begins with what is prior by nature and better known to us: the genera.

In constructing a demonstrative argument, it is not enough that the premises be true and entail the conclusion. They must also be ordered correctly: the premises must be prior to the conclusion, both ontologically and epistemologically. This lesson unpacks the author's distinction between two perspectives on priority — 'by nature' and 'by us' — and shows how they guide the method of demonstration in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions of demonstrative premises
Read the first part of the passage. List the four conditions the author states: priority (tahaddum bi al-dhat), truth (sidq), appropriateness (munasaba), and primariness (awwaliya). Notice the counterexample: a doctor using a geometrical premise fails the appropriateness condition.
These conditions are standard from Aristotle's Posterior Analytics.
2
Distinguish the two senses of 'prior' and 'better known'
The passage defines 'prior by nature' and 'better known by nature' using the criteria of removal and intention. For nature, prior is that whose removal entails removal of the posterior; better known is that whose existence is intended. For us, the sensible particulars are first known, then genera are better known than species.
Write down the four key terms: al-aqdam inda al-tabi'a, al-a'raf inda al-tabi'a, al-aqdam indana, al-a'raf indana.
3
Apply the distinction to an example from kalam
Consider the kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' The major premise 'everything temporally originated has a cause' is prior by nature (if you remove causation, temporal origination cannot exist) and is better known to us (we readily affirm it). Check whether the premises meet the four conditions.
This example is not in the text but applies the tool.
4
Determine the correct methodological order
The author concludes that demonstrative science should begin with what is prior by nature and better known to us: the highest genera. Then descend to intermediate species, then to ultimate species, without descending to individuals. This avoids the non-demonstrative methods of starting from simples to compounds or from particulars to universals by induction.
Contrast with the induction method, which yields only 'indicative' (dalil) not 'demonstrative' (burhan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في اعتبار مقدمات البرهان من جهة تقدمها وعليتها وسائر شرائطها ولما كانت مقدمات البرهان عللا للنتيجة، وللعلل تقدم بالذات كان لمقدمات البرهان تقدم على النتيجة بالذات. وكذلك في المعرفة والزمان لأن النتيجة لا تعرف إلا بها؛ ويجب أن تكون صادقة حتى تنتج الصادق، ومناسبة للنتيجة داخلة في العلم الذي فيه النتيجة. وأن تكون أوائل براهينها من مقدمات أولية بنفسها فإنها إذا لم تكن كذلك لم تكن المقدمات برهانية. وكثيرًا ما تؤخذ في الإقناع الجدلي كواذب مشهورة ينتج منها صادق، وكثيرًا ما تؤخذ صوادق غير مناسبة في قياسات نتائجها صوادق مثل احتجاج الطبيب أن الجراحات المستديرة أعسر بروا لأن المستدير أكبر إحاطة؛ وهذه مقدمة هندسية غير مناسبة لم يكن برهان. والأقدم والأعرف من المقدمات هي الأشياء التي نصيبها أولاً. وعند الطبيعة الأقدم هو الذي إذا رفع ارتفع ما بعده من غير انعكاس، والأعرف هو الذي نقصد وجوده فعلى هذا الجزئيات المحسوسة عندنا أقدم وأعرف من الكليات لأن أول شيء نصيبه هو المحسوسات. ومنها نصير إلى اقتناص الكليات العقلية والكليات الجنسية عند الطبيعة وعندنا أقدم من الكليات النوعية؛ لأنها إذا رفعت ارتفعت الأنواع؛ والأنواع أعرف عندها من الأجناس لأنها إنما نقصد وجود النوع لا وجود الجنس. نعم طبيعة النوع تلزمها طبيعة الجنس فلا تكون طبيعة الجنس عندها مقصودة بالذات بل بالعرض وإلا لما تكثرت أنواع الجنس في الطبيعة بل وقع الاقتصار على نوع واحد؛ إذ طبيعة الجنس وحدها لا يمكن أن يكون لها وجود محصل؛ وعندنا الأجناس أعرف من الأنواع. فإذا أردنا أن نحقق الكليات تحققا كليا يكون المبتدأ ما هو أعرف عندنا، وأقدم عند الطبيعة؛ وهو الأجناس ؛ ثم منه إلى الأنواع المتوسطة ثمَّ إلى الأنواع الأخيرة؛ ولا ينزل منها إلى الأشخاص. فأما إذا ابتدأنا من البسائط وجرينا على طريق التركيب إلى المركبات كان الابتداء مما هو أقدم في الطبع. ولو كان كذلك كان البيان برهانيًا؛ إذ البسائط أعرف عندنا وأقدم؛ وعند الطبيعة أقدم لا أعرف؛ إذ الطبيعة تقصد قصد المركب والمركبات أعرف عندها لأنها هي الغاية لتلك البسائط. وإن ابتدأنا من الجزئيات إلى الكليات بالاستقراء لم يكن البيان برهانيا بل دليلا، ويكون قد اتفق إن كان الأعرف عندنا هو الأعرف عند الطبيعة فيجب أن تتحقق هذه الأصول على هذا المأخذ.
Chapter: On considering the premises of demonstration in terms of their priority, causality, and other conditions. Since the premises of demonstration are causes of the conclusion, and causes have essential priority, the premises of demonstration have essential priority over the conclusion. Likewise in knowledge and time, because the conclusion is only known through them. And they must be true so that they produce truth, and appropriate to the conclusion, belonging to the science in which the conclusion is. And the first principles of demonstrations must be primary premises in themselves, for if they are not so, the premises are not demonstrative. Often in dialectical persuasion, famous falsehoods are taken and produce truth, and often true premises that are inappropriate are taken in syllogisms whose conclusions are true, like the doctor's argument that circular wounds are harder to heal because a circle has greater circumference; this is a geometrical premise inappropriate and is not a demonstration. The prior and better known among premises are things that we first obtain. By nature, the prior is that which if removed, what follows is removed without converse, and the better known is that whose existence we intend. Thus, sensible particulars are for us prior and better known than universals, because the first thing we obtain is sensibles. From them we proceed to capture intellectual universals. Generic universals are for nature and for us prior to specific universals, because if they are removed, the species are removed. And species are better known to nature than genera, because we intend the existence of the species, not the existence of the genus. Yes, the nature of the species necessitates the nature of the genus, so the nature of the genus is not intended essentially by nature but accidentally; otherwise the species of the genus would not multiply in nature, but only one species would occur, since the nature of the genus alone cannot have a determinate existence. And for us, genera are better known than species. So if we want to verify universals in a universal way, we begin with what is better known to us and prior by nature, which is genera; then from it to intermediate species, then to ultimate species, and do not descend to individuals. As for if we begin from simples and proceed on the method of composition to compounds, the beginning is what is prior by nature. And if it were so, the demonstration would be demonstrative, because simples are better known to us and prior; but to nature they are prior not better known, because nature aims at the compound and compounds are better known to it because they are the goal of those simples. And if we begin from particulars to universals by induction, the demonstration is not demonstrative but indicative, and it would be that if what is better known to us is what is better known to nature, these principles must be verified according to this approach.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the requirements for demonstrative premises (muqaddimat al-burhan) and the epistemological order of knowledge. It first lists conditions: premises must be essentially prior to the conclusion (tahaddum bi al-dhat), true, appropriate (i.e., within the same science), and primary (awwali). It gives a counterexample: a doctor using a geometrical premise about circles is not a demonstration because the premise is inappropriate. Then it defines 'prior' (aqdam) and 'better known' (a'raf) in two senses: by nature (inda al-tabi'a) and by us (indana). By nature, the prior is that which if removed everything else is removed (e.g., genus over species); the better known is that whose existence is intended (e.g., species over genus). By us, sensible particulars are first known, then genera are better known than species. The correct method for demonstrative science is to begin with what is prior by nature and better known to us, i.e., the highest genera, then descend through intermediate species to ultimate species, not to individuals. Alternative methods (starting from simples to compounds, or from particulars to universals by induction) are not demonstrative.

Objection that Generic Meaning is Better Known to Nature
Dialectical objection against species being better known than genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of an objection in dialectical reasoning and reconstruct the likely counter-argument based on the principles of demonstration in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
In jadal, the objection forces a refinement: what is 'better known to nature' must be clarified.

This passage presents a dialectical moment where an objection is raised against a prior claim. The objector asserts that generic meanings are better known to nature because they are known in themselves, not through something else. Understanding this objection and anticipating the answer trains you in the art of munazara—refining claims under pressure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prior claim being objected to
From context, the author likely argued that species are better known to nature than genera. The objector challenges this by asserting the opposite.
Recall the earlier lesson on 'species better known to nature'.
2
Extract the objector's reasoning
The objector says 'because even if it is not known in terms of something, it is itself.' This means the genus is known directly, not via a higher concept.
The phrase 'by the measure of truth it is better known' suggests an absolute perspective.
3
Identify the weakness in the objection
The objection confuses what is better known in itself (metaphysically prior) with what is better known to nature (epistemologically prior).
This distinction is standard in Islamic logic.
4
Reconstruct the missing response
The response begins 'there is no meaning to your statement.' It likely explains that the species, being more specific, is more directly known to human perception.
The complete answer would clarify the two senses of 'better known'.

Prior and Better Known: Two Perspectives
Author explains prior/better known and hierarchy of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between what is prior in nature and what is better known to us, and apply this to the hierarchy of genus and species.

Core Idea
What is prior in nature is not always what we know first.

In our quest for knowledge, we often confuse what is most fundamental in reality with what is most familiar to us. This passage from a classical logic text clarifies the crucial distinction between the order of being and the order of knowing. Understanding this distinction is essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs of concepts
The passage introduces two pairs: 'prior' vs. 'posterior' and 'better known' vs. 'less known', each considered from two perspectives: in nature and to us. Note the Arabic terms: al-aqdam (prior) and al-a'raf (better known).
2
Define 'prior in nature'
The author defines what is prior in nature: that which, if removed, everything after it is also removed, but not vice versa. This is a logical dependency test. Apply it to genus and species: if genus is removed, species cannot exist, but removing species does not remove genus.
3
Define 'better known in nature'
What is better known in nature is what nature intends as an end. Nature aims at the species, not the genus, because the genus alone cannot exist; it is actualized through species. Therefore, species are better known in nature.
4
Apply the distinction to the hierarchy of universals
Now determine: in nature, genera are prior (existentially foundational) but species are better known (intended). To us, however, genera are better known because we grasp the general category first before specifying. Sensible particulars are most prior and better known to us initially.
5
Summarize the inverse relationship
The passage reveals a general principle: what is prior in nature is often not what is better known to us, and vice versa. This has direct implications for how we should structure demonstrative proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التي نصيبها أولاً. وعند الطبيعة الأقدم هو الذي إذا رفع ارتفع ما بعده من غير انعكاس، والأعرف هو الذي نقصد وجوده فعلى هذا الجزئيات المحسوسة عندنا أقدم وأعرف من الكليات لأن أول شيء نصيبه هو المحسوسات. ومنها نصير إلى اقتناص الكليات العقلية والكليات الجنسية عند الطبيعة وعندنا أقدم من الكليات النوعية؛ لأنها إذا رفعت ارتفعت الأنواع؛ والأنواع أعرف عندها من الأجناس لأنها إنما نقصد وجود النوع لا وجود الجنس. نعم طبيعة النوع تلزمها طبيعة الجنس فلا تكون طبيعة الجنس عندها مقصودة بالذات بل بالعرض وإلا لما تكثرت أنواع الجنس في الطبيعة بل وقع الاقتصار على نوع واحد؛ إذ طبيعة الجنس وحدها لا يمكن أن يكون لها وجود محصل؛ وعندنا الأجناس أعرف من الأنواع. فإذا أردنا أن نحقق الكليات تحققا كليا يكون المبتدأ ما هو أعرف عندنا، وأقدم عند الطبيعة؛ وهو الأجناس؛ ثم منه إلى الأنواع المتوسطة ثم إلى الأنواع الأخيرة؛ ولا ينزل منها إلى الأشخاص. فأما إذا ابتدأنا من البسائط وجرينا على طريق التركيب إلى المركبات كان الابتداء مما هو أقدم في الطبع. ولو كان كذلك كان البيان برهانيًا؛ إذ البسائط أعرف عندنا وأقدم؛ وعند الطبيعة أقدم لا أعرف؛ إذ الطبيعة تقصد قصد المركب والمركبات أعرف عندها لأنها هي الغاية لتلك البسائط. وإن ابتدأنا من الجزئيات إلى الكليات بالاستقراء لم يكن البيان برهانيا بل دليلا، ويكون قد اتفق إن كان الأعرف عندنا هو الأعرف عند الطبيعة فيجب أن تتحقق هذه الأصول على هذا المأخذ. ثم عن البعض منهم أن المعنى الجنسي أعرف عند الطبيعة لأنه وإن لم يعرف بحسب شيء فهو نفسه، وبقياس الحق أعرف فيقال له لا معنى لقولك إنه بقياس الحق أعرف لأن الشيء إنما يعرف بعارفه، وعارفه إما لحق بالعقل أو كل من هو ذو عقل. وأما الطبيعة في قصدها انتظام الكل على سبيل الاستعارة فيكون الأعرف عندها ما يقصد انتظام الكل. وبالجملة فالطبيعة الجنسية أعرف عند العقول والطريق البرهانية يأخذ مما هو أعرف عند العقول إلى ما هو أعرف عند الطبيعة، والله أعلم بالصواب.
which we attain first. And according to nature, the prior is that which, if removed, what comes after it is removed without conversion, and the better known is that whose existence we intend. So according to this, sensible particulars are for us prior and better known than universals, because the first thing we attain is the sensibles. And from them we proceed to capture intelligible universals. And the generic universals are, according to nature and for us, prior to the specific universals; because if they are removed, the species are removed. And the species are better known according to nature than the genera because we intend the existence of the species, not the existence of the genus. Yes, the nature of the species entails the nature of the genus, so the nature of the genus is not intended by nature essentially but accidentally; otherwise the species of the genus would not multiply in nature, but would be confined to one species, since the nature of the genus alone cannot have a realized existence. And for us, the genera are better known than the species. So if we want to realize the universals in a universal realization, the beginning is with what is better known to us and prior in nature, which is the genera; then from there to the intermediate species, then to the final species; and it does not descend from them to individuals. As for if we begin from the simples and proceed on the path of composition to the composites, then the beginning is with what is prior in nature. And if it were so, then the demonstration would be a proof (burhani); because the simples are better known to us and prior; and according to nature they are prior but not better known, because nature intends the composite intentionally, and composites are better known according to nature because they are the goal of those simples. And if we begin from particulars to universals by induction, the demonstration is not a proof but rather an indication, and it would happen that if what is better known to us is also better known to nature, then these principles must be verified on this basis. Then from some of them: that the generic meaning is better known according to nature because, even if it is not known by something else, it is itself, and by the scale of truth it is better known. So it is said to him: there is no meaning to your saying 'by the scale of truth it is better known', because a thing is known only by its knower, and its knower is either he who is attached to the intellect or every one who possesses intellect. As for nature, in its intention of the organization of the whole metaphorically, what is better known to it is what organizes the whole. In summary, the generic nature is better known to intellects, and the demonstrative path takes from what is better known to intellects to what is better known to nature. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the order of knowledge. The author distinguishes between what is 'prior' (aqdam) and 'better known' (a'raf) in two senses: according to nature (fi al-tabi'a) and according to us ('indana). In nature, the prior is that which, if removed, necessitates removal of what follows (without conversion). The better known in nature is what is intended as an end. For us, the first things we perceive are sensible particulars, which are therefore prior and better known to us than universals. We then ascend from sensibles to intelligible universals. The generic universals (al-kulliyyat al-jinsiyya) are prior in nature to specific universals (al-kulliyyat al-naw'iyya) because if genera are removed, species are removed. However, species are better known in nature than genera because nature intends the existence of species, not genera. The genus is only accidentally intended, otherwise there would be only one species per genus. For us, genera are better known than species. Therefore, a proper scientific inquiry should begin with what is better known to us and prior in nature—the genera—then proceed to intermediate species, then to final species, without descending to individuals. The author then contrasts two methods: (1) starting from simples and composing to composites yields demonstrative proof (burhan), because simples are better known to us and prior in nature, though in nature they are prior but not better known (nature aims at composites). (2) Starting from particulars to universals by induction (istiqra') yields only an indication (dalil), not a proof. Finally, the author refutes a counter-claim that the generic meaning is better known in nature because it is self-evident; the response is that a thing is known only by a knower, and nature metaphorically intends the organization of the whole, so what is better known to nature is what organizes the whole. The correct demonstrative path is from what is better known to intellects to what is better known to nature.

الأقدم
الأعرف
الجنس
النوع

Key Takeaway

advanced
10 min
The Correct Path of Demonstration
Author contrasts demonstrative and inductive methods, refutes a counter-claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a demonstrative proof (burhani) and an inductive argument based on their starting points, and evaluate a counter-claim about what is better known to nature.

Core Idea
Demonstrative proof begins with what is prior in nature and known to the intellect, not with sense particulars.

Now that we understand the distinction between two senses of 'prior' and 'better known', the author applies it to the method of demonstration. He contrasts two approaches: one that starts from simples and composes to composites, yielding certainty, and another that starts from particulars and ascends by induction, yielding only indication. A counter-claim about the generic meaning being better known to nature is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Contrast the two methods of inquiry
The author describes two ways to proceed: (1) starting from simples (basa'it) and combining them to reach composites, and (2) starting from particulars and moving to universals by induction. Identify the starting point for each in terms of prior/better known.
2
Evaluate which method yields burhan
The author says starting from simples yields a demonstrative proof (burhani) because simples are prior in nature and also better known to us. But note: in nature, simples are prior but not better known; nature aims at composites. Composites are better known in nature. So the method must start with what is both prior in nature and better known to us – the simples.
3
Identify the flaw in induction for burhan
Starting from particulars to universals by induction does not produce certainty (burhan) but only an indication (dalil). This is because the starting point (particulars) is not prior in nature; the ascent is probabilistic. The author adds that induction would work only if what is better known to us coincides with what is better known to nature, which is rare.
4
Examine the counter-claim and its refutation
Some argue that the generic meaning is better known to nature because it is self-evident (by the scale of truth). The author refutes this: a thing is known only by a knower. Nature metaphorically intends the whole, so what is better known to nature is what organizes the whole, not the genus itself. Thus, the generic nature is better known to intellects, not to nature.
5
Conclude the correct method
The demonstrative path should take from what is better known to intellects (genera, simples) to what is better known to nature (composites, species). This ensures certainty. The author ends with: 'والله أعلم بالصواب' (God knows best).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التي نصيبها أولاً. وعند الطبيعة الأقدم هو الذي إذا رفع ارتفع ما بعده من غير انعكاس، والأعرف هو الذي نقصد وجوده فعلى هذا الجزئيات المحسوسة عندنا أقدم وأعرف من الكليات لأن أول شيء نصيبه هو المحسوسات. ومنها نصير إلى اقتناص الكليات العقلية والكليات الجنسية عند الطبيعة وعندنا أقدم من الكليات النوعية؛ لأنها إذا رفعت ارتفعت الأنواع؛ والأنواع أعرف عندها من الأجناس لأنها إنما نقصد وجود النوع لا وجود الجنس. نعم طبيعة النوع تلزمها طبيعة الجنس فلا تكون طبيعة الجنس عندها مقصودة بالذات بل بالعرض وإلا لما تكثرت أنواع الجنس في الطبيعة بل وقع الاقتصار على نوع واحد؛ إذ طبيعة الجنس وحدها لا يمكن أن يكون لها وجود محصل؛ وعندنا الأجناس أعرف من الأنواع. فإذا أردنا أن نحقق الكليات تحققا كليا يكون المبتدأ ما هو أعرف عندنا، وأقدم عند الطبيعة؛ وهو الأجناس؛ ثم منه إلى الأنواع المتوسطة ثم إلى الأنواع الأخيرة؛ ولا ينزل منها إلى الأشخاص. فأما إذا ابتدأنا من البسائط وجرينا على طريق التركيب إلى المركبات كان الابتداء مما هو أقدم في الطبع. ولو كان كذلك كان البيان برهانيًا؛ إذ البسائط أعرف عندنا وأقدم؛ وعند الطبيعة أقدم لا أعرف؛ إذ الطبيعة تقصد قصد المركب والمركبات أعرف عندها لأنها هي الغاية لتلك البسائط. وإن ابتدأنا من الجزئيات إلى الكليات بالاستقراء لم يكن البيان برهانيا بل دليلا، ويكون قد اتفق إن كان الأعرف عندنا هو الأعرف عند الطبيعة فيجب أن تتحقق هذه الأصول على هذا المأخذ. ثم عن البعض منهم أن المعنى الجنسي أعرف عند الطبيعة لأنه وإن لم يعرف بحسب شيء فهو نفسه، وبقياس الحق أعرف فيقال له لا معنى لقولك إنه بقياس الحق أعرف لأن الشيء إنما يعرف بعارفه، وعارفه إما لحق بالعقل أو كل من هو ذو عقل. وأما الطبيعة في قصدها انتظام الكل على سبيل الاستعارة فيكون الأعرف عندها ما يقصد انتظام الكل. وبالجملة فالطبيعة الجنسية أعرف عند العقول والطريق البرهانية يأخذ مما هو أعرف عند العقول إلى ما هو أعرف عند الطبيعة، والله أعلم بالصواب.
which we attain first. And according to nature, the prior is that which, if removed, what comes after it is removed without conversion, and the better known is that whose existence we intend. So according to this, sensible particulars are for us prior and better known than universals, because the first thing we attain is the sensibles. And from them we proceed to capture intelligible universals. And the generic universals are, according to nature and for us, prior to the specific universals; because if they are removed, the species are removed. And the species are better known according to nature than the genera because we intend the existence of the species, not the existence of the genus. Yes, the nature of the species entails the nature of the genus, so the nature of the genus is not intended by nature essentially but accidentally; otherwise the species of the genus would not multiply in nature, but would be confined to one species, since the nature of the genus alone cannot have a realized existence. And for us, the genera are better known than the species. So if we want to realize the universals in a universal realization, the beginning is with what is better known to us and prior in nature, which is the genera; then from there to the intermediate species, then to the final species; and it does not descend from them to individuals. As for if we begin from the simples and proceed on the path of composition to the composites, then the beginning is with what is prior in nature. And if it were so, then the demonstration would be a proof (burhani); because the simples are better known to us and prior; and according to nature they are prior but not better known, because nature intends the composite intentionally, and composites are better known according to nature because they are the goal of those simples. And if we begin from particulars to universals by induction, the demonstration is not a proof but rather an indication, and it would happen that if what is better known to us is also better known to nature, then these principles must be verified on this basis. Then from some of them: that the generic meaning is better known according to nature because, even if it is not known by something else, it is itself, and by the scale of truth it is better known. So it is said to him: there is no meaning to your saying 'by the scale of truth it is better known', because a thing is known only by its knower, and its knower is either he who is attached to the intellect or every one who possesses intellect. As for nature, in its intention of the organization of the whole metaphorically, what is better known to it is what organizes the whole. In summary, the generic nature is better known to intellects, and the demonstrative path takes from what is better known to intellects to what is better known to nature. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the order of knowledge. The author distinguishes between what is 'prior' (aqdam) and 'better known' (a'raf) in two senses: according to nature (fi al-tabi'a) and according to us ('indana). In nature, the prior is that which, if removed, necessitates removal of what follows (without conversion). The better known in nature is what is intended as an end. For us, the first things we perceive are sensible particulars, which are therefore prior and better known to us than universals. We then ascend from sensibles to intelligible universals. The generic universals (al-kulliyyat al-jinsiyya) are prior in nature to specific universals (al-kulliyyat al-naw'iyya) because if genera are removed, species are removed. However, species are better known in nature than genera because nature intends the existence of species, not genera. The genus is only accidentally intended, otherwise there would be only one species per genus. For us, genera are better known than species. Therefore, a proper scientific inquiry should begin with what is better known to us and prior in nature—the genera—then proceed to intermediate species, then to final species, without descending to individuals. The author then contrasts two methods: (1) starting from simples and composing to composites yields demonstrative proof (burhan), because simples are better known to us and prior in nature, though in nature they are prior but not better known (nature aims at composites). (2) Starting from particulars to universals by induction (istiqra') yields only an indication (dalil), not a proof. Finally, the author refutes a counter-claim that the generic meaning is better known in nature because it is self-evident; the response is that a thing is known only by a knower, and nature metaphorically intends the organization of the whole, so what is better known to nature is what organizes the whole. The correct demonstrative path is from what is better known to intellects to what is better known to nature.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Two Types of Principles of Demonstration
Author defines two types of principles of demonstration and distinguishes them from dialectical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an absolutely immediate principle and a posited principle of demonstration in a given Islamic science context.

Core Idea
Demonstration principles are either self-evident or posited; both are certain, unlike dialectical premises.

In this passage, the author of the matn explains that the term 'principle of demonstration' has two distinct meanings: one absolute and one relative to a particular science. Understanding this distinction is crucial for recognizing which premises in a demonstrative argument require no proof and which are accepted as starting points within a discipline. This lesson will guide you through the two types and show how they differ from dialectical premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate where the author says 'مبدأ البرهان يقال على وجهين'. Note the two types: (أ) the absolute premise without a middle term, and (ب) the posited premise that may have a middle term but is taken as a starting point in a science.
The absolute type is called 'المعروف والمتعين قبولها' – known and obligatory to accept.
2
Characterize each type
For type (أ): it has no middle term at all; it is immediate and not provable by syllogism. For type (ب): it can be proved by a middle term from a prior science, but within its own science it is posited.
Both types are certain and are one side of a contradictory pair exactly.
3
Compare with dialectical premises
The author states that a dialectical premise is also one side of a contradictory, but not 'that side itself' – meaning it is not the certain side. Recognize that demonstrative premises are fixed and certain, while dialectical ones are accepted only for the sake of argument.
This shows the epistemological priority of demonstration over dialectic.
4
Apply to an Islamic science
Consider the science of kalam. The premise 'The world is temporally originated' is a posited premise in kalam, but its proof comes from physics (a prior science). In contrast, 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause' might be considered an immediate principle.
This distinction helps in structuring sound demonstrative arguments in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في مبدأ البرهان ومبدأ البرهان يقال على وجهين فيقال مبدأ البرهان بحسب العلم مطلقا، ويقال بحسب علم ما: [أ] والأول هو مقدمة غير ذات وسط على الإطلاق أي ليس من شأنها أن تتعلق ببيان نسبة محمولها إلى موضوعها إيجابية كانت أو سلبا بحد أوسط؛ ويسمى العلم المتعارف والواجب قبولها. [ب] والثاني هو مقدمة يجوز أن تكون ذات وسط في نفسه لكنها توضع في ذلك العلم وضعا ويكون بيانها بوسط في علم قبله أو فيه لكن لا يكون في مرتبته بل بعدها. وكلا القسمين يتفقان في أن كل واحد منهما أحد طرفي النقيض بعينه لا يمكن أن يكون الآخر برهانيا، ويخالفان المقدمة الجدلية؛ إذ الجدلية وإن كانت أحد طرفي النقيض فليس بعينه على ما عرف. ثم المقدمة الوضعية تخص باسم آخر دون الحد والوضع قد يقال على الحدود المذكورة لأمور يجب تقديمها على العلم وهي غير المقدمات الموضوعة أو لا تكون في الحدود منازعة البتة بل ربما وقعت المنازعة فيها لا في معنى التصديق بل في التصور لخطأ وقع في التصور. وأما المقدمات فإنها إنما توضع ليقرر بها التصديق لا التصور فتقع فيها المنازعة، والمقدمات الموضوعة تسمى أصولا دون الحدود؛ إذ لا إيجاب فيها
Section: On the Principle of Demonstration. And the principle of demonstration is said in two ways: it is said 'principle of demonstration' according to science absolutely, and it is said according to some science. [A] The first is a premise that has no middle term absolutely, i.e., it is not such that it is concerned with explaining the relation of its predicate to its subject, whether affirmative or negative, by means of a middle term; and it is called the known and obligatory to accept. [B] And the second is a premise that may have a middle term in itself, but it is posited in that science as a posit, and its explanation is by a middle term in a science before it or in it, but not in its rank, but after it. And both divisions agree in that each one of them is one of the two sides of the contradictory opposite itself; it is not possible that the other be demonstrative. And they differ from the dialectical premise; for the dialectical premise, even if it is one side of the contradictory, is not [that side] itself as is known. Then the posited premise is distinguished by another name apart from the definition; and al-wad' may be said of the mentioned definitions for matters that must be presented to the science, and they are not like the posited premises; or there is no dispute in the definitions at all; rather dispute may occur in them not in the sense of assent (tasdiq) but in conception (tasawwur) due to an error in conception. As for the premises, they are only posited to establish assent, not conception, so dispute occurs in them. And the posited premises are called 'principles' (usul) as opposed to definitions (hudud), because there is no obligation in them.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the principle of demonstration (mabda' al-burhan). The author first states that this principle is spoken of in two ways: (1) absolute, i.e., premises that are immediate (without any middle term) in themselves, such as the first principles of knowledge (e.g., the law of non-contradiction). These are called 'the known and obligatory to accept.' (2) relative to a particular science: premises that may have a middle term in themselves but are posited in that science as starting points, and their proof comes from a prior or higher science. Both types share the characteristic that they are exactly one side of a contradictory pair (i.e., they are certain), whereas a dialectical premise, though also one side of a contradictory, is not that specific side (it is the opposite side in a dialectical context, and is not certain). The author then distinguishes between posited premises (muqaddimat wad'iyya) and definitions (hudud). Definitions are not subject to dispute over assent (tasdiq) but may involve dispute over conception (tasawwur). Premises are posited to establish assent, so dispute occurs in them. Posited premises are called 'principles' (usul), not definitions, because they carry no obligation (i.e., they are not self-evident).

مقدمة غير ذات وسط
المقدمة الجدلية
التصديق
التصور

C) The demonstrative premise is always a definition.

Key Takeaway
Demonstrative premises are certain and exactly one side of a contradiction, whereas dialectical premises are not the certain side.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Distinguishing Definitions from Premises in Demonstration
Author distinguishes posited premises from definitions, emphasizing that definitions involve conception and premises involve assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a definition (hadd) and a posited premise (muqaddima wad'iyya) based on their roles in conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Definitions concern conception and may be disputed; premises concern assent and are the foundation of demonstration.

After discussing the two types of principles of demonstration, the author turns to a related distinction: that between definitions (hudud) and posited premises (muqaddimat wad'iyya). He explains that definitions are not subject to dispute over assent but may involve error in conception, whereas premises are posited to establish assent and thus are the site of genuine disagreement. Understanding this difference is key to knowing where logical debate properly occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts discussed
Find where the author transitions from premises to definitions: 'ثم المقدمة الوضعية تخص باسم آخر دون الحد'. Note that he mentions 'الحدود' (definitions) and 'المقدمات الموضوعة' (posited premises).
The term 'الوضع' (wad') is applied to definitions but in a different sense.
2
Understand the function of definitions
The author says definitions are for matters that must be presented to the science, but they are not like posited premises. There is no dispute in definitions regarding assent; any dispute is in conception (tasawwur) due to error.
Definitions shape our understanding of terms, not our belief in propositions.
3
Understand the function of premises
Premises are posited to establish assent (tasdiq). Therefore, disputes occur over premises, not over definitions (except for conceptual errors).
Posited premises are called 'usul' (principles) as opposed to definitions.
4
Contrast the two using an Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'salat' in fiqh: it includes specific actions. Scholars may disagree over the definition (tasawwur), but that is based on conceptual error, not on assent. In contrast, the premise 'Salat is obligatory' is a posited premise that can be disputed on the basis of evidence.
This helps in organizing knowledge: definitions are for understanding terms, premises are for building arguments.

[ب] والثاني هو مقدمة يجوز أن تكون ذات وسط في نفسه لكنها توضع في ذلك العلم وضعا ويكون بيانها بوسط في علم قبله أو فيه لكن لا يكون في مرتبته بل بعدها. وكلا القسمين يتفقان في أن كل واحد منهما أحد طرفي النقيض بعينه لا يمكن أن يكون الآخر برهانيا، ويخالفان المقدمة الجدلية؛ إذ الجدلية وإن كانت أحد طرفي النقيض فليس بعينه على ما عرف. ثم المقدمة الوضعية تخص باسم آخر دون الحد والوضع قد يقال على الحدود المذكورة لأمور يجب تقديمها على العلم وهي غير المقدمات الموضوعة أو لا تكون في الحدود منازعة البتة بل ربما وقعت المنازعة فيها لا في معنى التصديق بل في التصور لخطأ وقع في التصور. وأما المقدمات فإنها إنما توضع ليقرر بها التصديق لا التصور فتقع فيها المنازعة، والمقدمات الموضوعة تسمى أصولا دون الحدود؛ إذ لا إيجاب فيها
Section: On the Principle of Demonstration. And the principle of demonstration is said in two ways: it is said 'principle of demonstration' according to science absolutely, and it is said according to some science. [A] The first is a premise that has no middle term absolutely, i.e., it is not such that it is concerned with explaining the relation of its predicate to its subject, whether affirmative or negative, by means of a middle term; and it is called the known and obligatory to accept. [B] And the second is a premise that may have a middle term in itself, but it is posited in that science as a posit, and its explanation is by a middle term in a science before it or in it, but not in its rank, but after it. And both divisions agree in that each one of them is one of the two sides of the contradictory opposite itself; it is not possible that the other be demonstrative. And they differ from the dialectical premise; for the dialectical premise, even if it is one side of the contradictory, is not [that side] itself as is known. Then the posited premise is distinguished by another name apart from the definition; and al-wad' may be said of the mentioned definitions for matters that must be presented to the science, and they are not like the posited premises; or there is no dispute in the definitions at all; rather dispute may occur in them not in the sense of assent (tasdiq) but in conception (tasawwur) due to an error in conception. As for the premises, they are only posited to establish assent, not conception, so dispute occurs in them. And the posited premises are called 'principles' (usul) as opposed to definitions (hudud), because there is no obligation in them.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the principle of demonstration (mabda' al-burhan). The author first states that this principle is spoken of in two ways: (1) absolute, i.e., premises that are immediate (without any middle term) in themselves, such as the first principles of knowledge (e.g., the law of non-contradiction). These are called 'the known and obligatory to accept.' (2) relative to a particular science: premises that may have a middle term in themselves but are posited in that science as starting points, and their proof comes from a prior or higher science. Both types share the characteristic that they are exactly one side of a contradictory pair (i.e., they are certain), whereas a dialectical premise, though also one side of a contradictory, is not that specific side (it is the opposite side in a dialectical context, and is not certain). The author then distinguishes between posited premises (muqaddimat wad'iyya) and definitions (hudud). Definitions are not subject to dispute over assent (tasdiq) but may involve dispute over conception (tasawwur). Premises are posited to establish assent, so dispute occurs in them. Posited premises are called 'principles' (usul), not definitions, because they carry no obligation (i.e., they are not self-evident).

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
10 min
The Two Senses of 'More Known' in Demonstration
Author explains concept of 'more known' in demonstration, distinguishing intellect from nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'more known to the intellect' and 'more known to nature' and explain how demonstration (burhan) moves from one to the other.

Core Idea
Demonstration journeys from what the intellect knows best to what nature knows best.

This passage from 'Mantiq Kabir' clarifies a central epistemological distinction: what is 'more known' can be understood in two ways — relative to the intellect or relative to nature. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how demonstrative proofs are structured in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two knowing agents
The passage states that a thing is known by its knower (arif). That knower is either the intellect (al-'aql) or nature (al-tabi'a). Note that 'nature' here is metaphorical — its purpose is the orderly arrangement of the whole.
Al-'aql refers to the universal human intellect; al-tabi'a refers to the inherent order and structure of reality.
2
Contrast the two 'more knowns'
What is more known to the intellect ('a'raf 'inda al-'uqul) are universal intelligibles — concepts like existence, causation, necessity. What is more known to nature ('a'raf 'inda al-tabi'a) are particular, concrete facts that contribute to the order of the cosmos.
This mirrors the Aristotelian distinction between 'prior in nature' and 'prior to us'.
3
Understand the direction of demonstration
The demonstrative path (al-tariq al-burhani) proceeds from what is more known to intellects to what is more known to nature. That is, it starts from universal, self-evident principles and moves toward specific, empirical conclusions.
This is opposite to how we often learn — from particulars to universals — but for certain proof, the universal is the starting point.
4
Apply to a real theological proof
For example, the classic Kalam argument: 'Every contingent being requires a cause. The world is contingent. Therefore, the world has a cause.' The first premise is more known to the intellect (a universal truth), while the conclusion is more known to nature (a specific fact about the world).
This shows why demonstration must begin with axiomatic principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه بقياس الحق أعرف لأن الشيء إنما يُعرف بعارفه، وعارفه إما الحق بالعقل أو كل من هو ذو عقل. وأما الطبيعة في قصدها انتظام الكل على سبيل الاستعارة فيكون الأعرف عندها ما يقصد انتظام الكل. وبالجملة فالطبيعة الجنسية أعرف عند العقول والطريق البرهانية يأخذ مما هو أعرف عند العقول إلى ما هو أعرف عند الطبيعة، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Indeed, it is known by the measure of truth that is more known, because a thing is only known by its knower, and its knower is either the truth by intellect or everyone who possesses intellect. As for nature, in its intention it is the ordering of the whole by way of metaphor, so what is more known to it is what tends to order the whole. In sum, the generic nature is more known to intellects, and the demonstrative path takes from what is more known to intellects to what is more known to nature. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological foundation of demonstration. The author explains that a thing is known through its knower (arif), and that knower is either the truth apprehended by the intellect or any rational being. He then makes a distinction between 'more known to the intellect' and 'more known to nature'. Nature's purpose is the orderly arrangement of the whole in a metaphorical sense, so what is more known to nature is what contributes to that order. However, the method of demonstration (al-tariq al-burhani) proceeds from what is more known to the intellect (e.g., simple, universal concepts) towards what is more known to nature (i.e., concrete, particular facts). This mirrors the Aristotelian distinction between 'prior in nature' and 'prior to us'.

Key Terms
الأعرف (al-a'raf): That which is more known, used to contrast what is more known to the intellect (a'raf 'inda al-'uqul) and what is more known to nature (a'raf 'inda al-tabi'a).
الطبيعة الجنسية (al-tabi'a al-jinsiyya): Generic nature, i.e., the universal essence as known by the intellect. [Note: Commonly used in falsafa; may be contrasted with specific nature.]

C) From sense perception to reason
D) From the particular to the universal

Key Takeaway
The direction of demonstration is from what is more known to intellect to what is more known to nature, contrary to the order of ordinary human cognition.

Epistemological Requirement of Demonstration Premises
Author begins a new chapter on the principles of demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological requirement for premises in a demonstration and explain why not all knowledge can be demonstrated.

Core Idea
Premises of a demonstration must be more known than the conclusion, or proof becomes infinite.

This passage opens a new section on demonstration by laying down a crucial epistemological condition: the premises of a burhan must be better known and clearer than the conclusion they support. The author warns against the tempting belief that everything can be demonstrated, which would lead to an infinite regress or circular reasoning. Understanding this principle is essential for constructing sound proofs in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Locate the statement: 'مبدأ البرهان يجب أن يكون أعرف وأوضح من البرهان'. This is the core epistemological requirement.
2
Understand 'better known and clearer'
The premises must be more evident or certain to the knower than the conclusion. This ensures the argument actually transmits certainty rather than just rearranging ignorance.
3
Recognize the false belief
The author rejects the belief that 'كل شيء سيتبين بالبرهان' (everything will be made evident by demonstration). Why is this false? Because it would require either an endless chain of premises or circularity.
4
Examine the resulting opinions
The author mentions two opinions that arise from combining the true condition with the false belief. The first (marked with [أ]) 'invalidates demonstration'. Though the text cuts off, we can infer it concerns the regress problem: if every premise needs a proof, nothing can be a starting point.
The passage is incomplete; later sections likely discuss the second opinion and the solution (such as accepting self-evident premises or axioms).

A chapter on knowing what constitutes the principles of demonstration, and its universality and necessity. So their explanation must be by establishing
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the principles of demonstration. The author asserts a key epistemological requirement: the premises of a demonstration must be more known and clearer than the conclusion they prove. He notes that a false belief often accompanies this, namely that everything can be demonstrated — which is absurd, as it would lead to an infinite regress or circular reasoning. He then hints at two opinions that arise from combining the correct principle (that premises must be clearer) with the false belief (that everything is demonstrable). The first opinion is 'what invalidates demonstration' — likely meaning that if premises themselves require demonstration, then nothing can be the starting point. The text cuts off, but the gist is that the principles must be accepted without proof to avoid regress.

Key Terms
مبدأ البرهان (mabda' al-burhan): The starting point or premise of a demonstration, which must be better known and clearer than the conclusion. [Note: Standard Aristotelian requirement; consistent across the tradition.]
أعرف وأوضح (a'raf wa awdah): Better known and clearer; epistemic superiority of premises over conclusion in demonstration. [Note: No significant shift.]
البرهان (al-burhan): Demonstration that yields certain knowledge from necessary premises. [Note: Standard term; Ibn Taymiyya later critiques its epistemological monopoly.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They must be from geometry.
D) They must be self-evident to everyone.

Key Takeaway
For a demonstration to yield certain knowledge, its premises must be epistemically prior to the conclusion; otherwise, the proof collapses into an infinite regress or circularity.

Why Demonstration Needs Self-Evident Principles
Refutation of infinite regress and circularity in demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that infinite regress and circularity in demonstration necessitate self-evident premises.

Core Idea
Demonstration must rest on self-evident principles or it collapses into absurdity.

This passage takes on a common misconception—that all knowledge must come through demonstration. The author shows that this leads to absurdity. Instead, he argues, demonstration must rest on principles that are self-evident. You will learn to identify the two logical fallacies that result from demanding a demonstration for everything, and understand why some truths must be self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two false consequences
Find where the author presents two opinions that result from claiming all knowledge is demonstrated: infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr).
The author labels them A and B in the Arabic text.
2
Explain the infinite regress argument
Why does an infinite chain of premises make demonstration impossible? Because to prove any proposition, you'd need an infinite number of prior proofs, which is humanly impossible.
This is a standard reductio ad absurdum.
3
Explain the circularity argument
Why is circular reasoning invalid? Because it uses a proposition to prove itself, leading to a self-contradiction where a thing is prior to itself.
The author says it implies priority of a thing over itself.
4
Conclude the necessity of self-evident premises
How does the author use these refutations to establish that some things are known by themselves? By showing that if not everything is unknown, and not everything is known by demonstration, then some knowledge must be self-evident.
He uses a dichotomy: either everything is known by itself or by demonstration, and the latter leads to regress/circularity, so the former must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في معرفة ما يكون من المبادئ للبرهان وكلية ذلك وضروريته لما علم أن مبدأ البرهان يجب أن يكون أعرف وأوضح من البرهان وهو الحق، واقترن به ظن أن كل شيء سيتبين بالبرهان؛ وهو باطل اجتمع منهما رأيان [أ] أحدهما ما يبطل البرهان؛ وذلك أنه لما كان المطلوب بالبرهان يتبين بمقدمات يحتاج أن يكون أوضح منه. فيجب أن يكون بيانها بإقامة البرهان عليه؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد متوقفا في إقامة البرهان عليه على مقدمات غير متناهية وذلك محال [ب] والثاني ما يفضي إلى الدور؛ وذلك لأنهم لما وضعوا أن كل شيء يتبين بالبرهان، وللبرهان مبادئ يكون البرهان منها فيبرهن هذا المبدأ بذلك، وذلك بهذا، وهذا هو الدور. ثمّ كل واحد من الرأيين باطل؛ وذلك لأن المقدمة المؤدية إليهما وهي أن كل علم إنما يقع بالبرهان. وإنه إما أن لا يكون علمًا أو يكون ببرهان باطلة بل الحق أن يقال إما أن لا يكون كل شيء مجهولا ، وإما أن يكون كل شيء [٢٨٥,] معلوما. والمعلوم إما معلوم لذاته أو بالبرهان. ولا يمكن أن يكون كل شيء مجهولاً فإنه إذا كان كل شيء مجهولاً لم يكن قولنا كل شيء مجهول بمعلوم، ولا كل شيء معلوما ببرهان وإلا لكان كل برهان يعلم ببرهان، هذا خلف بل من الأشياء ما يعلم بذاته ؛ وحينئذ يبطل قول كل واحد من الطائفتين. وذلك لأن الذاهب بمتوسطات بين الحدين؛ والمتوسطات متناهية لكونها محصورة بين حاصرتين حينئذ فتكون متناهية إلى ما يكون بينا بنفسه بلا واسطة. وقد مرَّ مثل هذا الكلام من قبل ؛ والكلام في الدور بأنه ما هو وكيف هو؛ وإنه باطل وإلا يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، فإنه من جملة ما قد مر أيضًا. وإن كان بطريق الإشارة فيجب أن نذكر في هذا المقام ما يتعلق بمقدمات البرهان لما كانت هذه المقدمات مفيدة لما لا يتغيَّر من العلوم يجب أن لا يمكن بغير هذه المقدمات عما هي عليه. وهذا المعنى أحد المعاني التي تسمى ضرورية؛ وقد مرَّ أيضًا ذكر ما يطلق عليه اسم الضرورة. والآن نقول: إن الضروري إما أن يقال بحسب الوجود المطلق بلا شرط وهو الذي لا يمكن أن يفرض معدوما البتة، وإما أن يقال بحسب العدم المطلق وهو الذي لا يمكن أن يفرض موجودًا ،البتة، وإما أن يقال بحسب
Chapter on knowing what constitutes principles for demonstration, their universality and necessity. Since it is known that the principle of demonstration must be more known and clearer than the demonstration, and it is truth, there arose from it the opinion that everything will be made evident by demonstration; but this is false, and from both [premises] two opinions arose: [A] one of them invalidates demonstration; that is because when what is sought by demonstration is made evident by premises that need to be clearer than it, then their demonstration must be by establishing a demonstration upon it; and then it follows that a single thing is dependent for establishing demonstration upon it on infinite premises, which is impossible. [B] The second leads to circularity; that is because they posited that everything is made evident by demonstration, and demonstration has principles from which it is made, then this principle is demonstrated by that, and that by this, which is circularity. Then each of the two opinions is false; because the premise leading to them, namely that all knowledge occurs only by demonstration, is false. Rather the truth is to say: either not everything is unknown, or everything is known. The known is either known by itself or by demonstration. It cannot be that everything is unknown, for if everything were unknown, then our statement 'everything is unknown' would not be known, nor that everything is known by demonstration; otherwise every demonstration would be known by demonstration, which is absurd. Rather, some things are known by themselves. Then the statement of each of the two groups is invalidated. That is because he who goes by means of intermediaries between the two extremes; the intermediaries are finite because they are bounded by two bounds, so they are finite up to what is self-evident without mediation. Similar speech has passed before. And the discussion about circularity, what it is and how; it is false, otherwise it would require the priority of a thing over itself, which is among what has also passed. And if it is by way of indication, we must mention in this place what pertains to the premises of demonstration. Since these premises yield unchangeable knowledge, it must not be possible without these premises to be other than they are. This meaning is one of the meanings called 'necessary'. And the mention of what is termed 'necessity' has also passed. And now we say: The necessary is either said with respect to absolute existence without condition, which is that which cannot be assumed non-existent at all; or with respect to absolute non-existence, which is that which cannot be assumed existent at all; or with respect to...
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the principles of demonstration (burhan). The author begins by stating that the principle of demonstration must be more known and clearer than the demonstration itself, and it is truth. From this, some erroneously thought that everything can be demonstrated. The author refutes this by showing it leads to two false opinions: (1) infinite regress, and (2) circular reasoning. Under the first opinion, if every premise needs a prior demonstration ad infinitum, then demonstration becomes impossible. Under the second, if everything is demonstrated, then principles are demonstrated by each other, which is circular. The author then argues that the premise 'all knowledge is by demonstration' is false. Instead, he proposes a dichotomy: either not everything is unknown, or everything is known. The known is either self-evident (known by itself) or known by demonstration. It is impossible that everything is unknown, because then the statement itself would be unknown. Similarly, it is impossible that everything is known by demonstration ad infinitum. Therefore, some things must be self-evident. The argument about intermediaries between two extremes shows that wherever there is a chain of intermediaries, it must terminate at self-evident premises. The author also discusses the concept of circularity and why it is invalid (because it would imply priority of a thing over itself). Finally, he introduces the notion of the necessary ( الضروري ), defined in three ways: (1) necessary in absolute existence (cannot be assumed non-existent), (2) necessary in absolute non-existence (cannot be assumed existent), and (3) presumably a third (the text cuts off). This passage is a classic treatment of foundational issues in epistemology, emphasizing that demonstration rests on self-evident truths.

Key Terms
مبدأ البرهان
دور
تسلسل
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It leads to certainty
B) It leads to either infinite regress or circular reasoning
C) It leads to skepticism
D) It leads to the denial of self-evident truths

Key Takeaway
For demonstration to be possible, there must be self-evident truths that do not themselves require demonstration.
