The Three Meanings of 'Necessary' (Daruri) in Demonstration
Classification of the meanings of 'necessary' (daruri) for demonstration premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three senses of 'necessary' (daruri) and explain their role in the foundations of demonstration.

Core Idea
The necessary has three meanings: existence that cannot be absent, non-existence that cannot be present, and a third sense that provides the bedrock for certain knowledge.

After establishing that demonstration must rest on necessary premises, the author defines what 'necessary' means. He provides a threefold division: necessary in existence, necessary in non-existence, and a third sense (cut off in the text). This classification is crucial for understanding the epistemic status of self-evident truths. You will learn to apply these meanings to propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall why premises must be necessary
The author says premises of demonstration must be such that they cannot be other than they are. This unchangeable quality is called 'necessary'.
This echoes the earlier requirement that premises be more known and clearer than the conclusion.
2
Identify the first meaning: necessary in existence
A proposition is necessary with respect to absolute existence if it cannot be assumed non-existent at all. For example, 'The whole is greater than its part' is always true.
This is a necessary truth in the most absolute sense.
3
Identify the second meaning: necessary in non-existence
A proposition is necessary with respect to absolute non-existence if it cannot be assumed existent at all. For example, a logical contradiction cannot be true.
These are necessary falsehoods, but still 'necessary' in the sense of impossibility.
4
Speculate on the third meaning
The text cuts off, but based on context, the third meaning might be necessary in a conditional sense, or necessary by assumption (like axioms in a system). Reflect on what else might fit.
Some logical works add a third sense: necessary with respect to a specific condition or framework.

Rather, some things are known by themselves. Then the statement of each of the two groups is invalidated. That is because he who goes by means of intermediaries between the two extremes; the intermediaries are finite because they are bounded by two bounds, so they are finite up to what is self-evident without mediation. Similar speech has passed before. And the discussion about circularity, what it is and how; it is false, otherwise it would require the priority of a thing over itself, which is among what has also passed. And if it is by way of indication, we must mention in this place what pertains to the premises of demonstration. Since these premises yield unchangeable knowledge, it must not be possible without these premises to be other than they are. This meaning is one of the meanings called 'necessary'. And the mention of what is termed 'necessity' has also passed. And now we say: The necessary is either said with respect to absolute existence without condition, which is that which cannot be assumed non-existent at all; or with respect to absolute non-existence, which is that which cannot be assumed existent at all; or with respect to...
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the principles of demonstration (burhan). The author begins by stating that the principle of demonstration must be more known and clearer than the demonstration itself, and it is truth. From this, some erroneously thought that everything can be demonstrated. The author refutes this by showing it leads to two false opinions: (1) infinite regress, and (2) circular reasoning. Under the first opinion, if every premise needs a prior demonstration ad infinitum, then demonstration becomes impossible. Under the second, if everything is demonstrated, then principles are demonstrated by each other, which is circular. The author then argues that the premise 'all knowledge is by demonstration' is false. Instead, he proposes a dichotomy: either not everything is unknown, or everything is known. The known is either self-evident (known by itself) or known by demonstration. It is impossible that everything is unknown, because then the statement itself would be unknown. Similarly, it is impossible that everything is known by demonstration ad infinitum. Therefore, some things must be self-evident. The argument about intermediaries between two extremes shows that wherever there is a chain of intermediaries, it must terminate at self-evident premises. The author also discusses the concept of circularity and why it is invalid (because it would imply priority of a thing over itself). Finally, he introduces the notion of the necessary ( الضروري ), defined in three ways: (1) necessary in absolute existence (cannot be assumed non-existent), (2) necessary in absolute non-existence (cannot be assumed existent), and (3) presumably a third (the text cuts off). This passage is a classic treatment of foundational issues in epistemology, emphasizing that demonstration rests on self-evident truths.

Key Terms
ضروري
وجود مطلق
عدم مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of the necessary in the sense of absolute non-existence?
A) 'The sun will rise tomorrow'
B) 'A thing is itself'
C) 'A circle is a square'
D) 'Fire is hot'

Key Takeaway
The necessary has three meanings, the first two being: a truth that cannot be absent, and a falsehood that cannot be present; these provide the foundation for necessary premises in demonstration.

Why Not All Knowledge Can Be By Demonstration: Refuting Regress and Circularity
Author refutes that all knowledge requires demonstration, pointing to regress and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's refutation of the claim that all knowledge requires demonstration, and identify the two impossible consequences (infinite regress and circularity).

Core Idea
If every truth needed proof, we'd get infinite regress or circularity — so some truths must be self-evident.

The author challenges the view that all knowledge is acquired through demonstration. He shows that this view leads to two absurdities: infinite regress or circular reasoning. The only escape is to accept self-evident truths that need no proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic premise
Find the premise that leads to the two impossible outcomes. The author states it explicitly: 'that all knowledge occurs only by demonstration'.
This premise is the root of the error.
2
Derive the first consequence: infinite regress
If every piece of knowledge requires a demonstration, then the premises of each demonstration also require demonstrations, and so on without end. This is tasalsul.
Infinite regress is impossible because it would require an actual infinite chain.
3
Derive the second consequence: circularity
Alternatively, the chain of demonstrations might circle back: premise A is proved by B, and B by A. This is dawr (circular reasoning).
Circularity provides no real knowledge because each premise depends on the other.
4
See the author's solution: self-evident truths
The author concludes that not everything is known by demonstration. Some truths are self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi), like first principles. These are the foundational endpoints that halt regress and avoid circularity.
The author also uses a reductio: if everything were unknown, the statement 'everything is unknown' would itself be unknown, which is self-contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان عليه؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد متوقفا في إقامة البرهان عليه على مقدمات غير متناهية وذلك محال [ب] والثاني ما يفضي إلى الدور؛ وذلك لأنهم لما وضعوا أن كل شيء يتبين بالبرهان، وللبرهان مبادئ يكون البرهان منها فيبرهن هذا المبدأ بذلك، وذلك بهذا، وهذا هو الدور. ثمّ كل واحد من الرأيين باطل؛ وذلك لأن المقدمة المؤدية إليهما وهي أن كل علم إنما يقع بالبرهان. وإنه إما أن لا يكون علمًا أو يكون ببرهان باطلة بل الحق أن يقال إما أن لا يكون كل شيء مجهولا ، وإما أن يكون كل شيء [٢٨٥,] معلوما. والمعلوم إما معلوم لذاته أو بالبرهان. ولا يمكن أن يكون كل شيء مجهولاً فإنه إذا كان كل شيء مجهولاً لم يكن قولنا كل شيء مجهول بمعلوم، ولا كل شيء معلوما ببرهان وإلا لكان كل برهان يعلم ببرهان، هذا خلف بل من الأشياء ما يعلم بذاته ؛ وحينئذ يبطل قول كل واحد من الطائفتين. وذلك لأن الذاهب بمتوسطات بين الحدين؛ والمتوسطات متناهية لكونها محصورة بين حاصرتين حينئذ فتكون متناهية إلى ما يكون بينا بنفسه بلا واسطة. وقد مرَّ مثل هذا الكلام من قبل
The demonstration upon it; then it would follow that a single thing depends for establishing a demonstration upon infinite premises, which is impossible. The second leads to circularity; because when they posited that everything is made evident by demonstration, and demonstration has principles from which the demonstration is made, then this principle is demonstrated by that, and that by this, and this is circularity. Then each of the two opinions is false; because the premise leading to them, which is that all knowledge occurs only by demonstration, and that either there is no knowledge or it is by demonstration, is false. Rather the truth is to say either not everything is unknown, or everything is known. The known is either known by itself or by demonstration. It is not possible that everything is unknown, for if everything were unknown, our statement 'everything is unknown' would not be known, nor everything known by demonstration, otherwise every demonstration would be known by demonstration, which is absurd. Rather among things there is what is known by itself; then the statement of each of the two groups is invalidated. Because that which proceeds with intermediates between the two limits; the intermediates are finite because they are bounded by two boundaries, then they are finite reaching what is evident by itself without medium. Such speech has passed before.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two erroneous views about demonstration. The author argues that if every piece of knowledge required a demonstration, then either there would be an infinite regress of premises (al-tasalsul) or a circularity (al-dawr). Both are impossible. The key premise leading to these views is the claim that all knowledge is acquired only through demonstration. The author shows this is false by pointing out that some things are known by themselves (ma'lum bi-dhatihi). For example, the first principles of reasoning are self-evident and do not require proof. The argument also notes that if everything were unknown, the very statement 'everything is unknown' would itself be unknown, which is self-contradictory. Similarly, if every demonstration needed to be demonstrated, that would lead to an infinite chain. Instead, there must be foundational self-evident truths where the chain terminates. The author also mentions that intermediates (al-mutawassitat) between two extremes are finite, bounded by those extremes, and thus lead to what is self-evident. This is a classic argument for the necessity of first principles in demonstrative science.

تسلسل
دور
بين بنفسه

B) Knowledge is impossible.
C) Some truths must be self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi).
D) Demonstration is useless.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress and circularity forces us to accept that some truths are self-evident and serve as the foundation for all demonstration.

The Definition of Universal Necessary Propositions
Author defines universal necessary propositions for demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a universal necessary proposition used in burhan and distinguish it from an absolute universal proposition.

Core Idea
Necessity in demonstration is conditional on the subject's description, not eternal existence.

In demonstrative science, not all universal propositions are treated equally. This passage clarifies what it means for a premise to be 'necessarily true' in the context of burhan. The key insight is that necessity is tied to the subject's description, not to its existence in all times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition given for 'Every J is B necessarily'
Find the phrase in the Arabic where the author explains the meaning: 'كل ج ب بالضرورة' and see how he qualifies it with 'ما دام موصوفًا بأنه ج'.
2
Extract the condition of necessity
Notice that the necessity holds only as long as the subject is described by J, not for all times of its existence.
This contrasts with absolute necessity.
3
Contrast with absolute universal propositions
Read the section on 'المطلقات الكلية' where the author says the predicate may hold only at some time for each individual. Compare the two.
The absolute universal does not require the predicate to hold at all times of the subject's description.
4
Identify the types of necessary predicates
The author lists genera, differentiae, and essential accidents as necessary predicates.
These are the kinds of predicates that can appear in demonstrative premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الكتاب فإذا قلنا كل ج ب بالضرورة عنينا أن كل ما يوصف بأنه «ج» بالضرورة فإنه موصوف بأنه «ب» بالضرورة ما دام موصوفًا بأنه «ج»؛ وإن لم يكن ما دام موجود الذات؛ لأن المحمولات الضرورية هنا أجناس وفصول وعوارض ذاتية لازمة. ولزوم هذه بالضرورة على هذه الجهة فإنه ليس إذا وصف شيء بنوع ما يجب أن يوصف بجنسه أو فصله أو حده أو ما يكون من لوازمه بالضرورة بل ما دام موصوفًا بذلك النوع. فإنه إذا زال النوع عن ذلك الشيء زال حده لا محالة وكثير من فصوله. وأما الجنس فربَّما يزول مثلا إذا استحال الأبيض فصار مشبعا أو الحلو فصار تفها لا طعم له، فزال حينئذ النوع وجنسه وهو الأبيض واللون، وزال الحلو والطعم. وربَّما لا يزول كما إذا استحال الأسود فصار أبيض بطل حمل النوع، ولم يبطل حمل الجنس. ولأن المقدمات البرهانية قيل فيها إنها يجب أن تكون كلية؛ وذلك إنا إذا قلنا في كتاب القياس كل ج ب عنينا به أنه ليس شيء مما يوصف بـ «ج» إلا والباء موجود له إن كان القول كليًّا ،موجباً، ومسلوب عنه إن كان سالبا كليا من غير اشتراط شيء آخر؛ وهو أن الوجود والسلب يكون في كل زمان أو مطلقا بل يجوز في المطلقات الكلية أن يكون المحمول موجوداً والكل واحد من أفراد الموضوع في وقت ما فقط. وأما هنا فإذا قلنا كل ج ب عنينا به أن كل واحد مما يوصف بـ «ج» فالباء ثابت له في جميع زمان اتصافه بالجيم؛ وهذه الكلية تبطل بوجهين : إما بأن لا يكون الباء ثابتا لبعض أفراد ما يوصف بالجيم دائما كالكتابة بالفعل بل بعض أفراده مسلوب عنه الكتابة بالفعل دائما. وإما بأن لا يكون المحمول ثابتًا لبعض أفراد ما يوصف بـ (ج) في بعض أزمان اتصافه به كالعلم بالنسبة إلى الإنسان فإنه ليس كل إنسان عالمًا في جميع أزمان اتصافه بالإنسانية بل بعض الأفراد سلب عنه العلم في بعض أزمان اتصافه بالإنسانية كالطفل مثلا. وعلى هذا القياس إذا قلنا لا شيء من «ج ب» عنينا به أنه لا شيء مما يوصف به إلا ويسلب عن الباء في جميع زمان اتصافه بـ «ج». وكذبه يكون إما بثبوت الباء لبعض أفراد الجيم دائمًا أو لبعض أفراده في بعض أزمان اتصافه بالجيم.
This book: when we say 'Every J is B necessarily', we mean that everything described as 'J' necessarily is described as 'B' necessarily as long as it is described as 'J', even if not as long as it exists in essence. Because the necessary predicates here are genera, differentiae, and essential necessary accidents. And the necessity in this manner is such that when a thing is described by a species, it must be described by its genus or differentia or definition or what is among its necessary concomitants, but only as long as it is described by that species. For when the species departs from that thing, its definition departs inevitably, and many of its differentiae. As for the genus, it may depart, for example, when the white changes to saturated, or the sweet becomes insipid with no taste; then the species and its genus—white and color—depart, and sweet and taste depart. And sometimes it does not depart, as when the black changes to white: the predication of the species is nullified, but the predication of the genus is not nullified. And because the demonstrative premises are said to be universal, that is, when we say in the Book of Syllogism 'Every J is B', we mean that nothing described as 'J' lacks B if the statement is universal affirmative, and B is negated from it if universal negative, without any other condition; and that existence and negation are for every time or absolutely, but in absolute universals it is allowed that the predicate exists for each individual of the subject only at some time. But here, when we say 'Every J is B', we mean that every individual described as 'J' has B in all the time of its being described as J. And this universality is invalidated in two ways: either by B not being constant for some individuals described as J always, like actual writing—some individuals are always negated actual writing; or by the predicate not being constant for some individuals described as J at some times of its being described as J, like knowledge with respect to man: not every man is knowledgeable in all times of being described as humanity, but some individuals have knowledge negated at some times of being described as humanity, e.g., a child. And likewise, when we say 'No J is B', we mean that nothing described as J is such that B is affirmed of it in all the time of its being described as J. And its falsity is either by B being constant for some individuals of J always, or for some individuals at some times of their being described as J.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines what is meant by a universal necessary proposition in the context of demonstration (burhan). The author clarifies that the necessity is not absolute or tied to the subject's essence in all times, but rather it is conditional upon the subject's description by the term 'J'. For example, if we say 'Every human is an animal necessarily', this is true as long as something is human; if it ceases to be human, the animality may still remain, but the necessity in the proposition is about the period of being human. The author explains that necessary predicates include genera, differentiae, and essential accidents. He then gives examples: when a white thing becomes saturated red, the species 'white' and its genus 'color' are lost; when sweet becomes tasteless, both the species and genus disappear. However, sometimes the genus persists while the species changes, as when black becomes white: the genus 'color' remains, but the species 'black' is gone. The author contrasts universal propositions in the syllogism (where universality may allow the predicate to hold only at some time for each individual) with necessary universal propositions in demonstration (where the predicate must hold for all times the subject is described by the subject term). He lists two ways a necessary universal affirmative can be false: (1) if the predicate is not constantly true for some individuals (e.g., 'every man writes actually' is false because some men never write), or (2) if the predicate is not true for some individuals at all times of their being described by the subject (e.g., 'every man is knowledgeable' is false because children lack knowledge). Similarly, a universal negative is false if the predicate is constantly true for some individuals or true for some at certain times.

Key Terms
بالضرورة
المحمولات الضرورية
المطلقات الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a universal necessary affirmative proposition 'Every J is B' false?
A) When B is not constant for any individual of J at any time.
B) When B is not constant for some individuals of J always, or for some individuals at some times of their being J.
C) When B is true for all individuals at all times.
D) When B is a genus of J.

Key Takeaway
A universal necessary proposition in demonstration requires the predicate to hold for every individual of the subject in all times the subject is described by the subject term.

Two Ways a Universal Necessary Proposition Fails
Author explains falsity conditions for universal necessary propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two reasons a universal necessary proposition can be false according to this passage.

Core Idea
A necessary universal fails if the predicate is not constant for all individuals at all times of their description.

The author not only defines the universal necessary proposition but also specifies when such a proposition is false. Understanding these falsity conditions is crucial for evaluating demonstrative arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ways a universal necessary affirmative can be false
Look at the Arabic: 'و هذه الكلية تبطل بوجهين'. Extract the two ways.
First: predicate not constant for some individuals always. Second: predicate not constant for some individuals at some times.
2
Analyze the first way with the example of writing
The author gives 'actual writing' as a predicate that is not always true for some individuals (e.g., those who never write). This shows the predicate fails to be necessary.
Contrast with writing as a potentiality.
3
Analyze the second way with the example of knowledge
Knowledge is not true for humans at all times (e.g., children). So 'Every human is knowledgeable' is false.
The subject term 'human' still applies, but the predicate fails at some times.
4
Apply to universal negative
The author similarly defines the universal negative and its falsity: either B is constant for some individuals always or for some individuals at some times.

This book: when we say 'Every J is B necessarily', we mean that everything described as 'J' necessarily is described as 'B' necessarily as long as it is described as 'J', even if not as long as it exists in essence. And this universality is invalidated in two ways: either by B not being constant for some individuals described as J always, like actual writing—some individuals are always negated actual writing; or by the predicate not being constant for some individuals described as J at some times of its being described as J, like knowledge with respect to man: not every man is knowledgeable in all times of being described as humanity, but some individuals have knowledge negated at some times of being described as humanity, e.g., a child. And likewise, when we say 'No J is B', we mean that nothing described as J is such that B is affirmed of it in all the time of its being described as J. And its falsity is either by B being constant for some individuals of J always, or for some individuals at some times of their being described as J.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines what is meant by a universal necessary proposition in the context of demonstration (burhan). The author clarifies that the necessity is not absolute or tied to the subject's essence in all times, but rather it is conditional upon the subject's description by the term 'J'. For example, if we say 'Every human is an animal necessarily', this is true as long as something is human; if it ceases to be human, the animality may still remain, but the necessity in the proposition is about the period of being human. The author explains that necessary predicates include genera, differentiae, and essential accidents. He then gives examples: when a white thing becomes saturated red, the species 'white' and its genus 'color' are lost; when sweet becomes tasteless, both the species and genus disappear. However, sometimes the genus persists while the species changes, as when black becomes white: the genus 'color' remains, but the species 'black' is gone. The author contrasts universal propositions in the syllogism (where universality may allow the predicate to hold only at some time for each individual) with necessary universal propositions in demonstration (where the predicate must hold for all times the subject is described by the subject term). He lists two ways a necessary universal affirmative can be false: (1) if the predicate is not constantly true for some individuals (e.g., 'every man writes actually' is false because some men never write), or (2) if the predicate is not true for some individuals at all times of their being described by the subject (e.g., 'every man is knowledgeable' is false because children lack knowledge). Similarly, a universal negative is false if the predicate is constantly true for some individuals or true for some at certain times.

Key Takeaway
A universal necessary proposition is false if the predicate fails to hold for every individual in every time they are described by the subject term.

The Five Types of Essential Predicates in Demonstration
Author classifies essential predicates into five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish and identify the five types of essential predicates (al-mahmulat al-dhatiyya) required in demonstrative premises, and correctly classify an example into its type.

Core Idea
Essential predicates are not only those in the subject's definition; they include predicates that take the subject or its genus in their own definition.

In demonstrative reasoning, the connection between subject and predicate must be essential, not accidental. The author of this passage classifies these essential predicates into five distinct types, each defined by how the predicate relates to the subject or its genus. Understanding this classification is crucial for constructing and evaluating demonstrative arguments in Islamic sciences such as kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching principle
Recognize that demonstrative premises require predicates that are essential (dhatiyya) to their subjects. The passage defines 'essential' in the context of predication and definition.
This is specific to the discipline of demonstration (burhan).
2
List the five types in order
The author enumerates five ways a predicate can be essential: (1) predicate in subject's definition, (2) predicate taking subject in its definition, (3) predicate taking genus of subject in its definition, (4) predicate taking subject of the bearer in its definition, (5) predicate taking subject of the genus in its definition.
Types 3-5 are introduced with 'aw' (or), indicating additional categories.
3
Examine the examples for each type
For each type, the author provides an explicit example from common logical discourse: (1) 'animal' for 'man', (2) 'snub-nosed' for 'nose', (3) 'deficient' for 'odd', (4) 'collector of sight' for 'black', (5) 'even of even' for a specific number. Understand how each example fits its relational definition.
These examples are foundational for recognizing the pattern in Islamic scientific definitions.
4
Apply the classification to a new example
Given a predicate-subject pair from an Islamic science, determine which type of essential relation holds. For instance, in the definition of 'wine' (khamr) as 'intoxicating drink', the predicate 'intoxicating' takes 'drink' in its definition, which corresponds to type 2.
This skill is essential for constructing valid demonstrative premises in kalam and fiqh.

فصل: في المحمولات الذاتية التي تشترط في البرهان. إذا كانت المقدمات برهانية يجب أن تكون محمولاتها ذاتية لموضوعاتها. والذي هو بذاته يقال على وجوه : [1] منها ما يكون خاصا بالحمل والوضع في هذا الكتاب. وذلك على ضربين: أحدهما المحمول الذي يؤخذ في حد الموضوع كالحيوان في حد الإنسان وهو الداخل في ماهية الموضوع لجنسه و جنس جنسه و فصل جنسه وغير ذلك مما هو مقوم للموضوع. وثانيهما المحمول الذي يؤخذ في حده الموضوع كالأفطس بالنسبة إلى الأنف فإنه يوجد في حده الأنف فيقال الأفطس أنف فيه تقعير. وكذلك العدد في حد الزوج والخط في حد الاستقامة فيقال الزوج عدد منقسم بمتساويين. أو يؤخذ في حده جنس الموضوع كالناقص المحمول على الفرد فإنه يوجد في حده العدد الذي هو جنس للفرد فيقال الناقص هو العدد الذي إذا جمع ما فيه من الأجزاء يؤخذ في حده موضوع المعروض له أعني موضوع الموضوع كالجامع للبصر المحمول على الأسود من حيث هو أسود فإنه يؤخذ في حده الجسم الذي هو موضوع الأسود فيقال لجامع للبصر جسم موصوف بالسواد. أو يؤخذ في حده موضوع جنسه كزوج الزوج المحمول على عدد معين والزوج جنس لذلك العدد لما أنه ينقسم إليه وإلى غيره. والعدد موضوع للزوج فزوج الزوج يؤخذ في حده العدد فيقال زوج الزوج عدد زوج بعده عدد زوج بعدد زوج؛ غير أنه لا يصح إلا وأن يؤخذ في حده العدد. ولا يلزم أن يؤخذ والزوج يؤخذ
Chapter: On the essential predicates that are required in demonstration. If the premises are demonstrative, their predicates must be essential to their subjects. And that which is essential is said in several ways: [1] Among them is what is specific to predication and employment in this book. And that is of two kinds: one is the predicate that is taken in the definition of the subject, like 'animal' in the definition of 'man'—it is that which enters the quiddity of the subject as its genus, or genus of its genus, or differentia of its genus, and other things that are constitutive of the subject. The second is the predicate that is taken in the definition of the subject, like 'snub-nosed' in relation to 'nose'—for it occurs in its definition, so it is said: snub-nosed is a nose with concavity. And similarly, 'number' in the definition of 'even', and 'line' in the definition of 'straightness'; so it is said: the even is a number divisible into two equal halves. Or it is taken in the definition of the genus of the subject, like 'deficient' predicated of 'odd'—for it occurs in its definition of 'number', which is the genus of 'odd'; so it is said: the deficient is the number such that when its parts are summed... Or it is taken in the definition of the subject of the bearer, i.e., the subject of the subject, like 'collector of sight' predicated of 'black' insofar as it is black—for it is taken in the definition of 'body', which is the subject of 'black'; so it is said: the collector of sight is a body described with blackness. Or it is taken in the definition of the subject of its genus, like 'even of even' predicated of a specific number—'even' is the genus of that number since it divides into it and others. And 'number' is the subject of 'even'; so 'even of even' takes 'number' in its definition; so it is said: even of even is a number, an even after which is an even by an even number; however, it is only valid if 'number' is taken in its definition. And it is not necessary that 'even' be taken...
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the essential predicates (al-mahmulat al-dhatiyya) required in demonstrative premises. The author states that in demonstration, predicates must be essential to their subjects. He then distinguishes four main types: (1) The predicate is part of the subject's definition, like 'animal' for 'man'—animal enters the quiddity of man as a genus. (2) The predicate takes the subject in its own definition, like 'snub-nosed' which is defined as 'a nose with concavity'—the subject (nose) appears in the definition of the predicate. Similarly, 'even' is defined using 'number', and 'straightness' using 'line'. (3) The predicate takes the genus of the subject in its definition, like 'deficient' predicated of 'odd'—'deficient' is defined as a number whose proper parts sum to less than it, where 'number' is the genus of 'odd'. (4) The predicate takes the subject of the bearer (i.e., the subject of the subject) in its definition, like 'collector of sight' (light-gathering) predicated of 'black'—here 'black' is a quality of body, so the definition of 'collector of sight' includes 'body', the subject of black. (5) The predicate takes the subject of the genus of the subject in its definition, like 'even of even' predicated of a specific number—'even' is a genus of that number, and 'number' is the subject of 'even', so 'even of even' is defined using 'number'. The passage ends abruptly, but the classification is clear. The author's explanation shows how essential predicates can be related to the subject in multiple ways, all ensuring a real connection between subject and predicate in demonstrative knowledge.

The Two Pillars of Essential Predicates
Classification of essential predicates into two main types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two main types of essential predicates (predicate in subject's definition vs. subject in predicate's definition) using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
An essential predicate is either part of the subject's definition or the subject is part of its definition.

In demonstrative reasoning, not every true predicate qualifies. Only those predicates that share a definitional bond with the subject are 'essential' (dhati). This lesson introduces the two fundamental types of that bond, as explained by the author, and equips you to identify them in Islamic scientific discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitional criterion
The author states that for a predicate to be essential, either (a) the predicate is taken in the definition of the subject, or (b) the subject is taken in the definition of the predicate. This is the core criterion. Highlight the phrase 'المحمول الذي يؤخذ في حد الموضوع' and 'المحمول الذي يؤخذ في حده الموضوع' in the Arabic.
These two cases are exhaustive for essential predicates.
2
Recognize type one: predicate in subject's definition
Example from Arabic grammar: 'al-fa'il' (subject) is defined as 'al-ism al-marfu'' (the nominative noun). Here, 'marfu'' (nominative) is a predicate that enters the definition of 'fa'il'. This is the first type.
The predicate is part of the quiddity of the subject.
3
Recognize type two: subject in predicate's definition
Example from Arabic grammar: the predicate 'marfu'' (nominative) is defined as 'what is in the nominative state'. Its definition includes 'ism' (noun), which is the subject or genus of the subject. Thus 'ism' is taken in the definition of 'marfu''.
The subject may appear directly or indirectly (e.g., the genus of the subject).
4
Check the definitional link
Verify that the relationship is definitional, not merely accidental. For instance, 'the prayer is obligatory' is not an essential predicate because 'obligatory' is not part of the definition of 'prayer' (prayer is defined as specific acts, not as obligatory).
Essential predicates are necessary for demonstrative certainty.

فصل: في المحمولات الذاتية التي تشترط في البرهان. إذا كانت المقدمات برهانية يجب أن تكون محمولاتها ذاتية لموضوعاتها. والذي هو بذاته يقال على وجوه: منها ما يكون خاصا بالحمل والوضع في هذا الكتاب. وذلك على ضربين: أحدهما المحمول الذي يؤخذ في حد الموضوع كالحيوان في حد الإنسان وهو الداخل في ماهية الموضوع لجنسه و جنس جنسه و فصل جنسه وغير ذلك مما هو مقوم للموضوع. وثانيهما المحمول الذي يؤخذ في حده الموضوع كالأفطس بالنسبة إلى الأنف فإنه يوجد في حده الأنف فيقال الأفطس أنف فيه تقعير. وكذلك العدد في حد الزوج والخط في حد الاستقامة فيقال الزوج عدد منقسم بمتساويين. أو يؤخذ في حده جنس الموضوع كالناقص المحمول موضوع للزوج فزوج الزوج يؤخذ في حده العدد فيقال زوج الزوج عدد زوج بعده عدد زوج بعدد زوج؛ غير أنه لا يصح إلا وأن يؤخذ في حده العدد. ولا يلزم أن يؤخذ والزوج يؤخذ وهو عدد، فالمحمول الذي شأنه كذا وكذا إنما يسمى ذاتيا لأنه خاص؛ إما بموضوع الصناعة أو بشيء من موضوع الصناعة. فيتبع الموضوع أو ذلك الشيء فلا يكون دخيلا عليه غريباً عنه. وأما ما يؤخذ في حده جنس موضوع المسألة فإن كان ذلك الجنس أعم من موضوع الصناعة لم يستعمل في الصناعة على الوجه العام بل خصص بموضوع الصناعة حتى يدخل في حده موضوع الصناعة كالمناسبة في الهندسة بالنسبة إلى المقادير مناسبة مقدارية، وبالنسبة إلى العدد مناسبة عددية. وأما ما خرج من موضوع الصناعة فلا يعتد به من حيث هو خارج عنه. فأما إذا كان خارجا من موضوع المسألة لا من موضوع الصناعة ولا يؤخذ في حده موضوع الصناعة بل جنسه وموضوعه أو أمر أعم منه إلى أن يؤخذ في حده موضوع الصناعة في آخر الأمر. فذلك مما يدخل في البرهان مثل قولك هذا الخط مساو لذلك الخط؛ وهذا المضورب في نفسه زوج. فإن المحمول في كل واحد منهما أعم من الموضوع فاستحال أن يؤخذ في حده الموضوع لكن لما كان يؤخذ في حد كل واحد منهما موضوع الصناعة لما أن محمول القول الأول يعرف بأنه اتحاد في الكمية، ومحمول القول الثاني بأنه عدد منقسم بمتساويين كانا داخلين في البرهان. وإذا كان كذلك فالمقدمات البرهانية ليست هي التي توجد في كالسطح للمثلث، وإما مع المخصص كما إذا حمل المساوي على الخط فإنما يحمل عليه المساوي لخط آخر، أو حمل الواحد على العالم فإنما يحمل عليه الواحد في العالمية لا مطلقا.
Section: On the essential predicates that are required in demonstration. If the premises are demonstrative, their predicates must be essential to their subjects. That which is essential [bi-dhatihi] is said in several ways: among them is what is specific to predication and position in this book. That is of two types: one is the predicate that is taken in the definition of the subject, like 'animal' in the definition of 'human'—it is that which enters into the quiddity of the subject by way of its genus and the genus of its genus and the differentia of its genus, and other things that constitute the subject. The second type is the predicate in whose definition the subject is taken, like 'snub' in relation to 'nose', for 'snub' is found in its definition by saying 'snub is a nose having concavity'. Similarly, 'number' in the definition of 'even', and 'line' in the definition of 'straightness'; one says 'even is a number divisible into equal parts'. Or the genus of the subject is taken in its definition, such as 'deficient' predicated on 'odd'; for it is found in its definition that number, which is the genus of 'odd', appears—one says 'deficient is a number whose sum of its parts...' Or the subject of the attribute [al-ma'rud]—that is, the subject of the subject—is taken in its definition, like 'possessing sight' predicated on 'black' insofar as it is black; for in its definition 'body' which is the subject of blackness is taken—one says 'possessing sight is a body described with blackness'. Or the subject of its genus is taken in its definition, like 'even-even' predicated on a certain number, and 'even' is the genus of that number because it is divided into it and other things; number is the subject of 'even', so 'even-even' takes number in its definition—one says 'even-even is a number having an even number after it...' but it is not valid unless number is taken in its definition. It is not necessary that 'even' is taken while it is already a number. So the predicate that is such is called 'essential' because it is specific: either to the subject of the science or to something from the subject of the science. Thus it follows the subject or that thing, and is not extraneous to it. As for what takes in its definition the genus of the subject of the problem: if that genus is more general than the subject of the science, it is not used in the science in the absolute sense, but is specified by the subject of the science until the subject of the science enters its definition—like 'proportionality' in geometry with respect to magnitudes is proportionality of magnitude, and with respect to number is proportionality of number. As for what falls outside the subject of the science, it is not considered insofar as it is outside it. However, if it falls outside the subject of the problem but not the subject of the science, and the subject of the science is not taken in its definition, but rather its genus and subject or something more general until the subject of the science is eventually taken in its definition—then that is admitted in demonstration, such as your saying 'this line is equal to that line' and 'this number is even'. For the predicate in each of them is more general than the subject, so it is impossible that the subject is taken in its definition; but since the subject of the science is taken in the definition of each of them—because the predicate of the first statement is known as unity in quantity, and the predicate of the second statement as a number divisible into equal parts—they are admitted in demonstration. If that is so, then demonstrative premises are not those in whose predicates' definitions the subjects themselves are found, or those subjects are found in the predicates' definitions; rather, they are those in which the predicate is found in the definition of the subject, or the subject is found, or that which constitutes the subject. So what is within that science in the definition of the predicate is either absolute, like 'surface' for 'triangle', or with specification, as when 'equal' is predicated of a line, it is only predicated as equal to another line, or when 'one' is predicated of the universe, it is only predicated as one in universality, not absolutely.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines essential predicates (al-mahmulat al-dhatiyya) and their role in demonstrative premises (al-muqaddimat al-burhaniyya). The author begins by stating that for premises to be demonstrative, their predicates must be essential to their subjects. 'Essential' here is not simply 'always true' but refers to a specific logical relation: the predicate either enters into the definition of the subject, or the subject enters into the definition of the predicate. The author then enumerates two main types: First, the predicate taken in the definition of the subject (e.g., 'animal' in the definition of 'human'); second, the predicate in whose definition the subject is taken. The second type has several sub-types: (a) the subject itself is taken in the predicate's definition (e.g., 'snub' defined with 'nose'); (b) the genus of the subject is taken (e.g., 'deficient' defined with 'number', which is genus of 'odd'); (c) the subject of the attribute (i.e., the subject of the subject) is taken (e.g., 'possessing sight' defined with 'body', the subject of 'black'); (d) the subject of the genus of the subject is taken (e.g., 'even-even' defined with 'number'). In all these, the predicate is called 'essential' because it follows the subject without being extraneous. The passage then addresses how this applies when the genus of the subject of a problem is more general than the subject of the science: it must be specified (e.g., 'proportionality' is specified as magnitude-proportionality in geometry). Moreover, sometimes the predicate is more general than the subject, yet the subject of the science is included in its definition, which allows the premise to be demonstrative (e.g., 'this line is equal to that line' – equality is defined via quantity, which is subject of geometry). Finally, the author summarizes: demonstrative premises are those where the predicate is found in the definition of the subject (or subject's constituents), or the subject is found in the definition of the predicate, with the condition that the definitional relation is within the science.

Key Takeaway
An essential predicate is defined by a definitional link: either it belongs to the subject's definition or the subject belongs to its definition.

Essential Predicates in Demonstration: Sub-types and Application
Enumeration of sub-types and conditions for demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sub-types of the second kind of essential predicate (subject in predicate's definition) and evaluate whether a predicate is admissible in a demonstrative science according to the conditions given.

Core Idea
Demonstrative premises require not just any definitional link, but one that ultimately involves the subject matter of the science.

Beyond the two basic types, the author details several sub-varieties of essential predicates where the subject appears in the predicate's definition indirectly—via genus, subject of attribute, etc. This lesson unpacks those sub-types and explains how they meet the requirement for demonstration, even when the predicate is more general than the subject.

Guided Path
1
Review the second type and its sub-types
The second type is 'the predicate in whose definition the subject is taken.' The author then enumerates four sub-types: (a) the subject itself, (b) the genus of the subject, (c) the subject of the attribute (i.e., the subject of the subject), and (d) the subject of the genus. Locate examples of each in the Arabic text.
All these still count as the subject being taken in the predicate's definition, albeit indirectly.
2
Analyze an example from Arabic grammar
Consider the predicate 'marfu'' (nominative). Its definition includes 'ism' (noun), which is the genus of the specific subject 'fa'il'. This is an instance of sub-type (b): genus of the subject taken in the predicate's definition. Check if the definition is complete: 'marfu'' means 'a noun in the nominative state'.
The genus 'ism' is part of the genus of the subject, not the subject itself.
3
Understand the role of the subject of the science
The author emphasizes that for a predicate to be usable in demonstration, the subject of the science (e.g., 'quantity' in geometry) must eventually appear in the predicate's definition, either directly or through specification. For example, 'equal' in geometry is defined via quantity, so equality statements are demonstrative.
This ensures the predicate is not extraneous to the science.
4
Determine admissibility of a predicate
For a given statement in an Islamic science, check: Does the predicate's definition include the subject of the science? If so, it is admissible. Example: In geometry, 'triangle' defines 'surface'? Actually, 'surface' is in the definition of 'triangle'? No, the passage says 'surface' is absolute. Better: In grammar, 'ism' is the subject of the science, so predicates like 'marfu'' are admissible because their definition includes 'ism'.
If the subject of the science is not involved, the predicate is not essential for demonstration in that science.

واحد منهما موضوع الصناعة لما أن محمول القول الأول يعرف بأنه اتحاد في الكمية، ومحمول القول الثاني بأنه عدد منقسم بمتساويين كانا داخلين في البرهان. وإذا كان كذلك فالمقدمات البرهانية ليست هي التي توجد في Section: On the essential predicates that are required in demonstration. If the premises are demonstrative, their predicates must be essential to their subjects. That which is essential [bi-dhatihi] is said in several ways: among them is what is specific to predication and position in this book. That is of two types: one is the predicate that is taken in the definition of the subject, like 'animal' in the definition of 'human'—it is that which enters into the quiddity of the subject by way of its genus and the genus of its genus and the differentia of its genus, and other things that constitute the subject. The second type is the predicate in whose definition the subject is taken, like 'snub' in relation to 'nose', for 'snub' is found in its definition by saying 'snub is a nose having concavity'. Similarly, 'number' in the definition of 'even', and 'line' in the definition of 'straightness'; one says 'even is a number divisible into equal parts'. Or the genus of the subject is taken in its definition, such as 'deficient' predicated on 'odd'; for it is found in its definition that number, which is the genus of 'odd', appears—one says 'deficient is a number whose sum of its parts...' Or the subject of the attribute [al-ma'rud]—that is, the subject of the subject—is taken in its definition, like 'possessing sight' predicated on 'black' insofar as it is black; for in its definition 'body' which is the subject of blackness is taken—one says 'possessing sight is a body described with blackness'. Or the subject of its genus is taken in its definition, like 'even-even' predicated on a certain number, and 'even' is the genus of that number because it is divided into it and other things; number is the subject of 'even', so 'even-even' takes number in its definition—one says 'even-even is a number having an even number after it...' but it is not valid unless number is taken in its definition. It is not necessary that 'even' is taken while it is already a number. So the predicate that is such is called 'essential' because it is specific: either to the subject of the science or to something from the subject of the science. Thus it follows the subject or that thing, and is not extraneous to it. As for what takes in its definition the genus of the subject of the problem: if that genus is more general than the subject of the science, it is not used in the science in the absolute sense, but is specified by the subject of the science until the subject of the science enters its definition—like 'proportionality' in geometry with respect to magnitudes is proportionality of magnitude, and with respect to number is proportionality of number. As for what falls outside the subject of the science, it is not considered insofar as it is outside it. However, if it falls outside the subject of the problem but not the subject of the science, and the subject of the science is not taken in its definition, but rather its genus and subject or something more general until the subject of the science is eventually taken in its definition—then that is admitted in demonstration, such as your saying 'this line is equal to that line' and 'this number is even'. For the predicate in each of them is more general than the subject, so it is impossible that the subject is taken in its definition; but since the subject of the science is taken in the definition of each of them—because the predicate of the first statement is known as unity in quantity, and the predicate of the second statement as a number divisible into equal parts—they are admitted in demonstration. If that is so, then demonstrative premises are not those in whose predicates' definitions the subjects themselves are found, or those subjects are found in the predicates' definitions; rather, they are those in which the predicate is found in the definition of the subject, or the subject is found, or that which constitutes the subject. So what is within that science in the definition of the predicate is either absolute, like 'surface' for 'triangle', or with specification, as when 'equal' is predicated of a line, it is only predicated as equal to another line, or when 'one' is predicated of the universe, it is only predicated as one in universality, not absolutely.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines essential predicates (al-mahmulat al-dhatiyya) and their role in demonstrative premises (al-muqaddimat al-burhaniyya). The author begins by stating that for premises to be demonstrative, their predicates must be essential to their subjects. 'Essential' here is not simply 'always true' but refers to a specific logical relation: the predicate either enters into the definition of the subject, or the subject enters into the definition of the predicate. The author then enumerates two main types: First, the predicate taken in the definition of the subject (e.g., 'animal' in the definition of 'human'); second, the predicate in whose definition the subject is taken. The second type has several sub-types: (a) the subject itself is taken in the predicate's definition (e.g., 'snub' defined with 'nose'); (b) the genus of the subject is taken (e.g., 'deficient' defined with 'number', which is genus of 'odd'); (c) the subject of the attribute (i.e., the subject of the subject) is taken (e.g., 'possessing sight' defined with 'body', the subject of 'black'); (d) the subject of the genus of the subject is taken (e.g., 'even-even' defined with 'number'). In all these, the predicate is called 'essential' because it follows the subject without being extraneous. The passage then addresses how this applies when the genus of the subject of a problem is more general than the subject of the science: it must be specified (e.g., 'proportionality' is specified as magnitude-proportionality in geometry). Moreover, sometimes the predicate is more general than the subject, yet the subject of the science is included in its definition, which allows the premise to be demonstrative (e.g., 'this line is equal to that line' – equality is defined via quantity, which is subject of geometry). Finally, the author summarizes: demonstrative premises are those where the predicate is found in the definition of the subject (or subject's constituents), or the subject is found in the definition of the predicate, with the condition that the definitional relation is within the science.

البرهان
الموضوع
الصناعة

Essential predicates usable in demonstration require their definition to ultimately involve the subject matter of the science, whether directly or through specification.

Foreign Accidents and Subject-Specificity in Demonstrative Science
Author explains why foreign accidents are not studied in demonstrative sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential from foreign accidents and explain why a demonstrative science must restrict its investigation to the essential accidents of its subject matter.

Core Idea
A science is defined by its subject; foreign accidents would dissolve it into metaphysics.

When studying a science like arithmetic, you examine what belongs to number as such. But what about properties like 'blackness' that happen to characterize crows? The logician argues that such foreign accidents cannot be the primary objects of a demonstrative science. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows why restricting inquiry to essential accidents is crucial for maintaining distinct sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction: essential vs. foreign accidents
The passage defines a foreign accident as one that is not essential, meaning the thing's essence does not depend on it. For example, blackness for a crow is foreign because the crow's essence remains even if it were not black.
Essential accidents are those that necessarily follow from the subject's definition.
2
Understand the two scenarios for foreign accidents
Foreign accidents can be taken in two ways: (1) specified to the subject of the art (e.g., by being appropriate to it or its genus) – then their foreignness is removed; (2) taken absolutely, meaning they are not restricted to that subject's essence.
3
See the consequence of taking foreign accidents absolutely
If a foreign accident is taken absolutely, its existence is not limited to the subject of that art, but exists in other things as well. Thus, investigating it would not be specific to that art, and the science would lose its particularity.
The passage warns that this would make every science enter into every other science, reducing all to one universal science of absolute existence.
4
Apply to arithmetic and geometry
Arithmetic is a particular science because it studies number per se; geometry studies magnitude per se. If both studied quantity in general, their subjects would be the same (quantity), and they would merge into one science. Similarly, if they studied existence per se, they would become metaphysics (first philosophy).
This illustrates that each demonstrative science must have a distinct subject matter characterized by its essential accidents.
5
Conclude for demonstrative sciences
The author concludes that foreign accidents are not investigated at all in demonstrative sciences, except incidentally if they happen to co-occur. The primary objects are always the essential accidents of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كان الشيء لا يخلو عنه ولكن يوخذ لغيره من أشياء غريبة كالسواد للغراب، فإنه ليس بذاتي؛ إذ لا يتعلق به ذات الشيء، ولا ذات ما يقومه / ولا ذات الشيء الذي يتقوم به. إذا عرفت هذا فلك أن تعرف أن الأعراض الغريبة لا تجعل مطلوبات في مسائل الصنائع البرهانية لأنها إن أخذت مخصّصة بموضوع الصناعة وذلك إنما يمكن إذا كانت مناسبة له أو لجنسه أو لما هو كالجنس زالت غرابتها. وإن أخذت مطلقة فلا يكون وجودها لموضوع الصناعة من حيث هو موضوع الصناعة بل يوجد في غيره، فلا يكون النظر فيها من جنس النظر المخصوص بالصناعة لأن العلم إنما يكون جزئياً إذا كان بحث فيه عما يعرض لموضوعه من جهة ما هو هو . فإن لم يفعل ذلك لم يكن العلم الجزئي جزئيا بل كليا؛ و دخل كل علم في كل علم ، وصار النظر ليس في موضوع مخصوص بل في الموجود المطلق: وكانت العلوم كلها علمًا واحدًا فلا تكون العلوم متباينة. فإن علم الحساب والهندسة إنما يكون كل واحد منهما علمًا على حدة إذا كان النظر فيه فيما يعرض لما فرض موضوعا له وهو العدد من جهة ما هو عدد للحساب، والمقدار من جهة ما هو مقدار للهندسة فإنه إذا نظر في موضوعهما من جهة ما هو كم كان موضوع كل واحد منهما الكم لا العدد ولا المقدار؛ فكان العلمان علمًا واحدًا. وكذلك إذا نظر كل واحد منهما في موضوعه من حيث هو موجود كان له أيضًا أن ينظر فيما يعرض للموجود من حيث هو موجود؛ فكان الحساب مرة يفارق الهندسة ولا يفارق كل واحد منهما الفلسفة الأولى. فظهر أن الأعراض الغريبة لا ينظر فيها أصلا في علم من العلوم البرهانية وإن اتفق إنتاج شيء منها في علم ما . فإن ذلك يكون بيانًا على سبيل العرض
As for when a thing is not free from it but it is taken for other than it from among foreign things, like blackness for the crow, then it is not essential; for the essence of the thing does not depend on it, nor the essence of what constitutes it, nor the essence of the thing by which it is constituted. If you know this, then you should know that foreign accidents are not made objects of inquiry in the questions of the demonstrative arts because if they are taken as particular to the subject of the art—and that can only be if they are appropriate to it, its genus, or what is like a genus—then their foreignness is removed. And if they are taken absolutely, then their existence is not for the subject of the art insofar as it is the subject of the art, but exists in other things, so the investigation of them is not of the kind specific to that art because a science is only particular if it investigates what occurs to its subject insofar as it is that subject. If it does not do this, then the particular science is not particular but universal; and every science enters into every science, and the investigation becomes not in a specific subject but in absolute existence; and all the sciences become one science, so sciences are not distinct. For the science of arithmetic and geometry are each a separate science only if the investigation is in what occurs to what is assumed as their subject—number insofar as it is number for arithmetic, and magnitude insofar as it is magnitude for geometry. For if they investigate their subjects insofar as they are quantity, then the subject of each is quantity, not number or magnitude; so the two sciences become one science. Likewise, if each investigates its subject insofar as it is existent, then it also has the ability to investigate what occurs to the existent insofar as it is existent; then arithmetic would sometimes differ from geometry, but each would not differ from first philosophy [metaphysics]. So it appears that foreign accidents are not investigated at all in any of the demonstrative sciences, even if a production of something from them happens to occur in some science; for that would be a demonstration incidentally.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage explains why foreign accidents are excluded from demonstrative sciences. The author first recapitulates that an accident like blackness for a crow is not essential because the crow's essence does not depend on it. Then he argues that foreign accidents are not sought as objects of inquiry in demonstrative arts for two reasons: either they are specified to the subject of the art (by being appropriate to it, its genus, or something analogous), which removes their foreignness; or they are taken absolutely, in which case they do not belong specifically to that art's subject but exist in other things as well. A science is particular only if it studies what belongs to its subject per se. If it studies foreign accidents, it becomes universal and loses its distinctiveness, potentially merging all sciences into one. The author gives the example of arithmetic and geometry: they are separate sciences because arithmetic studies number per se and geometry studies magnitude per se. If both studied quantity in general, they would be the same science. If they studied existence per se, they would become metaphysics (first philosophy). Thus, demonstrative sciences must restrict themselves to essential accidents of their own subject matter; foreign accidents are only studied incidentally, not as primary objects of inquiry.

الصناعة البرهانية
العلم الجزئي
الموجود المطلق

Key Takeaway
A demonstrative science must restrict its investigation to the essential accidents of its own subject matter; otherwise it loses its identity and merges into a more universal science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the science of tafsir (Quranic exegesis): its subject is the Quranic text insofar as it is revelation. Identify a foreign accident (e.g., the type of script or the age of the paper) and explain, using the passage's reasoning, why it should not be a primary object of tafsir.

Essential Attributes and the Scope of Demonstrative Sciences
Author argues that accidental attributes cannot be used as objects of inquiry in demonstrative sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an attribute in a given demonstrative science problem is essential (dhati) or alien accidental (arad gharib), and explain why alien accidents cannot serve as objects of inquiry in demonstrative sciences.

Core Idea
A genuine demonstrative science investigates only attributes that belong to its subject essentially; alien accidents yield only accidental demonstration.

This passage from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa' defines the boundary of a genuine demonstrative science. It distinguishes essential attributes that define a subject from alien accidents that come from outside. Understanding this distinction is crucial for ensuring that each science remains distinct and that its demonstrations yield certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'essential' (dhati)
Find the condition in the passage: something that never fails to accompany a thing and is taken only for that thing or its genus, not for something else.
2
Apply the definition to the example of blackness and the raven
Blackness is an alien accident because it is taken primarily as an attribute of coal, not essentially of the raven.
The passage uses this example to illustrate arad gharib.
3
Explain the principle that each demonstrative science must study only attributes of its subject as such
The text says a science is particular only if it investigates what accidentally occurs to its subject insofar as it is that subject. Otherwise sciences become one.
The examples of arithmetic and geometry show this.
4
Analyse the consequences of using alien accidents in a syllogism
If the middle term is alien, the demonstration is per se from another art; if the major term is alien, the predication is not essential and a middle is missing, leading to accidental or dialectical demonstration.
This yields no certainty.
5
Apply the principle to an Islamic science example
Consider the science of Fiqh: its subject is the actions of legally responsible persons. An attribute like 'health' (a medical concern) is alien accidental to Fiqh, so it cannot serve as a direct object of inquiry in Fiqh.
This shows the practical relevance of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذن كل ما لا يخلو عنه الشيء بوجه من هذه الوجوه؛ وليس لغير الموضوع أو جنسه فهو ذاتي. وأما إذا كان الشيء لا يخلو عنه ولكن يوخذ لغيره من أشياء غريبة كالسواد للغراب، فإنه ليس بذاتي؛ إذ لا يتعلق به ذات الشيء، ولا ذات ما يقومه ولا ذات الشيء الذي يتقوم به. إذا عرفت هذا فلك أن تعرف أن الأعراض الغريبة لا تجعل مطلوبات في مسائل الصنائع البرهانية لأنها إن أخذت مخصّصة بموضوع الصناعة وذلك إنما يمكن إذا كانت مناسبة له أو لجنسه أو لما هو كالجنس زالت غرابتها. وإن أخذت مطلقة فلا يكون وجودها لموضوع الصناعة من حيث هو موضوع الصناعة بل يوجد في غيره، فلا يكون النظر فيها من جنس النظر المخصوص بالصناعة لأن العلم إنما يكون جزئياً إذا كان بحث فيه عما يعرض لموضوعه من جهة ما هو هو. فإن لم يفعل ذلك لم يكن العلم الجزئي جزئيا بل كليا؛ ودخل كل علم في كل علم، وصار النظر ليس في موضوع مخصوص بل في الموجود المطلق: وكانت العلوم كلها علمًا واحدًا فلا تكون العلوم متباينة. فإن علم الحساب والهندسة إنما يكون كل واحد منهما علمًا على حدة إذا كان النظر فيه فيما يعرض لما فرض موضوعا له وهو العدد من جهة ما هو عدد للحساب، والمقدار من جهة ما هو مقدار للهندسة فإنه إذا نظر في موضوعهما من جهة ما هو كم كان موضوع كل واحد منهما الكم لا العدد ولا المقدار؛ فكان العلمان علمًا واحدًا. وكذلك إذا نظر كل واحد منهما في موضوعه من حيث هو موجود كان له أيضًا أن ينظر فيما يعرض للموجود من حيث هو موجود؛ فكان الحساب مرة يفارق الهندسة ولا يفارق كل واحد منهما الفلسفة الأولى. فظهر أن الأعراض الغريبة لا ينظر فيها أصلا في علم من العلوم البرهانية وإن اتفق إنتاج شيء منها في علم ما. فإن ذلك يكون بيانًا على سبيل العرض وإن كان من مقدمات صادقة لأن الأوسط في مثل هذا القياس إن كان غريباً كان مناسبا لموضوع آخر فيكون البرهان بالذات من صناعة أخرى، وبالعرض من هذه الصناعة؛ وإن كان الأكبر غريباً لا يكون حمله على الأوسط لأنه هو، ولا لأجل جنسه فيكون من حق الأوسط أن يكون بينه وبين الأكبر وسط آخر قد تُرك: وأخذت النتيجة لا على وجهها الذي تبين به لأنه لم تؤخذ في بيانها مقدمة بينة بنفسها، ولا مقدمة هي مبدأ العلم وأصل موضوع فلا يحصل من ذلك اليقين مطلقا ولا لازم عن أصل موضوع فلا يكون البيان حقيقيا بل بالعرض؛ وذلك خارج عما نحن فيه بل يكون جدليًا أو مغالطيّا، هذا ما أشار إليه الشيخ في الشفاء.
So, everything from which a thing does not lack in any of these respects, and is not for other than the subject or its genus, is essential (dhati). But if a thing does not lack it, but it is taken for something else from alien things, like blackness for the raven, then it is not essential, for the essence of the thing is not attached to it, nor the essence of what constitutes it, nor the essence of the thing by which it is constituted. If you have understood this, then you may know that alien accidents (a'rad ghariba) are not made into what is sought (matlubat) in the problems of the demonstrative arts, because if they are taken as specific to the subject of the art, that is only possible if they are suitable to it or to its genus or to what is like its genus, then their alienness is removed. And if they are taken absolutely, then their existence for the subject of the art is not insofar as it is the subject of the art, but they exist in something else, so the consideration of them is not of the same kind as the consideration specific to the art. For a science is particular only if it investigates what accidentally occurs to its subject insofar as it is it. If it does not do that, then the particular science is not particular but universal; and every science enters into every science, and the consideration is not about a specific subject but about absolute existence; and all sciences become one science, so sciences are not distinct. For the science of arithmetic and geometry each becomes a separate science only if the consideration in it is about what accidentally occurs to what is posited as its subject, namely number insofar as it is number for arithmetic, and magnitude insofar as it is magnitude for geometry. For if one considers their subjects insofar as they are quantity, then the subject of each is quantity, not number and magnitude; so the two sciences become one science. Likewise, if each of them considers its subject insofar as it is existence, then it also has to consider what accidentally occurs to existence insofar as it is existence; then arithmetic would at one time differ from geometry and at another not differ from it, and each of them would not differ from first philosophy. Thus it is clear that alien accidents are not considered at all in any of the demonstrative sciences, even if something of them happens to be produced in some science. For that would be a demonstration accidentally, even if it is from true premises, because the middle term in such a syllogism, if it is alien, is suitable to another subject, so the demonstration per se is from another art, and accidentally from this art. And if the major term is alien, it is not predicated of the middle term because it is it, nor because of its genus; then it is incumbent upon the middle term that between it and the major term there be another middle term that has been omitted; and the conclusion is taken not in the manner in which it was demonstrated, because in its demonstration no premise was taken that is self-evident, nor a premise that is the principle of the science and the origin of the subject. So no certainty is obtained at all, nor a necessary [consequence] from the origin of the subject. So the demonstration is not real but accidental; that is outside what we are in, but rather it is dialectical or sophistical. This is what the Shaykh indicated in al-Shifa'.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage begins by defining what is essential (dhati): something that never fails to accompany a thing and is considered only for that thing or its genus, not for something else. An accidental attribute (arad gharib) like blackness for a raven is not essential because it does not constitute the essence. The author then applies this to demonstrative sciences (sana'i' burhaniyya): alien accidents cannot be the subject of inquiry (matlubat) in a particular science. If they are taken specifically relative to the subject, their alienness disappears; if taken absolutely, they apply to more than one subject, so the science loses its specificity. Using the examples of arithmetic and geometry, the author shows that each science must consider attributes arising from its own subject as such—number for arithmetic, magnitude for geometry. If considered under a broader category like quantity or existence, the sciences become one. Consequently, alien accidents are not investigated in any demonstrative science. If they accidentally appear in a syllogism, the demonstration is not genuine but accidental, dialectical, or sophistical, because the middle or major term does not relate essentially to the subject, resulting in a lack of certainty. The author attributes this to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh).

Key Terms
عرض غريب
ذاتي
برهان
وسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why cannot alien accidents (a'rad ghariba) be used as objects of inquiry in demonstrative sciences?
A) Because they are not self-evident
B) Because they do not relate essentially to the subject of that science, leading to accidental demonstration
C) Because they are always false
D) Because they are too specific

Key Takeaway
Alien accidents belong to a different subject; using them in a demonstrative science leads to accidental, dialectical, or sophistical demonstration, not genuine certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the science of Tafsir. Its subject is the Qur'anic text. Determine whether the attribute 'revealed in Arabic' is essential or alien accidental to this subject, and explain why it is appropriate for a demonstrative inquiry in Tafsir.
?
In the science of Fiqh, can 'causes physical harm' (a medical attribute) be a proper object of inquiry? Use the criteria from the passage to justify your answer.

Refuting the Objection on Self-Evident Premises
Refutation of the objection that premises need not be self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and refute the objection that premises in demonstration need not be self-evident.

Core Idea
Demonstration ultimately rests on self-evident premises; non-self-evident premises require prior demonstration.

Some critics argued that demonstrative premises do not need to be self-evident, because otherwise every demonstration would require infinite regress. The author clarifies that genuine demonstration is composed of primary, self-evident premises (awwaliyat), and any premise that is itself demonstrated is not primary, but belongs to a different chain. Understanding this distinction safeguards the foundation of certainty in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the claim that premises do not have to be self-evident (bayyina bi-nafsiha) because they can themselves be demonstrated. Note that this objection threatens the stability of demonstrative sciences.
The objector says this would cause demonstrations to cease being demonstrations.
2
Understand the author’s response
The author distinguishes between the initial premises of a demonstration (which must be self-evident) and premises that are themselves proven through other demonstrations. He argues that true demonstrative syllogisms (aqyisa burhaniyya) are composed of primary premises (awwaliyat) that are necessarily self-evident.
Demonstrated premises are not primary, so they do not serve as foundational premises.
3
Assess the refutation’s effectiveness
Consider whether the objection truly invalidates demonstration. The author shows that the objector confuses the level of a demonstration: the final chain must rest on self-evident premises, even if intermediate steps are demonstrated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال: «إنه من جملة ما فيه من الكلام، فإنه لا يجب أن تكون المقدّمة المستعملة بينة بنفسها أو أصلا موضوعًا حتى يوجب اليقين بل يجوز أن تكون المقدّمة المستعملة مبرهنا عليها بأنه إذا أوجب ذلك فقد خرجت البراهين عن أن تكون براهين؛ وذلك باطل فإن أكثر البراهين على المطالب الكلية كذلك غير أنه لا يكونوا ردًا عليه. فإن المقدمة إذا لم تكن بينة بنفسها فلا تكون من مقدمات برهانية في الابتداء، والأقيسة البرهانية في الحقيقة هي التي يكون تأليفها من الأوَّليات فتكون المقدّمات أولية؛ وإنها بينة بنفسها لا محالة. والمبرهن عليها لا يكون من الأوليات فلا يكون من تلك المقدمات. ومنهم من زعم أن السبب في أن لا يستعمل في البرهان وسط من الأعراض الغريبة وهو أن الأوسط حينئذ لا يكون علة للأكبر فلا يكون البرهان برهان لم؛ وليس الأمر كما زعم إلا وأن يكون من اللوازم أن يكون البرهان برهان لم. ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك؛ إذ النظر كله ليس في برهان لِمَ بل في البرهان المطلق الواقع على ما يعطي اليقين. ولو كان كذلك فيكون ذلك العرض غير علة لا يخرج
And among them is one who said, 'It is part of what is [said] in his speech: the premise used does not have to be self-evident or an origin (subject) in order to necessitate certainty; rather, it is permissible for the premise used to be itself demonstrated, because if it necessitated that, then demonstrations would cease to be demonstrations.' And that is false, for most demonstrations on universal claims are like that, except that they are not a refutation against him. For if the premise is not self-evident, then it is not from demonstrative premises in the beginning; and true demonstrative syllogisms are those whose composition is from primary [premises] (awwaliyat), so the premises are primary; and they are necessarily self-evident. And what is demonstrated is not from the primary premises, so it is not from those premises. And among them is one who claimed that the reason why a middle term from alien accidents should not be used in demonstration is that the middle term then is not a cause of the major term, so the demonstration is not a demonstration of 'why' (burhan limma). But it is not as he claimed, unless it is from the necessary consequences that the demonstration be a 'why' demonstration. And it is not necessary to be like that; for the consideration is not entirely about the 'why' demonstration, but about absolute demonstration that yields certainty. And if it were like that, then that accident would not be a cause, it does not exclude...
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two objections regarding the rule that alien accidents cannot be used in demonstrative sciences. The first objection claims that premises need not be self-evident; they can themselves be demonstrated, otherwise demonstrations would regress. The author responds that while many demonstrations use non-self-evident premises, ultimately they must be composed of primary (awwali) self-evident premises; otherwise they are not genuinely demonstrative from the start. Demonstrated premises are not primary, so they belong to a different category. The second objection asserts that using an accidental middle term prevents the demonstration from being a burhan limma (demonstration of the 'why'), because the middle term is not a cause. The author replies that the requirement for a 'why' demonstration is not absolute; demonstration in general only requires certainty, not necessarily causal explanation. Thus, the objection rests on an unnecessary condition. The final sentence is cut off, but the refutation is clear.

Key Terms
أوليات
مقدمة بينة بنفسها
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of a premise that is itself demonstrated?
A) It is a self-evident premise.
B) It is a primary premise (awwali).
C) It is not a primary premise and cannot be part of the foundational set.
D) It invalidates the demonstration.

Key Takeaway
Genuine demonstration is built upon self-evident primary premises; non-self-evident premises require prior justification and are not foundational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a mutakallim's argument: 'The world is temporally originated because it changes; everything that changes is temporally originated.' Is the premise 'everything that changes is temporally originated' self-evident or demonstrated? How would the author classify it?
?
Identify a contemporary objection to logical foundations and refute it using the author's distinction between primary and derivative premises.

Refuting the Objection on Causal Middle Term
Refutation of the objection that middle term must be a cause for demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that the middle term in demonstration must always be a cause if the demonstration is a burhan limma.

Core Idea
Demonstration only requires certainty, not necessarily causal explanation.

Another objection claimed that using an accidental middle term prevents demonstration from being a burhan limma (demonstration of the 'why'), because the middle term is not a cause. The author replies that absolute demonstration (al-burhan al-mutlaq) does not require the middle term to be a cause; it only requires certainty. This refutes the objection by showing it imposes an unnecessary condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the claim that using a middle term from alien accidents (a'rad ghariba) prevents the demonstration from being a burhan limma, because the middle term is not a cause of the major term.
The objector assumes that all valid demonstration must be causal.
2
Understand the author’s distinction
The author distinguishes between burhan limma (demonstration of 'why') and absolute demonstration (al-burhan al-mutlaq) which only yields certainty. He argues that the requirement for a causal middle term applies only to the former, not the latter.
The phrase 'النظر كله ليس في برهان لِمَ بل في البرهان المطلق' clarifies this.
3
Evaluate the refutation
Consider whether the objection is valid. The author shows that the objector incorrectly imposes the condition of burhan limma on all demonstrations. The proper condition is certainty, not causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is not necessary to be like that; for the consideration is not entirely about the 'why' demonstration, but about absolute demonstration that yields certainty. And if it were like that, then that accident would not be a cause, it does not exclude...

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the objection that the middle term must be a cause invalid?
A) Because the middle term is always a cause.
B) Because absolute demonstration does not require a causal middle term; it only requires certainty.
C) Because accidents cannot be middle terms.
D) Because burhan limma is the only valid demonstration.

Key Takeaway
A demonstration does not need to be causal; absolute demonstration is valid as long as it yields certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
